The earliest PG edition was prepared by Les\r\nBowler\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nTHE PRINCE AND THE PAUPER\r\n\r\nby Mark Twain\r\n\r\nThe Great Seal\r\n\r\nI will set down a tale as it was told to me by one who had it of his\r\nfather, which latter had it of HIS father, this last having in like\r\nmanner had it of HIS father--and so on, back and still back, three\r\nhundred years and more, the fathers transmitting it to the sons and so\r\npreserving it. \xc2\xa0It may be history, it may be only a legend, a tradition.\r\nIt may have happened, it may not have happened: \xc2\xa0but it COULD have\r\nhappened. \xc2\xa0It may be that the wise and the learned believed it in the\r\nold days; it may be that only the unlearned and the simple loved it and\r\ncredited it.\r\n\r\nCONTENTS\r\n\r\n    I. The birth of the Prince and the Pauper.\r\n\r\nIn the ancient city of London, on a certain autumn day in the second\r\nquarter of the sixteenth century, a boy was born to a poor family of the\r\nname of Canty, who did not want him. \xc2\xa0On the same day another English\r\nchild was born to a rich family of the name of Tudor, who did want him.\r\nAll England wanted him too. \xc2\xa0England had so longed for him, and hoped\r\nfor him, and prayed God for him, that, now that he was really come, the\r\npeople went nearly mad for joy. \xc2\xa0Mere acquaintances hugged and kissed\r\neach other and cried. Everybody took a holiday, and high and low, rich\r\nand poor, feasted and danced and sang, and got very mellow; and they\r\nkept this up for days and nights together. \xc2\xa0By day, London was a sight\r\nto see, with gay banners waving from every balcony and housetop, and\r\nsplendid pageants marching along. \xc2\xa0By night, it was again a sight\r\nto see, with its great bonfires at every corner, and its troops of\r\nrevellers making merry around them. \xc2\xa0There was no talk in all England\r\nbut of the new baby, Edward Tudor, Prince of Wales, who lay lapped in\r\nsilks and satins, unconscious of all this fuss, and not knowing that\r\ngreat lords and ladies were tending him and watching over him--and not\r\ncaring, either. \xc2\xa0But there was no talk about the other baby, Tom Canty,\r\nlapped in his poor rags, except among the family of paupers whom he had\r\njust come to trouble with his presence.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER II. Tom\xe2\x80\x99s early life.\r\n\r\nLet us skip a number of years.\r\n\r\nLondon was fifteen hundred years old, and was a great town--for that\r\nday. It had a hundred thousand inhabitants--some think double as many.\r\n\xc2\xa0The streets were very narrow, and crooked, and dirty, especially in the\r\npart where Tom Canty lived, which was not far from London Bridge. \xc2\xa0The\r\nhouses were of wood, with the second story projecting over the first,\r\nand the third sticking its elbows out beyond the second. \xc2\xa0The higher\r\nthe houses grew, the broader they grew. \xc2\xa0They were skeletons of strong\r\ncriss-cross beams, with solid material between, coated with plaster.\r\n\xc2\xa0The beams were painted red or blue or black, according to the owner\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntaste, and this gave the houses a very picturesque look. \xc2\xa0The windows\r\nwere small, glazed with little diamond-shaped panes, and they opened\r\noutward, on hinges, like doors.\r\n\r\nThe house which Tom\xe2\x80\x99s father lived in was up a foul little pocket called\r\nOffal Court, out of Pudding Lane. \xc2\xa0It was small, decayed, and rickety,\r\nbut it was packed full of wretchedly poor families. Canty\xe2\x80\x99s tribe\r\noccupied a room on the third floor. \xc2\xa0The mother and father had a sort of\r\nbedstead in the corner; but Tom, his grandmother, and his two sisters,\r\nBet and Nan, were not restricted--they had all the floor to themselves,\r\nand might sleep where they chose. \xc2\xa0There were the remains of a blanket\r\nor two, and some bundles of ancient and dirty straw, but these could not\r\nrightly be called beds, for they were not organised; they were kicked\r\ninto a general pile, mornings, and selections made from the mass at\r\nnight, for service.\r\n\r\nBet and Nan were fifteen years old--twins. \xc2\xa0They were good-hearted\r\ngirls, unclean, clothed in rags, and profoundly ignorant. \xc2\xa0Their mother\r\nwas like them. \xc2\xa0But the father and the grandmother were a couple of\r\nfiends. \xc2\xa0They got drunk whenever they could; then they fought each other\r\nor anybody else who came in the way; they cursed and swore always, drunk\r\nor sober; John Canty was a thief, and his mother a beggar. \xc2\xa0They made\r\nbeggars of the children, but failed to make thieves of them. \xc2\xa0Among,\r\nbut not of, the dreadful rabble that inhabited the house, was a good old\r\npriest whom the King had turned out of house and home with a pension of\r\na few farthings, and he used to get the children aside and teach them\r\nright ways secretly. Father Andrew also taught Tom a little Latin, and\r\nhow to read and write; and would have done the same with the girls,\r\nbut they were afraid of the jeers of their friends, who could not have\r\nendured such a queer accomplishment in them.\r\n\r\nAll Offal Court was just such another hive as Canty\xe2\x80\x99s house.\r\nDrunkenness, riot and brawling were the order, there, every night and\r\nnearly all night long. \xc2\xa0Broken heads were as common as hunger in that\r\nplace. \xc2\xa0Yet little Tom was not unhappy. \xc2\xa0He had a hard time of it, but\r\ndid not know it. \xc2\xa0It was the sort of time that all the Offal Court boys\r\nhad, therefore he supposed it was the correct and comfortable thing.\r\n\xc2\xa0When he came home empty-handed at night, he knew his father would\r\ncurse him and thrash him first, and that when he was done the awful\r\ngrandmother would do it all over again and improve on it; and that away\r\nin the night his starving mother would slip to him stealthily with any\r\nmiserable scrap or crust she had been able to save for him by going\r\nhungry herself, notwithstanding she was often caught in that sort of\r\ntreason and soundly beaten for it by her husband.\r\n\r\nNo, Tom\xe2\x80\x99s life went along well enough, especially in summer. \xc2\xa0He only\r\nbegged just enough to save himself, for the laws against mendicancy were\r\nstringent, and the penalties heavy; so he put in a good deal of his time\r\nlistening to good Father Andrew\xe2\x80\x99s charming old tales and legends\r\nabout giants and fairies, dwarfs and genii, and enchanted castles, and\r\ngorgeous kings and princes. \xc2\xa0His head grew to be full of these wonderful\r\nthings, and many a night as he lay in the dark on his scant and\r\noffensive straw, tired, hungry, and smarting from a thrashing, he\r\nunleashed his imagination and soon forgot his aches and pains in\r\ndelicious picturings to himself of the charmed life of a petted prince\r\nin a regal palace. \xc2\xa0One desire came in time to haunt him day and night:\r\n\xc2\xa0it was to see a real prince, with his own eyes. \xc2\xa0He spoke of it once to\r\nsome of his Offal Court comrades; but they jeered him and scoffed him so\r\nunmercifully that he was glad to keep his dream to himself after that.\r\n\r\nHe often read the priest\xe2\x80\x99s old books and got him to explain and enlarge\r\nupon them. \xc2\xa0His dreamings and readings worked certain changes in him,\r\nby-and-by. \xc2\xa0His dream-people were so fine that he grew to lament his\r\nshabby clothing and his dirt, and to wish to be clean and better clad.\r\n\xc2\xa0He went on playing in the mud just the same, and enjoying it, too; but,\r\ninstead of splashing around in the Thames solely for the fun of it,\r\nhe began to find an added value in it because of the washings and\r\ncleansings it afforded.\r\n\r\nTom could always find something going on around the Maypole in\r\nCheapside, and at the fairs; and now and then he and the rest of London\r\nhad a chance to see a military parade when some famous unfortunate was\r\ncarried prisoner to the Tower, by land or boat. One summer\xe2\x80\x99s day he saw\r\npoor Anne Askew and three men burned at the stake in Smithfield, and\r\nheard an ex-Bishop preach a sermon to them which did not interest him.\r\nYes, Tom\xe2\x80\x99s life was varied and pleasant enough, on the whole.\r\n\r\nBy-and-by Tom\xe2\x80\x99s reading and dreaming about princely life wrought such a\r\nstrong effect upon him that he began to _act_ the prince, unconsciously.\r\nHis speech and manners became curiously ceremonious and courtly, to the\r\nvast admiration and amusement of his intimates. \xc2\xa0But Tom\xe2\x80\x99s influence\r\namong these young people began to grow now, day by day; and in time he\r\ncame to be looked up to, by them, with a sort of wondering awe, as a\r\nsuperior being. \xc2\xa0He seemed to know so much! and he could do and say such\r\nmarvellous things! and withal, he was so deep and wise! \xc2\xa0Tom\xe2\x80\x99s remarks,\r\nand Tom\xe2\x80\x99s performances, were reported by the boys to their elders; and\r\nthese, also, presently began to discuss Tom Canty, and to regard him\r\nas a most gifted and extraordinary creature. \xc2\xa0Full-grown people brought\r\ntheir perplexities to Tom for solution, and were often astonished at the\r\nwit and wisdom of his decisions. \xc2\xa0In fact he was become a hero to all\r\nwho knew him except his own family--these, only, saw nothing in him.\r\n\r\nPrivately, after a while, Tom organised a royal court! \xc2\xa0He was the\r\nprince; his special comrades were guards, chamberlains, equerries, lords\r\nand ladies in waiting, and the royal family. \xc2\xa0Daily the mock prince was\r\nreceived with elaborate ceremonials borrowed by Tom from his romantic\r\nreadings; daily the great affairs of the mimic kingdom were discussed\r\nin the royal council, and daily his mimic highness issued decrees to his\r\nimaginary armies, navies, and viceroyalties.\r\n\r\nAfter which, he would go forth in his rags and beg a few farthings, eat\r\nhis poor crust, take his customary cuffs and abuse, and then stretch\r\nhimself upon his handful of foul straw, and resume his empty grandeurs\r\nin his dreams.\r\n\r\nAnd still his desire to look just once upon a real prince, in the flesh,\r\ngrew upon him, day by day, and week by week, until at last it absorbed\r\nall other desires, and became the one passion of his life.\r\n\r\nOne January day, on his usual begging tour, he tramped despondently up\r\nand down the region round about Mincing Lane and Little East Cheap, hour\r\nafter hour, bare-footed and cold, looking in at cook-shop windows and\r\nlonging for the dreadful pork-pies and other deadly inventions displayed\r\nthere--for to him these were dainties fit for the angels; that is,\r\njudging by the smell, they were--for it had never been his good luck to\r\nown and eat one. There was a cold drizzle of rain; the atmosphere was\r\nmurky; it was a melancholy day. \xc2\xa0At night Tom reached home so wet and\r\ntired and hungry that it was not possible for his father and grandmother\r\nto observe his forlorn condition and not be moved--after their fashion;\r\nwherefore they gave him a brisk cuffing at once and sent him to bed.\r\n\xc2\xa0For a long time his pain and hunger, and the swearing and fighting\r\ngoing on in the building, kept him awake; but at last his thoughts\r\ndrifted away to far, romantic lands, and he fell asleep in the company\r\nof jewelled and gilded princelings who live in vast palaces, and had\r\nservants salaaming before them or flying to execute their orders. \xc2\xa0And\r\nthen, as usual, he dreamed that _he_ was a princeling himself.\r\n\r\nAll night long the glories of his royal estate shone upon him; he moved\r\namong great lords and ladies, in a blaze of light, breathing perfumes,\r\ndrinking in delicious music, and answering the reverent obeisances of\r\nthe glittering throng as it parted to make way for him, with here a\r\nsmile, and there a nod of his princely head.\r\n\r\nAnd when he awoke in the morning and looked upon the wretchedness\r\nabout him, his dream had had its usual effect--it had intensified the\r\nsordidness of his surroundings a thousandfold. \xc2\xa0Then came bitterness,\r\nand heart-break, and tears.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER III. Tom\xe2\x80\x99s meeting with the Prince.\r\n\r\nTom got up hungry, and sauntered hungry away, but with his thoughts busy\r\nwith the shadowy splendours of his night\xe2\x80\x99s dreams. He wandered here\r\nand there in the city, hardly noticing where he was going, or what\r\nwas happening around him. \xc2\xa0People jostled him, and some gave him rough\r\nspeech; but it was all lost on the musing boy. \xc2\xa0By-and-by he found\r\nhimself at Temple Bar, the farthest from home he had ever travelled in\r\nthat direction. \xc2\xa0He stopped and considered a moment, then fell into his\r\nimaginings again, and passed on outside the walls of London. \xc2\xa0The Strand\r\nhad ceased to be a country-road then, and regarded itself as a street,\r\nbut by a strained construction; for, though there was a tolerably\r\ncompact row of houses on one side of it, there were only some scattered\r\ngreat buildings on the other, these being palaces of rich nobles, with\r\nample and beautiful grounds stretching to the river--grounds that are\r\nnow closely packed with grim acres of brick and stone.\r\n\r\nTom discovered Charing Village presently, and rested himself at the\r\nbeautiful cross built there by a bereaved king of earlier days; then\r\nidled down a quiet, lovely road, past the great cardinal\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nstately palace, toward a far more mighty and majestic palace\r\nbeyond--Westminster. Tom stared in glad wonder at the vast pile of\r\nmasonry, the wide-spreading wings, the frowning bastions and turrets,\r\nthe huge stone gateway, with its gilded bars and its magnificent array\r\nof colossal granite lions, and other the signs and symbols of English\r\nroyalty. \xc2\xa0Was the desire of his soul to be satisfied at last? \xc2\xa0Here,\r\nindeed, was a king\xe2\x80\x99s palace. \xc2\xa0Might he not hope to see a prince now--a\r\nprince of flesh and blood, if Heaven were willing?\r\n\r\nAt each side of the gilded gate stood a living statue--that is to say,\r\nan erect and stately and motionless man-at-arms, clad from head to heel\r\nin shining steel armour. \xc2\xa0At a respectful distance were many country\r\nfolk, and people from the city, waiting for any chance glimpse of\r\nroyalty that might offer. \xc2\xa0Splendid carriages, with splendid people\r\nin them and splendid servants outside, were arriving and departing by\r\nseveral other noble gateways that pierced the royal enclosure.\r\n\r\nPoor little Tom, in his rags, approached, and was moving slowly and\r\ntimidly past the sentinels, with a beating heart and a rising hope, when\r\nall at once he caught sight through the golden bars of a spectacle that\r\nalmost made him shout for joy. \xc2\xa0Within was a comely boy, tanned and\r\nbrown with sturdy outdoor sports and exercises, whose clothing was all\r\nof lovely silks and satins, shining with jewels; at his hip a little\r\njewelled sword and dagger; dainty buskins on his feet, with red heels;\r\nand on his head a jaunty crimson cap, with drooping plumes fastened\r\nwith a great sparkling gem. \xc2\xa0Several gorgeous gentlemen stood near--his\r\nservants, without a doubt. \xc2\xa0Oh! he was a prince--a prince, a living\r\nprince, a real prince--without the shadow of a question; and the prayer\r\nof the pauper-boy\xe2\x80\x99s heart was answered at last.\r\n\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s breath came quick and short with excitement, and his eyes grew big\r\nwith wonder and delight. \xc2\xa0Everything gave way in his mind instantly\r\nto one desire: \xc2\xa0that was to get close to the prince, and have a good,\r\ndevouring look at him. \xc2\xa0Before he knew what he was about, he had his\r\nface against the gate-bars. \xc2\xa0The next instant one of the soldiers\r\nsnatched him rudely away, and sent him spinning among the gaping crowd\r\nof country gawks and London idlers. \xc2\xa0The soldier said,--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMind thy manners, thou young beggar!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe crowd jeered and laughed; but the young prince sprang to the gate\r\nwith his face flushed, and his eyes flashing with indignation, and cried\r\nout,--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow dar\xe2\x80\x99st thou use a poor lad like that? \xc2\xa0How dar\xe2\x80\x99st thou use the King\r\nmy father\xe2\x80\x99s meanest subject so? \xc2\xa0Open the gates, and let him in!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nYou should have seen that fickle crowd snatch off their hats then.\r\nYou should have heard them cheer, and shout, \xe2\x80\x9cLong live the Prince of\r\nWales!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe soldiers presented arms with their halberds, opened the gates,\r\nand presented again as the little Prince of Poverty passed in, in his\r\nfluttering rags, to join hands with the Prince of Limitless Plenty.\r\n\r\nEdward Tudor said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou lookest tired and hungry: \xc2\xa0thou\xe2\x80\x99st been treated ill. \xc2\xa0Come with\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHalf a dozen attendants sprang forward to--I don\xe2\x80\x99t know what; interfere,\r\nno doubt. \xc2\xa0But they were waved aside with a right royal gesture, and\r\nthey stopped stock still where they were, like so many statues. \xc2\xa0Edward\r\ntook Tom to a rich apartment in the palace, which he called his cabinet.\r\n\xc2\xa0By his command a repast was brought such as Tom had never encountered\r\nbefore except in books. \xc2\xa0The prince, with princely delicacy and\r\nbreeding, sent away the servants, so that his humble guest might not be\r\nembarrassed by their critical presence; then he sat near by, and asked\r\nquestions while Tom ate.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is thy name, lad?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTom Canty, an\xe2\x80\x99 it please thee, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Tis an odd one. \xc2\xa0Where dost live?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the city, please thee, sir. \xc2\xa0Offal Court, out of Pudding Lane.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOffal Court! \xc2\xa0Truly \xe2\x80\x99tis another odd one. \xc2\xa0Hast parents?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cParents have I, sir, and a grand-dam likewise that is but indifferently\r\nprecious to me, God forgive me if it be offence to say it--also twin\r\nsisters, Nan and Bet.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen is thy grand-dam not over kind to thee, I take it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNeither to any other is she, so please your worship. \xc2\xa0She hath a wicked\r\nheart, and worketh evil all her days.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoth she mistreat thee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere be times that she stayeth her hand, being asleep or overcome with\r\ndrink; but when she hath her judgment clear again, she maketh it up to\r\nme with goodly beatings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA fierce look came into the little prince\xe2\x80\x99s eyes, and he cried out--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! \xc2\xa0Beatings?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, indeed, yes, please you, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Beatings_!--and thou so frail and little. \xc2\xa0Hark ye: \xc2\xa0before the night\r\ncome, she shall hie her to the Tower. \xc2\xa0The King my father\xe2\x80\x9d--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn sooth, you forget, sir, her low degree. \xc2\xa0The Tower is for the great\r\nalone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue, indeed. \xc2\xa0I had not thought of that. \xc2\xa0I will consider of her\r\npunishment. \xc2\xa0Is thy father kind to thee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot more than Gammer Canty, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFathers be alike, mayhap. \xc2\xa0Mine hath not a doll\xe2\x80\x99s temper. \xc2\xa0He smiteth\r\nwith a heavy hand, yet spareth me: \xc2\xa0he spareth me not always with his\r\ntongue, though, sooth to say. \xc2\xa0How doth thy mother use thee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe is good, sir, and giveth me neither sorrow nor pain of any sort.\r\nAnd Nan and Bet are like to her in this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow old be these?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFifteen, an\xe2\x80\x99 it please you, sir.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Lady Elizabeth, my sister, is fourteen, and the Lady Jane Grey,\r\nmy cousin, is of mine own age, and comely and gracious withal; but\r\nmy sister the Lady Mary, with her gloomy mien and--Look you: \xc2\xa0do thy\r\nsisters forbid their servants to smile, lest the sin destroy their\r\nsouls?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey? \xc2\xa0Oh, dost think, sir, that _they_ have servants?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe little prince contemplated the little pauper gravely a moment, then\r\nsaid--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd prithee, why not? \xc2\xa0Who helpeth them undress at night? \xc2\xa0Who attireth\r\nthem when they rise?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone, sir. \xc2\xa0Would\xe2\x80\x99st have them take off their garment, and sleep\r\nwithout--like the beasts?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTheir garment! \xc2\xa0Have they but one?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, good your worship, what would they do with more? \xc2\xa0Truly they have\r\nnot two bodies each.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a quaint and marvellous thought! \xc2\xa0Thy pardon, I had not meant\r\nto laugh. \xc2\xa0But thy good Nan and thy Bet shall have raiment and lackeys\r\nenow, and that soon, too: \xc2\xa0my cofferer shall look to it. \xc2\xa0No, thank me\r\nnot; \xe2\x80\x99tis nothing. \xc2\xa0Thou speakest well; thou hast an easy grace in it.\r\n\xc2\xa0Art learned?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know not if I am or not, sir. \xc2\xa0The good priest that is called Father\r\nAndrew taught me, of his kindness, from his books.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKnow\xe2\x80\x99st thou the Latin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut scantly, sir, I doubt.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLearn it, lad:  \xe2\x80\x99tis hard only at first. \xc2\xa0The Greek is harder; but\r\nneither these nor any tongues else, I think, are hard to the Lady\r\nElizabeth and my cousin. \xc2\xa0Thou should\xe2\x80\x99st hear those damsels at it! \xc2\xa0But\r\ntell me of thy Offal Court. \xc2\xa0Hast thou a pleasant life there?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn truth, yes, so please you, sir, save when one is hungry. There\r\nbe Punch-and-Judy shows, and monkeys--oh such antic creatures! and so\r\nbravely dressed!--and there be plays wherein they that play do shout\r\nand fight till all are slain, and \xe2\x80\x99tis so fine to see, and costeth but\r\na farthing--albeit \xe2\x80\x99tis main hard to get the farthing, please your\r\nworship.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTell me more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe lads of Offal Court do strive against each other with the cudgel,\r\nlike to the fashion of the \xe2\x80\x98prentices, sometimes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe prince\xe2\x80\x99s eyes flashed. \xc2\xa0Said he--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarry, that would not I mislike. \xc2\xa0Tell me more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe strive in races, sir, to see who of us shall be fleetest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat would I like also. \xc2\xa0Speak on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn summer, sir, we wade and swim in the canals and in the river, and\r\neach doth duck his neighbour, and splatter him with water, and dive and\r\nshout and tumble and--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Twould be worth my father\xe2\x80\x99s kingdom but to enjoy it once! Prithee go\r\non.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe dance and sing about the Maypole in Cheapside; we play in the sand,\r\neach covering his neighbour up; and times we make mud pastry--oh\r\nthe lovely mud, it hath not its like for delightfulness in all the\r\nworld!--we do fairly wallow in the mud, sir, saving your worship\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npresence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, prithee, say no more, \xe2\x80\x99tis glorious! \xc2\xa0If that I could but clothe me\r\nin raiment like to thine, and strip my feet, and revel in the mud once,\r\njust once, with none to rebuke me or forbid, meseemeth I could forego\r\nthe crown!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd if that I could clothe me once, sweet sir, as thou art clad--just\r\nonce--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOho, would\xe2\x80\x99st like it? \xc2\xa0Then so shall it be. \xc2\xa0Doff thy rags, and don\r\nthese splendours, lad! \xc2\xa0It is a brief happiness, but will be not less\r\nkeen for that. \xc2\xa0We will have it while we may, and change again before\r\nany come to molest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA few minutes later the little Prince of Wales was garlanded with Tom\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfluttering odds and ends, and the little Prince of Pauperdom was tricked\r\nout in the gaudy plumage of royalty. \xc2\xa0The two went and stood side by\r\nside before a great mirror, and lo, a miracle: there did not seem to\r\nhave been any change made! \xc2\xa0They stared at each other, then at the\r\nglass, then at each other again. \xc2\xa0At last the puzzled princeling said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat dost thou make of this?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, good your worship, require me not to answer. \xc2\xa0It is not meet that\r\none of my degree should utter the thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen will _I_ utter it. \xc2\xa0Thou hast the same hair, the same eyes, the\r\nsame voice and manner, the same form and stature, the same face and\r\ncountenance that I bear. \xc2\xa0Fared we forth naked, there is none could\r\nsay which was you, and which the Prince of Wales. \xc2\xa0And, now that I\r\nam clothed as thou wert clothed, it seemeth I should be able the more\r\nnearly to feel as thou didst when the brute soldier--Hark ye, is not\r\nthis a bruise upon your hand?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes; but it is a slight thing, and your worship knoweth that the poor\r\nman-at-arms--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeace! \xc2\xa0It was a shameful thing and a cruel!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the little prince,\r\nstamping his bare foot.  \xe2\x80\x9cIf the King--Stir not a step till I come\r\nagain! It is a command!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn a moment he had snatched up and put away an article of national\r\nimportance that lay upon a table, and was out at the door and flying\r\nthrough the palace grounds in his bannered rags, with a hot face and\r\nglowing eyes. \xc2\xa0As soon as he reached the great gate, he seized the bars,\r\nand tried to shake them, shouting--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOpen! \xc2\xa0Unbar the gates!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe soldier that had maltreated Tom obeyed promptly; and as the prince\r\nburst through the portal, half-smothered with royal wrath, the soldier\r\nfetched him a sounding box on the ear that sent him whirling to the\r\nroadway, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake that, thou beggar\xe2\x80\x99s spawn, for what thou got\xe2\x80\x99st me from his\r\nHighness!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe crowd roared with laughter. \xc2\xa0The prince picked himself out of the\r\nmud, and made fiercely at the sentry, shouting--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am the Prince of Wales, my person is sacred; and thou shalt hang for\r\nlaying thy hand upon me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe soldier brought his halberd to a present-arms and said mockingly--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI salute your gracious Highness.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0Then angrily--\xe2\x80\x9cBe off, thou crazy\r\nrubbish!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHere the jeering crowd closed round the poor little prince, and hustled\r\nhim far down the road, hooting him, and shouting--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWay for his Royal Highness! \xc2\xa0Way for the Prince of Wales!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IV. The Prince\xe2\x80\x99s troubles begin.\r\n\r\nAfter hours of persistent pursuit and persecution, the little prince was\r\nat last deserted by the rabble and left to himself. \xc2\xa0As long as he had\r\nbeen able to rage against the mob, and threaten it royally, and\r\nroyally utter commands that were good stuff to laugh at, he was very\r\nentertaining; but when weariness finally forced him to be silent, he was\r\nno longer of use to his tormentors, and they sought amusement elsewhere.\r\nHe looked about him, now, but could not recognise the locality. \xc2\xa0He\r\nwas within the city of London--that was all he knew. \xc2\xa0He moved on,\r\naimlessly, and in a little while the houses thinned, and the passers-by\r\nwere infrequent. \xc2\xa0He bathed his bleeding feet in the brook which flowed\r\nthen where Farringdon Street now is; rested a few moments, then passed\r\non, and presently came upon a great space with only a few scattered\r\nhouses in it, and a prodigious church. \xc2\xa0He recognised this church.\r\n\xc2\xa0Scaffoldings were about, everywhere, and swarms of workmen; for it was\r\nundergoing elaborate repairs. \xc2\xa0The prince took heart at once--he felt\r\nthat his troubles were at an end, now. \xc2\xa0He said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the\r\nancient Grey Friars\xe2\x80\x99 Church, which the king my father hath taken from\r\nthe monks and given for a home for ever for poor and forsaken children,\r\nand new-named it Christ\xe2\x80\x99s Church. \xc2\xa0Right gladly will they serve the son\r\nof him who hath done so generously by them--and the more that that son\r\nis himself as poor and as forlorn as any that be sheltered here this\r\nday, or ever shall be.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was soon in the midst of a crowd of boys who were running, jumping,\r\nplaying at ball and leap-frog, and otherwise disporting themselves, and\r\nright noisily, too. \xc2\xa0They were all dressed alike, and in the fashion\r\nwhich in that day prevailed among serving-men and \xe2\x80\x98prentices{1}--that\r\nis to say, each had on the crown of his head a flat black cap about the\r\nsize of a saucer, which was not useful as a covering, it being of such\r\nscanty dimensions, neither was it ornamental; from beneath it the hair\r\nfell, unparted, to the middle of the forehead, and was cropped straight\r\naround; a clerical band at the neck; a blue gown that fitted closely\r\nand hung as low as the knees or lower; full sleeves; a broad red belt;\r\nbright yellow stockings, gartered above the knees; low shoes with large\r\nmetal buckles. It was a sufficiently ugly costume.\r\n\r\nThe boys stopped their play and flocked about the prince, who said with\r\nnative dignity--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood lads, say to your master that Edward Prince of Wales desireth\r\nspeech with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA great shout went up at this, and one rude fellow said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarry, art thou his grace\xe2\x80\x99s messenger, beggar?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe prince\xe2\x80\x99s face flushed with anger, and his ready hand flew to his\r\nhip, but there was nothing there. \xc2\xa0There was a storm of laughter, and\r\none boy said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidst mark that? \xc2\xa0He fancied he had a sword--belike he is the prince\r\nhimself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis sally brought more laughter. \xc2\xa0Poor Edward drew himself up proudly\r\nand said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am the prince; and it ill beseemeth you that feed upon the king my\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s bounty to use me so.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was vastly enjoyed, as the laughter testified. \xc2\xa0The youth who had\r\nfirst spoken, shouted to his comrades--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHo, swine, slaves, pensioners of his grace\xe2\x80\x99s princely father, where be\r\nyour manners? \xc2\xa0Down on your marrow bones, all of ye, and do reverence to\r\nhis kingly port and royal rags!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith boisterous mirth they dropped upon their knees in a body and did\r\nmock homage to their prey. \xc2\xa0The prince spurned the nearest boy with his\r\nfoot, and said fiercely--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake thou that, till the morrow come and I build thee a gibbet!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAh, but this was not a joke--this was going beyond fun. \xc2\xa0The laughter\r\nceased on the instant, and fury took its place. \xc2\xa0A dozen shouted--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHale him forth! \xc2\xa0To the horse-pond, to the horse-pond! \xc2\xa0Where be the\r\ndogs? \xc2\xa0Ho, there, Lion! ho, Fangs!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen followed such a thing as England had never seen before--the sacred\r\nperson of the heir to the throne rudely buffeted by plebeian hands, and\r\nset upon and torn by dogs.\r\n\r\nAs night drew to a close that day, the prince found himself far down in\r\nthe close-built portion of the city. \xc2\xa0His body was bruised, his hands\r\nwere bleeding, and his rags were all besmirched with mud. \xc2\xa0He wandered\r\non and on, and grew more and more bewildered, and so tired and faint\r\nhe could hardly drag one foot after the other. \xc2\xa0He had ceased to ask\r\nquestions of anyone, since they brought him only insult instead of\r\ninformation. \xc2\xa0He kept muttering to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cOffal Court--that is the\r\nname; if I can but find it before my strength is wholly spent and I\r\ndrop, then am I saved--for his people will take me to the palace and\r\nprove that I am none of theirs, but the true prince, and I shall have\r\nmine own again.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0And now and then his mind reverted to his treatment\r\nby those rude Christ\xe2\x80\x99s Hospital boys, and he said, \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I am king, they\r\nshall not have bread and shelter only, but also teachings out of books;\r\nfor a full belly is little worth where the mind is starved, and the\r\nheart. \xc2\xa0I will keep this diligently in my remembrance, that this day\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlesson be not lost upon me, and my people suffer thereby; for learning\r\nsofteneth the heart and breedeth gentleness and charity.\xe2\x80\x9d {1}\r\n\r\nThe lights began to twinkle, it came on to rain, the wind rose, and a\r\nraw and gusty night set in. \xc2\xa0The houseless prince, the homeless heir to\r\nthe throne of England, still moved on, drifting deeper into the maze\r\nof squalid alleys where the swarming hives of poverty and misery were\r\nmassed together.\r\n\r\nSuddenly a great drunken ruffian collared him and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOut to this time of night again, and hast not brought a farthing home,\r\nI warrant me! \xc2\xa0If it be so, an\xe2\x80\x99 I do not break all the bones in thy lean\r\nbody, then am I not John Canty, but some other.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe prince twisted himself loose, unconsciously brushed his profaned\r\nshoulder, and eagerly said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, art _his_ father, truly? \xc2\xa0Sweet heaven grant it be so--then wilt\r\nthou fetch him away and restore me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_His_ father? \xc2\xa0I know not what thou mean\xe2\x80\x99st; I but know I am _thy_\r\nfather, as thou shalt soon have cause to--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, jest not, palter not, delay not!--I am worn, I am wounded, I can\r\nbear no more. \xc2\xa0Take me to the king my father, and he will make thee rich\r\nbeyond thy wildest dreams. \xc2\xa0Believe me, man, believe me!--I speak no\r\nlie, but only the truth!--put forth thy hand and save me! \xc2\xa0I am indeed\r\nthe Prince of Wales!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man stared down, stupefied, upon the lad, then shook his head and\r\nmuttered--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGone stark mad as any Tom o\xe2\x80\x99 Bedlam!\xe2\x80\x9d--then collared him once more,\r\nand said with a coarse laugh and an oath, \xe2\x80\x9cBut mad or no mad, I and thy\r\nGammer Canty will soon find where the soft places in thy bones lie, or\r\nI\xe2\x80\x99m no true man!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWith this he dragged the frantic and struggling prince away, and\r\ndisappeared up a front court followed by a delighted and noisy swarm of\r\nhuman vermin.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER V. Tom as a Patrician.\r\n\r\nTom Canty, left alone in the prince\xe2\x80\x99s cabinet, made good use of his\r\nopportunity. \xc2\xa0He turned himself this way and that before the great\r\nmirror, admiring his finery; then walked away, imitating the prince\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nhigh-bred carriage, and still observing results in the glass. \xc2\xa0Next he\r\ndrew the beautiful sword, and bowed, kissing the blade, and laying it\r\nacross his breast, as he had seen a noble knight do, by way of salute to\r\nthe lieutenant of the Tower, five or six weeks before, when delivering\r\nthe great lords of Norfolk and Surrey into his hands for captivity. \xc2\xa0Tom\r\nplayed with the jewelled dagger that hung upon his thigh; he examined\r\nthe costly and exquisite ornaments of the room; he tried each of the\r\nsumptuous chairs, and thought how proud he would be if the Offal Court\r\nherd could only peep in and see him in his grandeur. \xc2\xa0He wondered if\r\nthey would believe the marvellous tale he should tell when he got home,\r\nor if they would shake their heads, and say his overtaxed imagination\r\nhad at last upset his reason.\r\n\r\nAt the end of half an hour it suddenly occurred to him that the prince\r\nwas gone a long time; then right away he began to feel lonely; very\r\nsoon he fell to listening and longing, and ceased to toy with the\r\npretty things about him; he grew uneasy, then restless, then distressed.\r\nSuppose some one should come, and catch him in the prince\xe2\x80\x99s clothes, and\r\nthe prince not there to explain. \xc2\xa0Might they not hang him at once,\r\nand inquire into his case afterward? \xc2\xa0He had heard that the great\r\nwere prompt about small matters. \xc2\xa0His fear rose higher and higher; and\r\ntrembling he softly opened the door to the antechamber, resolved to\r\nfly and seek the prince, and, through him, protection and release. \xc2\xa0Six\r\ngorgeous gentlemen-servants and two young pages of high degree, clothed\r\nlike butterflies, sprang to their feet and bowed low before him. \xc2\xa0He\r\nstepped quickly back and shut the door. \xc2\xa0He said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, they mock at me! \xc2\xa0They will go and tell. \xc2\xa0Oh! why came I here to\r\ncast away my life?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe walked up and down the floor, filled with nameless fears, listening,\r\nstarting at every trifling sound. \xc2\xa0Presently the door swung open, and a\r\nsilken page said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Lady Jane Grey.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe door closed and a sweet young girl, richly clad, bounded toward him.\r\nBut she stopped suddenly, and said in a distressed voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, what aileth thee, my lord?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s breath was nearly failing him; but he made shift to stammer out--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, be merciful, thou! \xc2\xa0In sooth I am no lord, but only poor Tom Canty\r\nof Offal Court in the city. \xc2\xa0Prithee let me see the prince, and he will\r\nof his grace restore to me my rags, and let me hence unhurt. \xc2\xa0Oh, be\r\nthou merciful, and save me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy this time the boy was on his knees, and supplicating with his eyes\r\nand uplifted hands as well as with his tongue. \xc2\xa0The young girl seemed\r\nhorror-stricken. \xc2\xa0She cried out--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO my lord, on thy knees?--and to _me_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen she fled away in fright; and Tom, smitten with despair, sank down,\r\nmurmuring--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is no help, there is no hope. \xc2\xa0Now will they come and take me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhilst he lay there benumbed with terror, dreadful tidings were speeding\r\nthrough the palace. \xc2\xa0The whisper--for it was whispered always--flew from\r\nmenial to menial, from lord to lady, down all the long corridors, from\r\nstory to story, from saloon to saloon, \xe2\x80\x9cThe prince hath gone mad, the\r\nprince hath gone mad!\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0Soon every saloon, every marble hall, had its\r\ngroups of glittering lords and ladies, and other groups of dazzling\r\nlesser folk, talking earnestly together in whispers, and every face\r\nhad in it dismay. Presently a splendid official came marching by these\r\ngroups, making solemn proclamation--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIN THE NAME OF THE KING!\r\n\r\nLet none list to this false and foolish matter, upon pain of death, nor\r\ndiscuss the same, nor carry it abroad. \xc2\xa0In the name of the King!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe whisperings ceased as suddenly as if the whisperers had been\r\nstricken dumb.\r\n\r\nSoon there was a general buzz along the corridors, of \xe2\x80\x9cThe prince! See,\r\nthe prince comes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoor Tom came slowly walking past the low-bowing groups, trying to\r\nbow in return, and meekly gazing upon his strange surroundings with\r\nbewildered and pathetic eyes. \xc2\xa0Great nobles walked upon each side of\r\nhim, making him lean upon them, and so steady his steps. Behind him\r\nfollowed the court-physicians and some servants.\r\n\r\nPresently Tom found himself in a noble apartment of the palace and heard\r\nthe door close behind him. \xc2\xa0Around him stood those who had come with\r\nhim. Before him, at a little distance, reclined a very large and very\r\nfat man, with a wide, pulpy face, and a stern expression. \xc2\xa0His large\r\nhead was very grey; and his whiskers, which he wore only around his\r\nface, like a frame, were grey also. \xc2\xa0His clothing was of rich stuff,\r\nbut old, and slightly frayed in places. \xc2\xa0One of his swollen legs had a\r\npillow under it, and was wrapped in bandages. \xc2\xa0There was silence now;\r\nand there was no head there but was bent in reverence, except this\r\nman\xe2\x80\x99s. \xc2\xa0This stern-countenanced invalid was the dread Henry VIII. \xc2\xa0He\r\nsaid--and his face grew gentle as he began to speak--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow now, my lord Edward, my prince? \xc2\xa0Hast been minded to cozen me, the\r\ngood King thy father, who loveth thee, and kindly useth thee, with a\r\nsorry jest?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoor Tom was listening, as well as his dazed faculties would let him,\r\nto the beginning of this speech; but when the words \xe2\x80\x98me, the good King\xe2\x80\x99\r\nfell upon his ear, his face blanched, and he dropped as instantly upon\r\nhis knees as if a shot had brought him there. Lifting up his hands, he\r\nexclaimed--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou the _King_? \xc2\xa0Then am I undone indeed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis speech seemed to stun the King. \xc2\xa0His eyes wandered from face to\r\nface aimlessly, then rested, bewildered, upon the boy before him. \xc2\xa0Then\r\nhe said in a tone of deep disappointment--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlack, I had believed the rumour disproportioned to the truth; but I\r\nfear me \xe2\x80\x99tis not so.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0He breathed a heavy sigh, and said in a gentle\r\nvoice, \xe2\x80\x9cCome to thy father, child: \xc2\xa0thou art not well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom was assisted to his feet, and approached the Majesty of England,\r\nhumble and trembling. \xc2\xa0The King took the frightened face between his\r\nhands, and gazed earnestly and lovingly into it awhile, as if seeking\r\nsome grateful sign of returning reason there, then pressed the curly\r\nhead against his breast, and patted it tenderly. \xc2\xa0Presently he said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDost not know thy father, child? \xc2\xa0Break not mine old heart; say thou\r\nknow\xe2\x80\x99st me. \xc2\xa0Thou _dost_ know me, dost thou not?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYea: \xc2\xa0thou art my dread lord the King, whom God preserve!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue, true--that is well--be comforted, tremble not so; there is none\r\nhere would hurt thee; there is none here but loves thee. Thou art better\r\nnow; thy ill dream passeth--is\xe2\x80\x99t not so? \xc2\xa0Thou wilt not miscall thyself\r\nagain, as they say thou didst a little while agone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI pray thee of thy grace believe me, I did but speak the truth, most\r\ndread lord; for I am the meanest among thy subjects, being a pauper\r\nborn, and \xe2\x80\x99tis by a sore mischance and accident I am here, albeit I was\r\ntherein nothing blameful. \xc2\xa0I am but young to die, and thou canst save me\r\nwith one little word. \xc2\xa0Oh speak it, sir!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDie? \xc2\xa0Talk not so, sweet prince--peace, peace, to thy troubled\r\nheart--thou shalt not die!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom dropped upon his knees with a glad cry--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod requite thy mercy, O my King, and save thee long to bless thy\r\nland!\xe2\x80\x9d Then springing up, he turned a joyful face toward the two lords\r\nin waiting, and exclaimed, \xe2\x80\x9cThou heard\xe2\x80\x99st it! \xc2\xa0I am not to die: \xc2\xa0the\r\nKing hath said it!\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0There was no movement, save that all bowed with\r\ngrave respect; but no one spoke. \xc2\xa0He hesitated, a little confused, then\r\nturned timidly toward the King, saying, \xe2\x80\x9cI may go now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo? \xc2\xa0Surely, if thou desirest. \xc2\xa0But why not tarry yet a little? Whither\r\nwould\xe2\x80\x99st go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom dropped his eyes, and answered humbly--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeradventure I mistook; but I did think me free, and so was I moved\r\nto seek again the kennel where I was born and bred to misery, yet which\r\nharboureth my mother and my sisters, and so is home to me; whereas these\r\npomps and splendours whereunto I am not used--oh, please you, sir, to\r\nlet me go!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King was silent and thoughtful a while, and his face betrayed a\r\ngrowing distress and uneasiness. \xc2\xa0Presently he said, with something of\r\nhope in his voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPerchance he is but mad upon this one strain, and hath his wits\r\nunmarred as toucheth other matter. \xc2\xa0God send it may be so! \xc2\xa0We will make\r\ntrial.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he asked Tom a question in Latin, and Tom answered him lamely in\r\nthe same tongue. \xc2\xa0The lords and doctors manifested their gratification\r\nalso. The King said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Twas not according to his schooling and ability, but showeth that his\r\nmind is but diseased, not stricken fatally. \xc2\xa0How say you, sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe physician addressed bowed low, and replied--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt jumpeth with my own conviction, sire, that thou hast divined\r\naright.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King looked pleased with this encouragement, coming as it did from\r\nso excellent authority, and continued with good heart--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow mark ye all: \xc2\xa0we will try him further.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe put a question to Tom in French. \xc2\xa0Tom stood silent a moment,\r\nembarrassed by having so many eyes centred upon him, then said\r\ndiffidently--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have no knowledge of this tongue, so please your majesty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King fell back upon his couch. \xc2\xa0The attendants flew to his\r\nassistance; but he put them aside, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrouble me not--it is nothing but a scurvy faintness. \xc2\xa0Raise me! There,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98tis sufficient. \xc2\xa0Come hither, child; there, rest thy poor troubled head\r\nupon thy father\xe2\x80\x99s heart, and be at peace. \xc2\xa0Thou\xe2\x80\x99lt soon be well:  \xe2\x80\x99tis\r\nbut a passing fantasy. \xc2\xa0Fear thou not; thou\xe2\x80\x99lt soon be well.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0Then\r\nhe turned toward the company: \xc2\xa0his gentle manner changed, and baleful\r\nlightnings began to play from his eyes. \xc2\xa0He said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cList ye all! \xc2\xa0This my son is mad; but it is not permanent. \xc2\xa0Over-study\r\nhath done this, and somewhat too much of confinement. \xc2\xa0Away with his\r\nbooks and teachers! see ye to it. \xc2\xa0Pleasure him with sports, beguile him\r\nin wholesome ways, so that his health come again.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0He raised himself\r\nhigher still, and went on with energy, \xe2\x80\x9cHe is mad; but he is my son,\r\nand England\xe2\x80\x99s heir; and, mad or sane, still shall he reign! \xc2\xa0And hear ye\r\nfurther, and proclaim it: whoso speaketh of this his distemper worketh\r\nagainst the peace and order of these realms, and shall to the gallows!\r\n. . . Give me to drink--I burn: \xc2\xa0this sorrow sappeth my strength. . . .\r\nThere, take away the cup. . . . Support me. \xc2\xa0There, that is well. \xc2\xa0Mad,\r\nis he? \xc2\xa0Were he a thousand times mad, yet is he Prince of Wales, and I\r\nthe King will confirm it. \xc2\xa0This very morrow shall he be installed in his\r\nprincely dignity in due and ancient form. \xc2\xa0Take instant order for it, my\r\nlord Hertford.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOne of the nobles knelt at the royal couch, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe King\xe2\x80\x99s majesty knoweth that the Hereditary Great Marshal of England\r\nlieth attainted in the Tower. \xc2\xa0It were not meet that one attainted--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeace! \xc2\xa0Insult not mine ears with his hated name. \xc2\xa0Is this man to\r\nlive for ever? \xc2\xa0Am I to be baulked of my will? \xc2\xa0Is the prince to tarry\r\nuninstalled, because, forsooth, the realm lacketh an Earl Marshal\r\nfree of treasonable taint to invest him with his honours? No, by the\r\nsplendour of God! \xc2\xa0Warn my Parliament to bring me Norfolk\xe2\x80\x99s doom before\r\nthe sun rise again, else shall they answer for it grievously!\xe2\x80\x9d {1}\r\n\r\nLord Hertford said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe King\xe2\x80\x99s will is law;\xe2\x80\x9d and, rising, returned to his former place.\r\n\r\nGradually the wrath faded out of the old King\xe2\x80\x99s face, and he said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKiss me, my prince. \xc2\xa0There . . . what fearest thou? \xc2\xa0Am I not thy\r\nloving father?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art good to me that am unworthy, O mighty and gracious lord: that\r\nin truth I know. \xc2\xa0But--but--it grieveth me to think of him that is to\r\ndie, and--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, \xe2\x80\x99tis like thee, \xe2\x80\x99tis like thee! \xc2\xa0I know thy heart is still the\r\nsame, even though thy mind hath suffered hurt, for thou wert ever of a\r\ngentle spirit. \xc2\xa0But this duke standeth between thee and thine honours:\r\n\xc2\xa0I will have another in his stead that shall bring no taint to his great\r\noffice. Comfort thee, my prince: \xc2\xa0trouble not thy poor head with this\r\nmatter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut is it not I that speed him hence, my liege? \xc2\xa0How long might he not\r\nlive, but for me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake no thought of him, my prince: \xc2\xa0he is not worthy. \xc2\xa0Kiss me once\r\nagain, and go to thy trifles and amusements; for my malady distresseth\r\nme. \xc2\xa0I am aweary, and would rest. \xc2\xa0Go with thine uncle Hertford and thy\r\npeople, and come again when my body is refreshed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom, heavy-hearted, was conducted from the presence, for this last\r\nsentence was a death-blow to the hope he had cherished that now he would\r\nbe set free. \xc2\xa0Once more he heard the buzz of low voices exclaiming, \xe2\x80\x9cThe\r\nprince, the prince comes!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis spirits sank lower and lower as he moved between the glittering\r\nfiles of bowing courtiers; for he recognised that he was indeed a\r\ncaptive now, and might remain for ever shut up in this gilded cage, a\r\nforlorn and friendless prince, except God in his mercy take pity on him\r\nand set him free.\r\n\r\nAnd, turn where he would, he seemed to see floating in the air the\r\nsevered head and the remembered face of the great Duke of Norfolk, the\r\neyes fixed on him reproachfully.\r\n\r\nHis old dreams had been so pleasant; but this reality was so dreary!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VI. Tom receives instructions.\r\n\r\nTom was conducted to the principal apartment of a noble suite, and made\r\nto sit down--a thing which he was loth to do, since there were elderly\r\nmen and men of high degree about him. \xc2\xa0He begged them to be seated\r\nalso, but they only bowed their thanks or murmured them, and remained\r\nstanding. He would have insisted, but his \xe2\x80\x98uncle\xe2\x80\x99 the Earl of Hertford\r\nwhispered in his ear--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrithee, insist not, my lord; it is not meet that they sit in thy\r\npresence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Lord St. John was announced, and after making obeisance to Tom, he\r\nsaid--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI come upon the King\xe2\x80\x99s errand, concerning a matter which requireth\r\nprivacy. \xc2\xa0Will it please your royal highness to dismiss all that attend\r\nyou here, save my lord the Earl of Hertford?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nObserving that Tom did not seem to know how to proceed, Hertford\r\nwhispered him to make a sign with his hand, and not trouble himself to\r\nspeak unless he chose. \xc2\xa0When the waiting gentlemen had retired, Lord St.\r\nJohn said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis majesty commandeth, that for due and weighty reasons of state, the\r\nprince\xe2\x80\x99s grace shall hide his infirmity in all ways that be within his\r\npower, till it be passed and he be as he was before. \xc2\xa0To wit, that he\r\nshall deny to none that he is the true prince, and heir to England\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngreatness; that he shall uphold his princely dignity, and shall receive,\r\nwithout word or sign of protest, that reverence and observance which\r\nunto it do appertain of right and ancient usage; that he shall cease to\r\nspeak to any of that lowly birth and life his malady hath conjured\r\nout of the unwholesome imaginings of o\xe2\x80\x99er-wrought fancy; that he shall\r\nstrive with diligence to bring unto his memory again those faces which\r\nhe was wont to know--and where he faileth he shall hold his peace,\r\nneither betraying by semblance of surprise or other sign that he hath\r\nforgot; that upon occasions of state, whensoever any matter shall\r\nperplex him as to the thing he should do or the utterance he should\r\nmake, he shall show nought of unrest to the curious that look on, but\r\ntake advice in that matter of the Lord Hertford, or my humble self,\r\nwhich are commanded of the King to be upon this service and close at\r\ncall, till this commandment be dissolved. Thus saith the King\xe2\x80\x99s majesty,\r\nwho sendeth greeting to your royal highness, and prayeth that God will\r\nof His mercy quickly heal you and have you now and ever in His holy\r\nkeeping.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Lord St. John made reverence and stood aside. \xc2\xa0Tom replied\r\nresignedly--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe King hath said it. \xc2\xa0None may palter with the King\xe2\x80\x99s command, or fit\r\nit to his ease, where it doth chafe, with deft evasions. The King shall\r\nbe obeyed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLord Hertford said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTouching the King\xe2\x80\x99s majesty\xe2\x80\x99s ordainment concerning books and such like\r\nserious matters, it may peradventure please your highness to ease your\r\ntime with lightsome entertainment, lest you go wearied to the banquet\r\nand suffer harm thereby.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s face showed inquiring surprise; and a blush followed when he saw\r\nLord St. John\xe2\x80\x99s eyes bent sorrowfully upon him. \xc2\xa0His lordship said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThy memory still wrongeth thee, and thou hast shown surprise--but\r\nsuffer it not to trouble thee, for \xe2\x80\x99tis a matter that will not bide,\r\nbut depart with thy mending malady. \xc2\xa0My Lord of Hertford speaketh of\r\nthe city\xe2\x80\x99s banquet which the King\xe2\x80\x99s majesty did promise, some two months\r\nflown, your highness should attend. \xc2\xa0Thou recallest it now?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt grieves me to confess it had indeed escaped me,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom, in a\r\nhesitating voice; and blushed again.\r\n\r\nAt this moment the Lady Elizabeth and the Lady Jane Grey were announced.\r\nThe two lords exchanged significant glances, and Hertford stepped\r\nquickly toward the door. \xc2\xa0As the young girls passed him, he said in a\r\nlow voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI pray ye, ladies, seem not to observe his humours, nor show surprise\r\nwhen his memory doth lapse--it will grieve you to note how it doth stick\r\nat every trifle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMeantime Lord St. John was saying in Tom\xe2\x80\x99s ear--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease you, sir, keep diligently in mind his majesty\xe2\x80\x99s desire. Remember\r\nall thou canst--_seem_ to remember all else. \xc2\xa0Let them not perceive that\r\nthou art much changed from thy wont, for thou knowest how tenderly thy\r\nold play-fellows bear thee in their hearts and how \xe2\x80\x99twould grieve them.\r\nArt willing, sir, that I remain?--and thine uncle?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom signified assent with a gesture and a murmured word, for he was\r\nalready learning, and in his simple heart was resolved to acquit himself\r\nas best he might, according to the King\xe2\x80\x99s command.\r\n\r\nIn spite of every precaution, the conversation among the young people\r\nbecame a little embarrassing at times. \xc2\xa0More than once, in truth,\r\nTom was near to breaking down and confessing himself unequal to his\r\ntremendous part; but the tact of the Princess Elizabeth saved him, or a\r\nword from one or the other of the vigilant lords, thrown in apparently\r\nby chance, had the same happy effect. \xc2\xa0Once the little Lady Jane turned\r\nto Tom and dismayed him with this question,--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHast paid thy duty to the Queen\xe2\x80\x99s majesty to-day, my lord?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom hesitated, looked distressed, and was about to stammer out something\r\nat hazard, when Lord St. John took the word and answered for him\r\nwith the easy grace of a courtier accustomed to encounter delicate\r\ndifficulties and to be ready for them--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe hath indeed, madam, and she did greatly hearten him, as touching his\r\nmajesty\xe2\x80\x99s condition; is it not so, your highness?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom mumbled something that stood for assent, but felt that he was\r\ngetting upon dangerous ground. \xc2\xa0Somewhat later it was mentioned that\r\nTom was to study no more at present, whereupon her little ladyship\r\nexclaimed--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Tis a pity, \xe2\x80\x99tis a pity! \xc2\xa0Thou wert proceeding bravely. \xc2\xa0But bide thy\r\ntime in patience: \xc2\xa0it will not be for long. \xc2\xa0Thou\xe2\x80\x99lt yet be graced\r\nwith learning like thy father, and make thy tongue master of as many\r\nlanguages as his, good my prince.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy father!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Tom, off his guard for the moment.  \xe2\x80\x9cI trow he cannot\r\nspeak his own so that any but the swine that kennel in the styes may\r\ntell his meaning; and as for learning of any sort soever--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked up and encountered a solemn warning in my Lord St. John\xe2\x80\x99s\r\neyes.\r\n\r\nHe stopped, blushed, then continued low and sadly: \xe2\x80\x9cAh, my malady\r\npersecuteth me again, and my mind wandereth. \xc2\xa0I meant the King\xe2\x80\x99s grace\r\nno irreverence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe know it, sir,\xe2\x80\x9d said the Princess Elizabeth, taking her \xe2\x80\x98brother\xe2\x80\x99s\xe2\x80\x99\r\nhand between her two palms, respectfully but caressingly; \xe2\x80\x9ctrouble not\r\nthyself as to that. \xc2\xa0The fault is none of thine, but thy distemper\xe2\x80\x99s.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou\xe2\x80\x99rt a gentle comforter, sweet lady,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom, gratefully, \xe2\x80\x9cand my\r\nheart moveth me to thank thee for\xe2\x80\x99t, an\xe2\x80\x99 I may be so bold.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOnce the giddy little Lady Jane fired a simple Greek phrase at Tom.\r\n\xc2\xa0The Princess Elizabeth\xe2\x80\x99s quick eye saw by the serene blankness of the\r\ntarget\xe2\x80\x99s front that the shaft was overshot; so she tranquilly delivered\r\na return volley of sounding Greek on Tom\xe2\x80\x99s behalf, and then straightway\r\nchanged the talk to other matters.\r\n\r\nTime wore on pleasantly, and likewise smoothly, on the whole. Snags and\r\nsandbars grew less and less frequent, and Tom grew more and more at\r\nhis ease, seeing that all were so lovingly bent upon helping him and\r\noverlooking his mistakes. \xc2\xa0When it came out that the little ladies were\r\nto accompany him to the Lord Mayor\xe2\x80\x99s banquet in the evening, his heart\r\ngave a bound of relief and delight, for he felt that he should not be\r\nfriendless, now, among that multitude of strangers; whereas, an\r\nhour earlier, the idea of their going with him would have been an\r\ninsupportable terror to him.\r\n\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s guardian angels, the two lords, had had less comfort in the\r\ninterview than the other parties to it. \xc2\xa0They felt much as if they were\r\npiloting a great ship through a dangerous channel; they were on the\r\nalert constantly, and found their office no child\xe2\x80\x99s play. Wherefore,\r\nat last, when the ladies\xe2\x80\x99 visit was drawing to a close and the Lord\r\nGuilford Dudley was announced, they not only felt that their charge had\r\nbeen sufficiently taxed for the present, but also that they themselves\r\nwere not in the best condition to take their ship back and make their\r\nanxious voyage all over again. \xc2\xa0So they respectfully advised Tom to\r\nexcuse himself, which he was very glad to do, although a slight shade\r\nof disappointment might have been observed upon my Lady Jane\xe2\x80\x99s face when\r\nshe heard the splendid stripling denied admittance.\r\n\r\nThere was a pause now, a sort of waiting silence which Tom could not\r\nunderstand. \xc2\xa0He glanced at Lord Hertford, who gave him a sign--but he\r\nfailed to understand that also. \xc2\xa0The ready Elizabeth came to the rescue\r\nwith her usual easy grace. \xc2\xa0She made reverence and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave we leave of the prince\xe2\x80\x99s grace my brother to go?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed your ladyships can have whatsoever of me they will, for the\r\nasking; yet would I rather give them any other thing that in my poor\r\npower lieth, than leave to take the light and blessing of their presence\r\nhence. \xc2\xa0Give ye good den, and God be with ye!\xe2\x80\x9d Then he smiled inwardly\r\nat the thought, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Tis not for nought I have dwelt but among princes in\r\nmy reading, and taught my tongue some slight trick of their broidered\r\nand gracious speech withal!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen the illustrious maidens were gone, Tom turned wearily to his\r\nkeepers and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMay it please your lordships to grant me leave to go into some corner\r\nand rest me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLord Hertford said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo please your highness, it is for you to command, it is for us to\r\nobey. That thou should\xe2\x80\x99st rest is indeed a needful thing, since thou\r\nmust journey to the city presently.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe touched a bell, and a page appeared, who was ordered to desire the\r\npresence of Sir William Herbert. \xc2\xa0This gentleman came straightway, and\r\nconducted Tom to an inner apartment. \xc2\xa0Tom\xe2\x80\x99s first movement there was\r\nto reach for a cup of water; but a silk-and-velvet servitor seized it,\r\ndropped upon one knee, and offered it to him on a golden salver.\r\n\r\nNext the tired captive sat down and was going to take off his buskins,\r\ntimidly asking leave with his eye, but another silk-and-velvet\r\ndiscomforter went down upon his knees and took the office from him. \xc2\xa0He\r\nmade two or three further efforts to help himself, but being promptly\r\nforestalled each time, he finally gave up, with a sigh of resignation\r\nand a murmured \xe2\x80\x9cBeshrew me, but I marvel they do not require to breathe\r\nfor me also!\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0Slippered, and wrapped in a sumptuous robe, he laid\r\nhimself down at last to rest, but not to sleep, for his head was too\r\nfull of thoughts and the room too full of people. \xc2\xa0He could not dismiss\r\nthe former, so they stayed; he did not know enough to dismiss the\r\nlatter, so they stayed also, to his vast regret--and theirs.\r\n\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s departure had left his two noble guardians alone. \xc2\xa0They mused a\r\nwhile, with much head-shaking and walking the floor, then Lord St. John\r\nsaid--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlainly, what dost thou think?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlainly, then, this. \xc2\xa0The King is near his end; my nephew is mad--mad\r\nwill mount the throne, and mad remain. \xc2\xa0God protect England, since she\r\nwill need it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cVerily it promiseth so, indeed. \xc2\xa0But . . . have you no misgivings as to\r\n. . . as to . . .\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe speaker hesitated, and finally stopped. \xc2\xa0He evidently felt that he\r\nwas upon delicate ground. \xc2\xa0Lord Hertford stopped before him, looked into\r\nhis face with a clear, frank eye, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpeak on--there is none to hear but me. \xc2\xa0Misgivings as to what?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am full loth to word the thing that is in my mind, and thou so near\r\nto him in blood, my lord. \xc2\xa0But craving pardon if I do offend, seemeth it\r\nnot strange that madness could so change his port and manner?--not but\r\nthat his port and speech are princely still, but that they _differ_,\r\nin one unweighty trifle or another, from what his custom was aforetime.\r\n\xc2\xa0Seemeth it not strange that madness should filch from his memory his\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s very lineaments; the customs and observances that are his due\r\nfrom such as be about him; and, leaving him his Latin, strip him of his\r\nGreek and French? \xc2\xa0My lord, be not offended, but ease my mind of its\r\ndisquiet and receive my grateful thanks. \xc2\xa0It haunteth me, his saying he\r\nwas not the prince, and so--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeace, my lord, thou utterest treason! \xc2\xa0Hast forgot the King\xe2\x80\x99s command?\r\nRemember I am party to thy crime if I but listen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSt. John paled, and hastened to say--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI was in fault, I do confess it. \xc2\xa0Betray me not, grant me this grace\r\nout of thy courtesy, and I will neither think nor speak of this thing\r\nmore. Deal not hardly with me, sir, else am I ruined.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am content, my lord. \xc2\xa0So thou offend not again, here or in the\r\nears of others, it shall be as though thou hadst not spoken. \xc2\xa0But thou\r\nneed\xe2\x80\x99st not have misgivings. \xc2\xa0He is my sister\xe2\x80\x99s son; are not his voice,\r\nhis face, his form, familiar to me from his cradle? Madness can do all\r\nthe odd conflicting things thou seest in him, and more. \xc2\xa0Dost not recall\r\nhow that the old Baron Marley, being mad, forgot the favour of his\r\nown countenance that he had known for sixty years, and held it was\r\nanother\xe2\x80\x99s; nay, even claimed he was the son of Mary Magdalene, and that\r\nhis head was made of Spanish glass; and, sooth to say, he suffered none\r\nto touch it, lest by mischance some heedless hand might shiver it? \xc2\xa0Give\r\nthy misgivings easement, good my lord. \xc2\xa0This is the very prince--I know\r\nhim well--and soon will be thy king; it may advantage thee to bear this\r\nin mind, and more dwell upon it than the other.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter some further talk, in which the Lord St. John covered up his\r\nmistake as well as he could by repeated protests that his faith was\r\nthoroughly grounded now, and could not be assailed by doubts again, the\r\nLord Hertford relieved his fellow-keeper, and sat down to keep watch and\r\nward alone. \xc2\xa0He was soon deep in meditation, and evidently the longer he\r\nthought, the more he was bothered. \xc2\xa0By-and-by he began to pace the floor\r\nand mutter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTush, he _must_ be the prince! \xc2\xa0Will any be in all the land maintain\r\nthere can be two, not of one blood and birth, so marvellously twinned?\r\n\xc2\xa0And even were it so, \xe2\x80\x99twere yet a stranger miracle that chance should\r\ncast the one into the other\xe2\x80\x99s place. Nay, \xe2\x80\x99tis folly, folly, folly!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPresently he said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow were he impostor and called himself prince, look you _that_ would\r\nbe natural; that would be reasonable. \xc2\xa0But lived ever an impostor yet,\r\nwho, being called prince by the king, prince by the court, prince by\r\nall, _denied_ his dignity and pleaded against his exaltation? \xc2\xa0_No_! \xc2\xa0By\r\nthe soul of St. Swithin, no! \xc2\xa0This is the true prince, gone mad!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VII. Tom\xe2\x80\x99s first royal dinner.\r\n\r\nSomewhat after one in the afternoon, Tom resignedly underwent the ordeal\r\nof being dressed for dinner. \xc2\xa0He found himself as finely clothed as\r\nbefore, but everything different, everything changed, from his ruff to\r\nhis stockings. \xc2\xa0He was presently conducted with much state to a spacious\r\nand ornate apartment, where a table was already set for one. \xc2\xa0Its\r\nfurniture was all of massy gold, and beautified with designs which\r\nwell-nigh made it priceless, since they were the work of Benvenuto. \xc2\xa0The\r\nroom was half-filled with noble servitors. \xc2\xa0A chaplain said grace, and\r\nTom was about to fall to, for hunger had long been constitutional with\r\nhim, but was interrupted by my lord the Earl of Berkeley, who fastened a\r\nnapkin about his neck; for the great post of Diaperers to the Prince\r\nof Wales was hereditary in this nobleman\xe2\x80\x99s family. \xc2\xa0Tom\xe2\x80\x99s cupbearer was\r\npresent, and forestalled all his attempts to help himself to wine. \xc2\xa0The\r\nTaster to his highness the Prince of Wales was there also, prepared to\r\ntaste any suspicious dish upon requirement, and run the risk of being\r\npoisoned. \xc2\xa0He was only an ornamental appendage at this time, and was\r\nseldom called upon to exercise his function; but there had been times,\r\nnot many generations past, when the office of taster had its perils,\r\nand was not a grandeur to be desired. \xc2\xa0Why they did not use a dog or a\r\nplumber seems strange; but all the ways of royalty are strange. \xc2\xa0My\r\nLord d\xe2\x80\x99Arcy, First Groom of the Chamber, was there, to do goodness knows\r\nwhat; but there he was--let that suffice. \xc2\xa0The Lord Chief Butler was\r\nthere, and stood behind Tom\xe2\x80\x99s chair, overseeing the solemnities, under\r\ncommand of the Lord Great Steward and the Lord Head Cook, who stood\r\nnear. \xc2\xa0Tom had three hundred and eighty-four servants beside these;\r\nbut they were not all in that room, of course, nor the quarter of them;\r\nneither was Tom aware yet that they existed.\r\n\r\nAll those that were present had been well drilled within the hour to\r\nremember that the prince was temporarily out of his head, and to be\r\ncareful to show no surprise at his vagaries. \xc2\xa0These \xe2\x80\x98vagaries\xe2\x80\x99 were\r\nsoon on exhibition before them; but they only moved their compassion and\r\ntheir sorrow, not their mirth. \xc2\xa0It was a heavy affliction to them to see\r\nthe beloved prince so stricken.\r\n\r\nPoor Tom ate with his fingers mainly; but no one smiled at it, or even\r\nseemed to observe it. \xc2\xa0He inspected his napkin curiously, and with deep\r\ninterest, for it was of a very dainty and beautiful fabric, then said\r\nwith simplicity--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrithee, take it away, lest in mine unheedfulness it be soiled.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Hereditary Diaperer took it away with reverent manner, and without\r\nword or protest of any sort.\r\n\r\nTom examined the turnips and the lettuce with interest, and asked what\r\nthey were, and if they were to be eaten; for it was only recently that\r\nmen had begun to raise these things in England in place of importing\r\nthem as luxuries from Holland. {1} \xc2\xa0His question was answered with grave\r\nrespect, and no surprise manifested. \xc2\xa0When he had finished his dessert,\r\nhe filled his pockets with nuts; but nobody appeared to be aware of it,\r\nor disturbed by it. \xc2\xa0But the next moment he was himself disturbed by\r\nit, and showed discomposure; for this was the only service he had been\r\npermitted to do with his own hands during the meal, and he did not doubt\r\nthat he had done a most improper and unprincely thing. \xc2\xa0At that moment\r\nthe muscles of his nose began to twitch, and the end of that organ to\r\nlift and wrinkle. \xc2\xa0This continued, and Tom began to evince a growing\r\ndistress. \xc2\xa0He looked appealingly, first at one and then another of the\r\nlords about him, and tears came into his eyes. \xc2\xa0They sprang forward with\r\ndismay in their faces, and begged to know his trouble. \xc2\xa0Tom said with\r\ngenuine anguish--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI crave your indulgence: \xc2\xa0my nose itcheth cruelly. \xc2\xa0What is the custom\r\nand usage in this emergence? \xc2\xa0Prithee, speed, for \xe2\x80\x99tis but a little time\r\nthat I can bear it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNone smiled; but all were sore perplexed, and looked one to the other\r\nin deep tribulation for counsel. \xc2\xa0But behold, here was a dead wall, and\r\nnothing in English history to tell how to get over it. \xc2\xa0The Master of\r\nCeremonies was not present: \xc2\xa0there was no one who felt safe to venture\r\nupon this uncharted sea, or risk the attempt to solve this solemn\r\nproblem. \xc2\xa0Alas! there was no Hereditary Scratcher. \xc2\xa0Meantime the tears\r\nhad overflowed their banks, and begun to trickle down Tom\xe2\x80\x99s cheeks. \xc2\xa0His\r\ntwitching nose was pleading more urgently than ever for relief. \xc2\xa0At last\r\nnature broke down the barriers of etiquette: \xc2\xa0Tom lifted up an inward\r\nprayer for pardon if he was doing wrong, and brought relief to the\r\nburdened hearts of his court by scratching his nose himself.\r\n\r\nHis meal being ended, a lord came and held before him a broad, shallow,\r\ngolden dish with fragrant rosewater in it, to cleanse his mouth and\r\nfingers with; and my lord the Hereditary Diaperer stood by with a napkin\r\nfor his use. \xc2\xa0Tom gazed at the dish a puzzled moment or two, then raised\r\nit to his lips, and gravely took a draught. \xc2\xa0Then he returned it to the\r\nwaiting lord, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, it likes me not, my lord: \xc2\xa0it hath a pretty flavour, but it\r\nwanteth strength.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis new eccentricity of the prince\xe2\x80\x99s ruined mind made all the hearts\r\nabout him ache; but the sad sight moved none to merriment.\r\n\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s next unconscious blunder was to get up and leave the table\r\njust when the chaplain had taken his stand behind his chair, and with\r\nuplifted hands, and closed, uplifted eyes, was in the act of beginning\r\nthe blessing. \xc2\xa0Still nobody seemed to perceive that the prince had done\r\na thing unusual.\r\n\r\nBy his own request our small friend was now conducted to his private\r\ncabinet, and left there alone to his own devices. \xc2\xa0Hanging upon hooks in\r\nthe oaken wainscoting were the several pieces of a suit of shining steel\r\narmour, covered all over with beautiful designs exquisitely inlaid\r\nin gold. \xc2\xa0This martial panoply belonged to the true prince--a recent\r\npresent from Madam Parr the Queen. Tom put on the greaves, the\r\ngauntlets, the plumed helmet, and such other pieces as he could don\r\nwithout assistance, and for a while was minded to call for help and\r\ncomplete the matter, but bethought him of the nuts he had brought away\r\nfrom dinner, and the joy it would be to eat them with no crowd to eye\r\nhim, and no Grand Hereditaries to pester him with undesired services;\r\nso he restored the pretty things to their several places, and soon was\r\ncracking nuts, and feeling almost naturally happy for the first time\r\nsince God for his sins had made him a prince. \xc2\xa0When the nuts were all\r\ngone, he stumbled upon some inviting books in a closet, among them one\r\nabout the etiquette of the English court. \xc2\xa0This was a prize. He lay down\r\nupon a sumptuous divan, and proceeded to instruct himself with honest\r\nzeal. \xc2\xa0Let us leave him there for the present.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER VIII. The Question of the Seal.\r\n\r\nAbout five o\xe2\x80\x99clock Henry VIII. awoke out of an unrefreshing nap, and\r\nmuttered to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cTroublous dreams, troublous dreams! Mine end is\r\nnow at hand: \xc2\xa0so say these warnings, and my failing pulses do confirm\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d Presently a wicked light flamed up in his eye, and he muttered,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet will not I die till _He_ go before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis attendants perceiving that he was awake, one of them asked his\r\npleasure concerning the Lord Chancellor, who was waiting without.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAdmit him, admit him!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed the King eagerly.\r\n\r\nThe Lord Chancellor entered, and knelt by the King\xe2\x80\x99s couch, saying--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have given order, and, according to the King\xe2\x80\x99s command, the peers of\r\nthe realm, in their robes, do now stand at the bar of the House, where,\r\nhaving confirmed the Duke of Norfolk\xe2\x80\x99s doom, they humbly wait his\r\nmajesty\xe2\x80\x99s further pleasure in the matter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King\xe2\x80\x99s face lit up with a fierce joy. \xc2\xa0Said he--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLift me up! \xc2\xa0In mine own person will I go before my Parliament, and\r\nwith mine own hand will I seal the warrant that rids me of--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis voice failed; an ashen pallor swept the flush from his cheeks; and\r\nthe attendants eased him back upon his pillows, and hurriedly assisted\r\nhim with restoratives. \xc2\xa0Presently he said sorrowfully--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlack, how have I longed for this sweet hour! and lo, too late it\r\ncometh, and I am robbed of this so coveted chance. \xc2\xa0But speed ye, speed\r\nye! let others do this happy office sith \xe2\x80\x99tis denied to me. I put my\r\nGreat Seal in commission: \xc2\xa0choose thou the lords that shall compose it,\r\nand get ye to your work. \xc2\xa0Speed ye, man! \xc2\xa0Before the sun shall rise and\r\nset again, bring me his head that I may see it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAccording to the King\xe2\x80\x99s command, so shall it be. \xc2\xa0Will\xe2\x80\x99t please your\r\nmajesty to order that the Seal be now restored to me, so that I may\r\nforth upon the business?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Seal? \xc2\xa0Who keepeth the Seal but thou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease your majesty, you did take it from me two days since, saying it\r\nshould no more do its office till your own royal hand should use it upon\r\nthe Duke of Norfolk\xe2\x80\x99s warrant.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhy, so in sooth I did: \xc2\xa0I do remember. . . . What did I with it?...\r\nI am very feeble. . . .
