So oft these days doth my memory play the\r\ntraitor with me. . . . \xe2\x80\x99tis strange, strange--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King dropped into inarticulate mumblings, shaking his grey head\r\nweakly from time to time, and gropingly trying to recollect what he\r\nhad done with the Seal. \xc2\xa0At last my Lord Hertford ventured to kneel and\r\noffer information--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSire, if that I may be so bold, here be several that do remember with\r\nme how that you gave the Great Seal into the hands of his highness the\r\nPrince of Wales to keep against the day that--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue, most true!\xe2\x80\x9d interrupted the King.  \xe2\x80\x9cFetch it! \xc2\xa0Go: \xc2\xa0time flieth!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nLord Hertford flew to Tom, but returned to the King before very long,\r\ntroubled and empty-handed. \xc2\xa0He delivered himself to this effect--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt grieveth me, my lord the King, to bear so heavy and unwelcome\r\ntidings; but it is the will of God that the prince\xe2\x80\x99s affliction abideth\r\nstill, and he cannot recall to mind that he received the Seal. \xc2\xa0So came\r\nI quickly to report, thinking it were waste of precious time, and\r\nlittle worth withal, that any should attempt to search the long array of\r\nchambers and saloons that belong unto his royal high--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA groan from the King interrupted the lord at this point. \xc2\xa0After a\r\nlittle while his majesty said, with a deep sadness in his tone--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrouble him no more, poor child. \xc2\xa0The hand of God lieth heavy upon him,\r\nand my heart goeth out in loving compassion for him, and sorrow that I\r\nmay not bear his burden on mine old trouble-weighted shoulders, and so\r\nbring him peace.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe closed his eyes, fell to mumbling, and presently was silent. After\r\na time he opened his eyes again, and gazed vacantly around until his\r\nglance rested upon the kneeling Lord Chancellor. Instantly his face\r\nflushed with wrath--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat, thou here yet! \xc2\xa0By the glory of God, an\xe2\x80\x99 thou gettest not about\r\nthat traitor\xe2\x80\x99s business, thy mitre shall have holiday the morrow for\r\nlack of a head to grace withal!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe trembling Chancellor answered--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood your Majesty, I cry you mercy! \xc2\xa0I but waited for the Seal.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMan, hast lost thy wits? \xc2\xa0The small Seal which aforetime I was wont\r\nto take with me abroad lieth in my treasury. \xc2\xa0And, since the Great Seal\r\nhath flown away, shall not it suffice? \xc2\xa0Hast lost thy wits? \xc2\xa0Begone!\r\n\xc2\xa0And hark ye--come no more till thou do bring his head.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe poor Chancellor was not long in removing himself from this dangerous\r\nvicinity; nor did the commission waste time in giving the royal assent\r\nto the work of the slavish Parliament, and appointing the morrow for the\r\nbeheading of the premier peer of England, the luckless Duke of Norfolk.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER IX. The river pageant.\r\n\r\nAt nine in the evening the whole vast river-front of the palace was\r\nblazing with light. \xc2\xa0The river itself, as far as the eye could reach\r\ncitywards, was so thickly covered with watermen\xe2\x80\x99s boats and with\r\npleasure-barges, all fringed with coloured lanterns, and gently agitated\r\nby the waves, that it resembled a glowing and limitless garden of\r\nflowers stirred to soft motion by summer winds. \xc2\xa0The grand terrace of\r\nstone steps leading down to the water, spacious enough to mass the army\r\nof a German principality upon, was a picture to see, with its ranks\r\nof royal halberdiers in polished armour, and its troops of brilliantly\r\ncostumed servitors flitting up and down, and to and fro, in the hurry of\r\npreparation.\r\n\r\nPresently a command was given, and immediately all living creatures\r\nvanished from the steps. \xc2\xa0Now the air was heavy with the hush of\r\nsuspense and expectancy. \xc2\xa0As far as one\xe2\x80\x99s vision could carry, he might\r\nsee the myriads of people in the boats rise up, and shade their eyes\r\nfrom the glare of lanterns and torches, and gaze toward the palace.\r\n\r\nA file of forty or fifty state barges drew up to the steps. \xc2\xa0They were\r\nrichly gilt, and their lofty prows and sterns were elaborately carved.\r\nSome of them were decorated with banners and streamers; some with\r\ncloth-of-gold and arras embroidered with coats-of-arms; others with\r\nsilken flags that had numberless little silver bells fastened to them,\r\nwhich shook out tiny showers of joyous music whenever the breezes\r\nfluttered them; others of yet higher pretensions, since they belonged to\r\nnobles in the prince\xe2\x80\x99s immediate service, had their sides picturesquely\r\nfenced with shields gorgeously emblazoned with armorial bearings. \xc2\xa0Each\r\nstate barge was towed by a tender. \xc2\xa0Besides the rowers, these tenders\r\ncarried each a number of men-at-arms in glossy helmet and breastplate,\r\nand a company of musicians.\r\n\r\nThe advance-guard of the expected procession now appeared in the great\r\ngateway, a troop of halberdiers.  \xe2\x80\x98They were dressed in striped hose of\r\nblack and tawny, velvet caps graced at the sides with silver roses, and\r\ndoublets of murrey and blue cloth, embroidered on the front and back\r\nwith the three feathers, the prince\xe2\x80\x99s blazon, woven in gold. \xc2\xa0Their\r\nhalberd staves were covered with crimson velvet, fastened with gilt\r\nnails, and ornamented with gold tassels. \xc2\xa0Filing off on the right and\r\nleft, they formed two long lines, extending from the gateway of the\r\npalace to the water\xe2\x80\x99s edge. \xc2\xa0A thick rayed cloth or carpet was\r\nthen unfolded, and laid down between them by attendants in the\r\ngold-and-crimson liveries of the prince. \xc2\xa0This done, a flourish of\r\ntrumpets resounded from within. \xc2\xa0A lively prelude arose from the\r\nmusicians on the water; and two ushers with white wands marched with a\r\nslow and stately pace from the portal. \xc2\xa0They were followed by an officer\r\nbearing the civic mace, after whom came another carrying the city\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nsword; then several sergeants of the city guard, in their full\r\naccoutrements, and with badges on their sleeves; then the Garter\r\nKing-at-arms, in his tabard; then several Knights of the Bath, each with\r\na white lace on his sleeve; then their esquires; then the judges, in\r\ntheir robes of scarlet and coifs; then the Lord High Chancellor of\r\nEngland, in a robe of scarlet, open before, and purfled with minever;\r\nthen a deputation of aldermen, in their scarlet cloaks; and then the\r\nheads of the different civic companies, in their robes of state. Now\r\ncame twelve French gentlemen, in splendid habiliments, consisting of\r\npourpoints of white damask barred with gold, short mantles of\r\ncrimson velvet lined with violet taffeta, and carnation coloured\r\nhauts-de-chausses, and took their way down the steps. \xc2\xa0They were of the\r\nsuite of the French ambassador, and were followed by twelve cavaliers of\r\nthe suite of the Spanish ambassador, clothed in black velvet, unrelieved\r\nby any ornament. \xc2\xa0Following these came several great English nobles with\r\ntheir attendants.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThere was a flourish of trumpets within; and the Prince\xe2\x80\x99s uncle, the\r\nfuture great Duke of Somerset, emerged from the gateway, arrayed in a\r\n\xe2\x80\x98doublet of black cloth-of-gold, and a cloak of crimson satin flowered\r\nwith gold, and ribanded with nets of silver.\xe2\x80\x99 \xc2\xa0He turned, doffed\r\nhis plumed cap, bent his body in a low reverence, and began to step\r\nbackward, bowing at each step. \xc2\xa0A prolonged trumpet-blast followed, and\r\na proclamation, \xe2\x80\x9cWay for the high and mighty the Lord Edward, Prince of\r\nWales!\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0High aloft on the palace walls a long line of red tongues of\r\nflame leapt forth with a thunder-crash; the massed world on the river\r\nburst into a mighty roar of welcome; and Tom Canty, the cause and hero\r\nof it all, stepped into view and slightly bowed his princely head.\r\n\r\nHe was \xe2\x80\x98magnificently habited in a doublet of white satin, with a\r\nfront-piece of purple cloth-of-tissue, powdered with diamonds, and edged\r\nwith ermine. \xc2\xa0Over this he wore a mantle of white cloth-of-gold, pounced\r\nwith the triple-feathered crest, lined with blue satin, set with pearls\r\nand precious stones, and fastened with a clasp of brilliants. \xc2\xa0About his\r\nneck hung the order of the Garter, and several princely foreign orders;\xe2\x80\x99\r\nand wherever light fell upon him jewels responded with a blinding flash.\r\n\xc2\xa0O Tom Canty, born in a hovel, bred in the gutters of London, familiar\r\nwith rags and dirt and misery, what a spectacle is this!\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER X. The Prince in the toils.\r\n\r\nWe left John Canty dragging the rightful prince into Offal Court, with\r\na noisy and delighted mob at his heels. \xc2\xa0There was but one person in it\r\nwho offered a pleading word for the captive, and he was not heeded; he\r\nwas hardly even heard, so great was the turmoil. \xc2\xa0The Prince continued\r\nto struggle for freedom, and to rage against the treatment he was\r\nsuffering, until John Canty lost what little patience was left in him,\r\nand raised his oaken cudgel in a sudden fury over the Prince\xe2\x80\x99s head.\r\n\xc2\xa0The single pleader for the lad sprang to stop the man\xe2\x80\x99s arm, and the\r\nblow descended upon his own wrist. \xc2\xa0Canty roared out--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou\xe2\x80\x99lt meddle, wilt thou? \xc2\xa0Then have thy reward.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis cudgel crashed down upon the meddler\xe2\x80\x99s head: \xc2\xa0there was a groan, a\r\ndim form sank to the ground among the feet of the crowd, and the next\r\nmoment it lay there in the dark alone. \xc2\xa0The mob pressed on, their\r\nenjoyment nothing disturbed by this episode.\r\n\r\nPresently the Prince found himself in John Canty\xe2\x80\x99s abode, with the door\r\nclosed against the outsiders. \xc2\xa0By the vague light of a tallow candle\r\nwhich was thrust into a bottle, he made out the main features of the\r\nloathsome den, and also the occupants of it. \xc2\xa0Two frowsy girls and\r\na middle-aged woman cowered against the wall in one corner, with the\r\naspect of animals habituated to harsh usage, and expecting and dreading\r\nit now. From another corner stole a withered hag with streaming grey\r\nhair and malignant eyes. \xc2\xa0John Canty said to this one--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTarry! \xc2\xa0There\xe2\x80\x99s fine mummeries here. \xc2\xa0Mar them not till thou\xe2\x80\x99st enjoyed\r\nthem: \xc2\xa0then let thy hand be heavy as thou wilt. \xc2\xa0Stand forth, lad. \xc2\xa0Now\r\nsay thy foolery again, an thou\xe2\x80\x99st not forgot it. Name thy name. \xc2\xa0Who art\r\nthou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe insulted blood mounted to the little prince\xe2\x80\x99s cheek once more, and\r\nhe lifted a steady and indignant gaze to the man\xe2\x80\x99s face and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Tis but ill-breeding in such as thou to command me to speak. \xc2\xa0I tell\r\nthee now, as I told thee before, I am Edward, Prince of Wales, and none\r\nother.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe stunning surprise of this reply nailed the hag\xe2\x80\x99s feet to the floor\r\nwhere she stood, and almost took her breath. \xc2\xa0She stared at the Prince\r\nin stupid amazement, which so amused her ruffianly son, that he burst\r\ninto a roar of laughter. \xc2\xa0But the effect upon Tom Canty\xe2\x80\x99s mother and\r\nsisters was different. \xc2\xa0Their dread of bodily injury gave way at once to\r\ndistress of a different sort. \xc2\xa0They ran forward with woe and dismay in\r\ntheir faces, exclaiming--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, poor Tom, poor lad!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe mother fell on her knees before the Prince, put her hands upon his\r\nshoulders, and gazed yearningly into his face through her rising tears.\r\nThen she said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my poor boy! \xc2\xa0Thy foolish reading hath wrought its woeful work at\r\nlast, and ta\xe2\x80\x99en thy wit away. \xc2\xa0Ah! why did\xe2\x80\x99st thou cleave to it when I\r\nso warned thee \xe2\x80\x98gainst it? \xc2\xa0Thou\xe2\x80\x99st broke thy mother\xe2\x80\x99s heart.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Prince looked into her face, and said gently--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThy son is well, and hath not lost his wits, good dame. \xc2\xa0Comfort thee:\r\nlet me to the palace where he is, and straightway will the King my\r\nfather restore him to thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe King thy father! \xc2\xa0Oh, my child! unsay these words that be freighted\r\nwith death for thee, and ruin for all that be near to thee. \xc2\xa0Shake of\r\nthis gruesome dream. \xc2\xa0Call back thy poor wandering memory. \xc2\xa0Look upon\r\nme. Am not I thy mother that bore thee, and loveth thee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Prince shook his head and reluctantly said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod knoweth I am loth to grieve thy heart; but truly have I never\r\nlooked upon thy face before.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe woman sank back to a sitting posture on the floor, and, covering her\r\neyes with her hands, gave way to heart-broken sobs and wailings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet the show go on!\xe2\x80\x9d shouted Canty.  \xe2\x80\x9cWhat, Nan!--what, Bet! mannerless\r\nwenches! will ye stand in the Prince\xe2\x80\x99s presence? \xc2\xa0Upon your knees, ye\r\npauper scum, and do him reverence!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe followed this with another horse-laugh. \xc2\xa0The girls began to plead\r\ntimidly for their brother; and Nan said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn thou wilt but let him to bed, father, rest and sleep will heal his\r\nmadness: \xc2\xa0prithee, do.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo, father,\xe2\x80\x9d said Bet; \xe2\x80\x9che is more worn than is his wont. \xc2\xa0To-morrow\r\nwill he be himself again, and will beg with diligence, and come not\r\nempty home again.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis remark sobered the father\xe2\x80\x99s joviality, and brought his mind to\r\nbusiness. \xc2\xa0He turned angrily upon the Prince, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe morrow must we pay two pennies to him that owns this hole; two\r\npennies, mark ye--all this money for a half-year\xe2\x80\x99s rent, else out of\r\nthis we go. \xc2\xa0Show what thou\xe2\x80\x99st gathered with thy lazy begging.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Prince said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOffend me not with thy sordid matters. \xc2\xa0I tell thee again I am the\r\nKing\xe2\x80\x99s son.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA sounding blow upon the Prince\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder from Canty\xe2\x80\x99s broad palm\r\nsent him staggering into goodwife Canty\xe2\x80\x99s arms, who clasped him to her\r\nbreast, and sheltered him from a pelting rain of cuffs and slaps by\r\ninterposing her own person. \xc2\xa0The frightened girls retreated to their\r\ncorner; but the grandmother stepped eagerly forward to assist her son.\r\n\xc2\xa0The Prince sprang away from Mrs. Canty, exclaiming--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou shalt not suffer for me, madam. \xc2\xa0Let these swine do their will\r\nupon me alone.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis speech infuriated the swine to such a degree that they set about\r\ntheir work without waste of time. \xc2\xa0Between them they belaboured the boy\r\nright soundly, and then gave the girls and their mother a beating for\r\nshowing sympathy for the victim.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow,\xe2\x80\x9d said Canty, \xe2\x80\x9cto bed, all of ye. \xc2\xa0The entertainment has tired me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe light was put out, and the family retired. \xc2\xa0As soon as the snorings\r\nof the head of the house and his mother showed that they were asleep,\r\nthe young girls crept to where the Prince lay, and covered him tenderly\r\nfrom the cold with straw and rags; and their mother crept to him also,\r\nand stroked his hair, and cried over him, whispering broken words of\r\ncomfort and compassion in his ear the while. \xc2\xa0She had saved a morsel for\r\nhim to eat, also; but the boy\xe2\x80\x99s pains had swept away all appetite--at\r\nleast for black and tasteless crusts. \xc2\xa0He was touched by her brave and\r\ncostly defence of him, and by her commiseration; and he thanked her in\r\nvery noble and princely words, and begged her to go to her sleep and try\r\nto forget her sorrows. \xc2\xa0And he added that the King his father would not\r\nlet her loyal kindness and devotion go unrewarded. \xc2\xa0This return to his\r\n\xe2\x80\x98madness\xe2\x80\x99 broke her heart anew, and she strained him to her breast again\r\nand again, and then went back, drowned in tears, to her bed.\r\n\r\nAs she lay thinking and mourning, the suggestion began to creep into\r\nher mind that there was an undefinable something about this boy that was\r\nlacking in Tom Canty, mad or sane. \xc2\xa0She could not describe it, she could\r\nnot tell just what it was, and yet her sharp mother-instinct seemed to\r\ndetect it and perceive it. \xc2\xa0What if the boy were really not her son,\r\nafter all? \xc2\xa0Oh, absurd! \xc2\xa0She almost smiled at the idea, spite of her\r\ngriefs and troubles. \xc2\xa0No matter, she found that it was an idea that\r\nwould not \xe2\x80\x98down,\xe2\x80\x99 but persisted in haunting her. \xc2\xa0It pursued her, it\r\nharassed her, it clung to her, and refused to be put away or ignored.\r\n\xc2\xa0At last she perceived that there was not going to be any peace for her\r\nuntil she should devise a test that should prove, clearly and without\r\nquestion, whether this lad was her son or not, and so banish these\r\nwearing and worrying doubts. \xc2\xa0Ah, yes, this was plainly the right way\r\nout of the difficulty; therefore she set her wits to work at once to\r\ncontrive that test. \xc2\xa0But it was an easier thing to propose than to\r\naccomplish. \xc2\xa0She turned over in her mind one promising test after\r\nanother, but was obliged to relinquish them all--none of them were\r\nabsolutely sure, absolutely perfect; and an imperfect one could not\r\nsatisfy her. \xc2\xa0Evidently she was racking her head in vain--it seemed\r\nmanifest that she must give the matter up. \xc2\xa0While this depressing\r\nthought was passing through her mind, her ear caught the regular\r\nbreathing of the boy, and she knew he had fallen asleep. \xc2\xa0And while she\r\nlistened, the measured breathing was broken by a soft, startled\r\ncry, such as one utters in a troubled dream. \xc2\xa0This chance occurrence\r\nfurnished her instantly with a plan worth all her laboured tests\r\ncombined. \xc2\xa0She at once set herself feverishly, but noiselessly, to work\r\nto relight her candle, muttering to herself, \xe2\x80\x9cHad I but seen him _then_,\r\nI should have known! \xc2\xa0Since that day, when he was little, that the\r\npowder burst in his face, he hath never been startled of a sudden out of\r\nhis dreams or out of his thinkings, but he hath cast his hand before his\r\neyes, even as he did that day; and not as others would do it, with the\r\npalm inward, but always with the palm turned outward--I have seen it a\r\nhundred times, and it hath never varied nor ever failed. \xc2\xa0Yes, I shall\r\nsoon know, now!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBy this time she had crept to the slumbering boy\xe2\x80\x99s side, with the\r\ncandle, shaded, in her hand. \xc2\xa0She bent heedfully and warily over him,\r\nscarcely breathing in her suppressed excitement, and suddenly flashed\r\nthe light in his face and struck the floor by his ear with her knuckles.\r\n\xc2\xa0The sleeper\xe2\x80\x99s eyes sprang wide open, and he cast a startled stare about\r\nhim--but he made no special movement with his hands.\r\n\r\nThe poor woman was smitten almost helpless with surprise and grief;\r\nbut she contrived to hide her emotions, and to soothe the boy to sleep\r\nagain; then she crept apart and communed miserably with herself upon\r\nthe disastrous result of her experiment. \xc2\xa0She tried to believe that her\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s madness had banished this habitual gesture of his; but she could\r\nnot do it.  \xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d she said, \xe2\x80\x9chis _hands_ are not mad; they could not\r\nunlearn so old a habit in so brief a time. \xc2\xa0Oh, this is a heavy day for\r\nme!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStill, hope was as stubborn now as doubt had been before; she could not\r\nbring herself to accept the verdict of the test; she must try the thing\r\nagain--the failure must have been only an accident; so she startled the\r\nboy out of his sleep a second and a third time, at intervals--with the\r\nsame result which had marked the first test; then she dragged herself to\r\nbed, and fell sorrowfully asleep, saying, \xe2\x80\x9cBut I cannot give him up--oh\r\nno, I cannot, I cannot--he _must_ be my boy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe poor mother\xe2\x80\x99s interruptions having ceased, and the Prince\xe2\x80\x99s pains\r\nhaving gradually lost their power to disturb him, utter weariness at\r\nlast sealed his eyes in a profound and restful sleep. Hour after hour\r\nslipped away, and still he slept like the dead. Thus four or five hours\r\npassed. Then his stupor began to lighten. Presently, while half asleep\r\nand half awake, he murmured--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSir William!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter a moment--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHo, Sir William Herbert! \xc2\xa0Hie thee hither, and list to the strangest\r\ndream that ever . . . Sir William! dost hear? \xc2\xa0Man, I did think me\r\nchanged to a pauper, and . . . Ho there! \xc2\xa0Guards! Sir William! \xc2\xa0What!\r\nis there no groom of the chamber in waiting? Alack! it shall go hard\r\nwith--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat aileth thee?\xe2\x80\x9d asked a whisper near him.  \xe2\x80\x9cWho art thou calling?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSir William Herbert. \xc2\xa0Who art thou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI? \xc2\xa0Who should I be, but thy sister Nan? \xc2\xa0Oh, Tom, I had forgot!\r\nThou\xe2\x80\x99rt mad yet--poor lad, thou\xe2\x80\x99rt mad yet: \xc2\xa0would I had never woke to\r\nknow it again! \xc2\xa0But prithee master thy tongue, lest we be all beaten\r\ntill we die!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe startled Prince sprang partly up, but a sharp reminder from his\r\nstiffened bruises brought him to himself, and he sank back among his\r\nfoul straw with a moan and the ejaculation--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlas! it was no dream, then!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn a moment all the heavy sorrow and misery which sleep had banished\r\nwere upon him again, and he realised that he was no longer a petted\r\nprince in a palace, with the adoring eyes of a nation upon him, but\r\na pauper, an outcast, clothed in rags, prisoner in a den fit only for\r\nbeasts, and consorting with beggars and thieves.\r\n\r\nIn the midst of his grief he began to be conscious of hilarious noises\r\nand shoutings, apparently but a block or two away. \xc2\xa0The next moment\r\nthere were several sharp raps at the door; John Canty ceased from\r\nsnoring and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho knocketh? \xc2\xa0What wilt thou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA voice answered--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKnow\xe2\x80\x99st thou who it was thou laid thy cudgel on?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. \xc2\xa0Neither know I, nor care.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBelike thou\xe2\x80\x99lt change thy note eftsoons. \xc2\xa0An thou would save thy neck,\r\nnothing but flight may stead thee. \xc2\xa0The man is this moment delivering up\r\nthe ghost.  \xe2\x80\x99Tis the priest, Father Andrew!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod-a-mercy!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Canty. \xc2\xa0He roused his family, and hoarsely\r\ncommanded, \xe2\x80\x9cUp with ye all and fly--or bide where ye are and perish!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nScarcely five minutes later the Canty household were in the street and\r\nflying for their lives. \xc2\xa0John Canty held the Prince by the wrist, and\r\nhurried him along the dark way, giving him this caution in a low voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMind thy tongue, thou mad fool, and speak not our name. \xc2\xa0I will choose\r\nme a new name, speedily, to throw the law\xe2\x80\x99s dogs off the scent. \xc2\xa0Mind\r\nthy tongue, I tell thee!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe growled these words to the rest of the family--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it so chance that we be separated, let each make for London Bridge;\r\nwhoso findeth himself as far as the last linen-draper\xe2\x80\x99s shop on the\r\nbridge, let him tarry there till the others be come, then will we flee\r\ninto Southwark together.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt this moment the party burst suddenly out of darkness into light;\r\nand not only into light, but into the midst of a multitude of singing,\r\ndancing, and shouting people, massed together on the river frontage.\r\nThere was a line of bonfires stretching as far as one could see, up\r\nand down the Thames; London Bridge was illuminated; Southwark Bridge\r\nlikewise; the entire river was aglow with the flash and sheen of\r\ncoloured lights; and constant explosions of fireworks filled the skies\r\nwith an intricate commingling of shooting splendours and a thick rain\r\nof dazzling sparks that almost turned night into day; everywhere were\r\ncrowds of revellers; all London seemed to be at large.\r\n\r\nJohn Canty delivered himself of a furious curse and commanded a retreat;\r\nbut it was too late. \xc2\xa0He and his tribe were swallowed up in that\r\nswarming hive of humanity, and hopelessly separated from each other in\r\nan instant. We are not considering that the Prince was one of his tribe;\r\nCanty still kept his grip upon him. \xc2\xa0The Prince\xe2\x80\x99s heart was beating high\r\nwith hopes of escape, now. \xc2\xa0A burly waterman, considerably exalted with\r\nliquor, found himself rudely shoved by Canty in his efforts to plough\r\nthrough the crowd; he laid his great hand on Canty\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, whither so fast, friend? \xc2\xa0Dost canker thy soul with sordid\r\nbusiness when all that be leal men and true make holiday?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMine affairs are mine own, they concern thee not,\xe2\x80\x9d answered Canty,\r\nroughly; \xe2\x80\x9ctake away thy hand and let me pass.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSith that is thy humour, thou\xe2\x80\x99lt _not_ pass, till thou\xe2\x80\x99st drunk to the\r\nPrince of Wales, I tell thee that,\xe2\x80\x9d said the waterman, barring the way\r\nresolutely.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive me the cup, then, and make speed, make speed!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOther revellers were interested by this time. \xc2\xa0They cried out--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe loving-cup, the loving-cup! make the sour knave drink the\r\nloving-cup, else will we feed him to the fishes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo a huge loving-cup was brought; the waterman, grasping it by one of\r\nits handles, and with the other hand bearing up the end of an imaginary\r\nnapkin, presented it in due and ancient form to Canty, who had to grasp\r\nthe opposite handle with one of his hands and take off the lid with the\r\nother, according to ancient custom. This left the Prince hand-free for\r\na second, of course. \xc2\xa0He wasted no time, but dived among the forest of\r\nlegs about him and disappeared. \xc2\xa0In another moment he could not have\r\nbeen harder to find, under that tossing sea of life, if its billows had\r\nbeen the Atlantic\xe2\x80\x99s and he a lost sixpence.\r\n\r\nHe very soon realised this fact, and straightway busied himself about\r\nhis own affairs without further thought of John Canty. \xc2\xa0He quickly\r\nrealised another thing, too. \xc2\xa0To wit, that a spurious Prince of Wales\r\nwas being feasted by the city in his stead. \xc2\xa0He easily concluded that\r\nthe pauper lad, Tom Canty, had deliberately taken advantage of his\r\nstupendous opportunity and become a usurper.\r\n\r\nTherefore there was but one course to pursue--find his way to the\r\nGuildhall, make himself known, and denounce the impostor. \xc2\xa0He also made\r\nup his mind that Tom should be allowed a reasonable time for spiritual\r\npreparation, and then be hanged, drawn and quartered, according to the\r\nlaw and usage of the day in cases of high treason.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XI. At Guildhall.\r\n\r\nThe royal barge, attended by its gorgeous fleet, took its stately way\r\ndown the Thames through the wilderness of illuminated boats. The air was\r\nladen with music; the river banks were beruffled with joy-flames; the\r\ndistant city lay in a soft luminous glow from its countless invisible\r\nbonfires; above it rose many a slender spire into the sky, incrusted\r\nwith sparkling lights, wherefore in their remoteness they seemed like\r\njewelled lances thrust aloft; as the fleet swept along, it was greeted\r\nfrom the banks with a continuous hoarse roar of cheers and the ceaseless\r\nflash and boom of artillery.\r\n\r\nTo Tom Canty, half buried in his silken cushions, these sounds and this\r\nspectacle were a wonder unspeakably sublime and astonishing. To his\r\nlittle friends at his side, the Princess Elizabeth and the Lady Jane\r\nGrey, they were nothing.\r\n\r\nArrived at the Dowgate, the fleet was towed up the limpid Walbrook\r\n(whose channel has now been for two centuries buried out of sight under\r\nacres of buildings) to Bucklersbury, past houses and under bridges\r\npopulous with merry-makers and brilliantly lighted, and at last came to\r\na halt in a basin where now is Barge Yard, in the centre of the ancient\r\ncity of London. \xc2\xa0Tom disembarked, and he and his gallant procession\r\ncrossed Cheapside and made a short march through the Old Jewry and\r\nBasinghall Street to the Guildhall.\r\n\r\nTom and his little ladies were received with due ceremony by the Lord\r\nMayor and the Fathers of the City, in their gold chains and scarlet\r\nrobes of state, and conducted to a rich canopy of state at the head of\r\nthe great hall, preceded by heralds making proclamation, and by the Mace\r\nand the City Sword. \xc2\xa0The lords and ladies who were to attend upon Tom\r\nand his two small friends took their places behind their chairs.\r\n\r\nAt a lower table the Court grandees and other guests of noble degree\r\nwere seated, with the magnates of the city; the commoners took places at\r\na multitude of tables on the main floor of the hall. \xc2\xa0From their lofty\r\nvantage-ground the giants Gog and Magog, the ancient guardians of the\r\ncity, contemplated the spectacle below them with eyes grown familiar\r\nto it in forgotten generations. \xc2\xa0There was a bugle-blast and a\r\nproclamation, and a fat butler appeared in a high perch in the leftward\r\nwall, followed by his servitors bearing with impressive solemnity a\r\nroyal baron of beef, smoking hot and ready for the knife.\r\n\r\nAfter grace, Tom (being instructed) rose--and the whole house with\r\nhim--and drank from a portly golden loving-cup with the Princess\r\nElizabeth; from her it passed to the Lady Jane, and then traversed the\r\ngeneral assemblage. \xc2\xa0So the banquet began.\r\n\r\nBy midnight the revelry was at its height. \xc2\xa0Now came one of those\r\npicturesque spectacles so admired in that old day. \xc2\xa0A description of it\r\nis still extant in the quaint wording of a chronicler who witnessed it:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Space being made, presently entered a baron and an earl appareled after\r\nthe Turkish fashion in long robes of bawdkin powdered with gold; hats on\r\ntheir heads of crimson velvet, with great rolls of gold, girded with two\r\nswords, called scimitars, hanging by great bawdricks of gold. \xc2\xa0Next came\r\nyet another baron and another earl, in two long gowns of yellow satin,\r\ntraversed with white satin, and in every bend of white was a bend of\r\ncrimson satin, after the fashion of Russia, with furred hats of gray on\r\ntheir heads; either of them having an hatchet in their hands, and boots\r\nwith pykes\xe2\x80\x99 (points a foot long), \xe2\x80\x99turned up. \xc2\xa0And after them came\r\na knight, then the Lord High Admiral, and with him five nobles, in\r\ndoublets of crimson velvet, voyded low on the back and before to the\r\ncannell-bone, laced on the breasts with chains of silver; and over\r\nthat, short cloaks of crimson satin, and on their heads hats after\r\nthe dancers\xe2\x80\x99 fashion, with pheasants\xe2\x80\x99 feathers in them. \xc2\xa0These were\r\nappareled after the fashion of Prussia. \xc2\xa0The torchbearers, which were\r\nabout an hundred, were appareled in crimson satin and green, like Moors,\r\ntheir faces black. Next came in a mommarye. Then the minstrels, which\r\nwere disguised, danced; and the lords and ladies did wildly dance also,\r\nthat it was a pleasure to behold.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nAnd while Tom, in his high seat, was gazing upon this \xe2\x80\x98wild\xe2\x80\x99 dancing,\r\nlost in admiration of the dazzling commingling of kaleidoscopic colours\r\nwhich the whirling turmoil of gaudy figures below him presented, the\r\nragged but real little Prince of Wales was proclaiming his rights and\r\nhis wrongs, denouncing the impostor, and clamouring for admission at\r\nthe gates of Guildhall! The crowd enjoyed this episode prodigiously,\r\nand pressed forward and craned their necks to see the small rioter.\r\nPresently they began to taunt him and mock at him, purposely to goad him\r\ninto a higher and still more entertaining fury. \xc2\xa0Tears of mortification\r\nsprang to his eyes, but he stood his ground and defied the mob right\r\nroyally. \xc2\xa0Other taunts followed, added mockings stung him, and he\r\nexclaimed--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI tell ye again, you pack of unmannerly curs, I am the Prince of Wales!\r\nAnd all forlorn and friendless as I be, with none to give me word of\r\ngrace or help me in my need, yet will not I be driven from my ground,\r\nbut will maintain it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThough thou be prince or no prince, \xe2\x80\x99tis all one, thou be\xe2\x80\x99st a gallant\r\nlad, and not friendless neither! \xc2\xa0Here stand I by thy side to prove\r\nit; and mind I tell thee thou might\xe2\x80\x99st have a worser friend than Miles\r\nHendon and yet not tire thy legs with seeking. Rest thy small jaw, my\r\nchild; I talk the language of these base kennel-rats like to a very\r\nnative.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe speaker was a sort of Don Caesar de Bazan in dress, aspect, and\r\nbearing. \xc2\xa0He was tall, trim-built, muscular. \xc2\xa0His doublet and trunks\r\nwere of rich material, but faded and threadbare, and their gold-lace\r\nadornments were sadly tarnished; his ruff was rumpled and damaged;\r\nthe plume in his slouched hat was broken and had a bedraggled and\r\ndisreputable look; at his side he wore a long rapier in a rusty iron\r\nsheath; his swaggering carriage marked him at once as a ruffler of\r\nthe camp. \xc2\xa0The speech of this fantastic figure was received with an\r\nexplosion of jeers and laughter. \xc2\xa0Some cried, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Tis another prince in\r\ndisguise!\xe2\x80\x9d \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Ware thy tongue, friend: \xc2\xa0belike he is dangerous!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n  \xe2\x80\x9cMarry, he looketh it--mark his eye!\xe2\x80\x9d  \xe2\x80\x9cPluck the lad from him--to the\r\nhorse-pond wi\xe2\x80\x99 the cub!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nInstantly a hand was laid upon the Prince, under the impulse of this\r\nhappy thought; as instantly the stranger\xe2\x80\x99s long sword was out and the\r\nmeddler went to the earth under a sounding thump with the flat of it.\r\nThe next moment a score of voices shouted, \xe2\x80\x9cKill the dog! \xc2\xa0Kill him!\r\nKill him!\xe2\x80\x9d and the mob closed in on the warrior, who backed himself\r\nagainst a wall and began to lay about him with his long weapon like a\r\nmadman. \xc2\xa0His victims sprawled this way and that, but the mob-tide poured\r\nover their prostrate forms and dashed itself against the champion with\r\nundiminished fury.\r\n\r\nHis moments seemed numbered, his destruction certain, when suddenly a\r\ntrumpet-blast sounded, a voice shouted, \xe2\x80\x9cWay for the King\xe2\x80\x99s messenger!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n and a troop of horsemen came charging down upon the mob, who fled out of\r\nharm\xe2\x80\x99s reach as fast as their legs could carry them. The bold stranger\r\ncaught up the Prince in his arms, and was soon far away from danger and\r\nthe multitude.\r\n\r\nReturn we within the Guildhall. \xc2\xa0Suddenly, high above the jubilant roar\r\nand thunder of the revel, broke the clear peal of a bugle-note. \xc2\xa0There\r\nwas instant silence--a deep hush; then a single voice rose--that of the\r\nmessenger from the palace--and began to pipe forth a proclamation, the\r\nwhole multitude standing listening.\r\n\r\nThe closing words, solemnly pronounced, were--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe King is dead!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe great assemblage bent their heads upon their breasts with one\r\naccord; remained so, in profound silence, a few moments; then all sank\r\nupon their knees in a body, stretched out their hands toward Tom, and a\r\nmighty shout burst forth that seemed to shake the building--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLong live the King!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoor Tom\xe2\x80\x99s dazed eyes wandered abroad over this stupefying spectacle,\r\nand finally rested dreamily upon the kneeling princesses beside him, a\r\nmoment, then upon the Earl of Hertford. A sudden purpose dawned in his\r\nface. \xc2\xa0He said, in a low tone, at Lord Hertford\xe2\x80\x99s ear--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnswer me truly, on thy faith and honour! \xc2\xa0Uttered I here a command,\r\nthe which none but a king might hold privilege and prerogative to utter,\r\nwould such commandment be obeyed, and none rise up to say me nay?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone, my liege, in all these realms. \xc2\xa0In thy person bides the majesty\r\nof England. \xc2\xa0Thou art the king--thy word is law.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom responded, in a strong, earnest voice, and with great animation--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen shall the king\xe2\x80\x99s law be law of mercy, from this day, and never\r\nmore be law of blood! \xc2\xa0Up from thy knees and away! \xc2\xa0To the Tower, and\r\nsay the King decrees the Duke of Norfolk shall not die!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe words were caught up and carried eagerly from lip to lip far and\r\nwide over the hall, and as Hertford hurried from the presence, another\r\nprodigious shout burst forth--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe reign of blood is ended! \xc2\xa0Long live Edward, King of England!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XII. The Prince and his Deliverer.\r\n\r\nAs soon as Miles Hendon and the little prince were clear of the mob,\r\nthey struck down through back lanes and alleys toward the river. \xc2\xa0Their\r\nway was unobstructed until they approached London Bridge; then they\r\nploughed into the multitude again, Hendon keeping a fast grip upon\r\nthe Prince\xe2\x80\x99s--no, the King\xe2\x80\x99s--wrist. \xc2\xa0The tremendous news was already\r\nabroad, and the boy learned it from a thousand voices at once--\xe2\x80\x9cThe King\r\nis dead!\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0The tidings struck a chill to the heart of the poor little\r\nwaif, and sent a shudder through his frame. \xc2\xa0He realised the greatness\r\nof his loss, and was filled with a bitter grief; for the grim tyrant who\r\nhad been such a terror to others had always been gentle with him. \xc2\xa0The\r\ntears sprang to his eyes and blurred all objects. \xc2\xa0For an instant\r\nhe felt himself the most forlorn, outcast, and forsaken of God\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncreatures--then another cry shook the night with its far-reaching\r\nthunders:  \xe2\x80\x9cLong live King Edward the Sixth!\xe2\x80\x9d and this made his eyes\r\nkindle, and thrilled him with pride to his fingers\xe2\x80\x99 ends. \xe2\x80\x9cAh,\xe2\x80\x9d he\r\nthought, \xe2\x80\x9chow grand and strange it seems--_I am King_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOur friends threaded their way slowly through the throngs upon the\r\nbridge. \xc2\xa0This structure, which had stood for six hundred years, and\r\nhad been a noisy and populous thoroughfare all that time, was a curious\r\naffair, for a closely packed rank of stores and shops, with family\r\nquarters overhead, stretched along both sides of it, from one bank of\r\nthe river to the other. \xc2\xa0The Bridge was a sort of town to itself; it\r\nhad its inn, its beer-houses, its bakeries, its haberdasheries, its food\r\nmarkets, its manufacturing industries, and even its church. \xc2\xa0It\r\nlooked upon the two neighbours which it linked together--London\r\nand Southwark--as being well enough as suburbs, but not otherwise\r\nparticularly important. \xc2\xa0It was a close corporation, so to speak; it was\r\na narrow town, of a single street a fifth of a mile long, its\r\npopulation was but a village population and everybody in it knew all\r\nhis fellow-townsmen intimately, and had known their fathers and mothers\r\nbefore them--and all their little family affairs into the bargain. \xc2\xa0It\r\nhad its aristocracy, of course--its fine old families of butchers, and\r\nbakers, and what-not, who had occupied the same old premises for five\r\nor six hundred years, and knew the great history of the Bridge from\r\nbeginning to end, and all its strange legends; and who always talked\r\nbridgy talk, and thought bridgy thoughts, and lied in a long, level,\r\ndirect, substantial bridgy way. \xc2\xa0It was just the sort of population to\r\nbe narrow and ignorant and self-conceited. Children were born on the\r\nBridge, were reared there, grew to old age, and finally died without\r\never having set a foot upon any part of the world but London Bridge\r\nalone. \xc2\xa0Such people would naturally imagine that the mighty and\r\ninterminable procession which moved through its street night and day,\r\nwith its confused roar of shouts and cries, its neighings and bellowing\r\nand bleatings and its muffled thunder-tramp, was the one great thing in\r\nthis world, and themselves somehow the proprietors of it. \xc2\xa0And so they\r\nwere, in effect--at least they could exhibit it from their windows, and\r\ndid--for a consideration--whenever a returning king or hero gave it a\r\nfleeting splendour, for there was no place like it for affording a long,\r\nstraight, uninterrupted view of marching columns.\r\n\r\nMen born and reared upon the Bridge found life unendurably dull and\r\ninane elsewhere. \xc2\xa0History tells of one of these who left the Bridge at\r\nthe age of seventy-one and retired to the country. \xc2\xa0But he could only\r\nfret and toss in his bed; he could not go to sleep, the deep stillness\r\nwas so painful, so awful, so oppressive. \xc2\xa0When he was worn out with it,\r\nat last, he fled back to his old home, a lean and haggard spectre, and\r\nfell peacefully to rest and pleasant dreams under the lulling music of\r\nthe lashing waters and the boom and crash and thunder of London Bridge.\r\n\r\nIn the times of which we are writing, the Bridge furnished \xe2\x80\x98object\r\nlessons\xe2\x80\x99 in English history for its children--namely, the livid and\r\ndecaying heads of renowned men impaled upon iron spikes atop of its\r\ngateways. \xc2\xa0But we digress.\r\n\r\nHendon\xe2\x80\x99s lodgings were in the little inn on the Bridge. \xc2\xa0As he neared\r\nthe door with his small friend, a rough voice said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo, thou\xe2\x80\x99rt come at last! \xc2\xa0Thou\xe2\x80\x99lt not escape again, I warrant thee;\r\nand if pounding thy bones to a pudding can teach thee somewhat, thou\xe2\x80\x99lt\r\nnot keep us waiting another time, mayhap,\xe2\x80\x9d--and John Canty put out his\r\nhand to seize the boy.\r\n\r\nMiles Hendon stepped in the way and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot too fast, friend. \xc2\xa0Thou art needlessly rough, methinks. \xc2\xa0What is\r\nthe lad to thee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf it be any business of thine to make and meddle in others\xe2\x80\x99 affairs,\r\nhe is my son.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Tis a lie!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the little King, hotly.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBoldly said, and I believe thee, whether thy small headpiece be sound\r\nor cracked, my boy. \xc2\xa0But whether this scurvy ruffian be thy father\r\nor no, \xe2\x80\x99tis all one, he shall not have thee to beat thee and abuse,\r\naccording to his threat, so thou prefer to bide with me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do, I do--I know him not, I loathe him, and will die before I will go\r\nwith him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen \xe2\x80\x99tis settled, and there is nought more to say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe will see, as to that!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed John Canty, striding past Hendon to\r\nget at the boy; \xe2\x80\x9cby force shall he--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf thou do but touch him, thou animated offal, I will spit thee like a\r\ngoose!\xe2\x80\x9d said Hendon, barring the way and laying his hand upon his sword\r\nhilt. \xc2\xa0Canty drew back.  \xe2\x80\x9cNow mark ye,\xe2\x80\x9d continued Hendon, \xe2\x80\x9cI took this\r\nlad under my protection when a mob of such as thou would have mishandled\r\nhim, mayhap killed him; dost imagine I will desert him now to a worser\r\nfate?--for whether thou art his father or no--and sooth to say, I think\r\nit is a lie--a decent swift death were better for such a lad than life\r\nin such brute hands as thine. \xc2\xa0So go thy ways, and set quick about it,\r\nfor I like not much bandying of words, being not over-patient in my\r\nnature.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohn Canty moved off, muttering threats and curses, and was swallowed\r\nfrom sight in the crowd. \xc2\xa0Hendon ascended three flights of stairs to his\r\nroom, with his charge, after ordering a meal to be sent thither. \xc2\xa0It\r\nwas a poor apartment, with a shabby bed and some odds and ends of old\r\nfurniture in it, and was vaguely lighted by a couple of sickly candles.\r\nThe little King dragged himself to the bed and lay down upon it, almost\r\nexhausted with hunger and fatigue. \xc2\xa0He had been on his feet a good\r\npart of a day and a night (for it was now two or three o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the\r\nmorning), and had eaten nothing meantime. \xc2\xa0He murmured drowsily--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrithee call me when the table is spread,\xe2\x80\x9d and sank into a deep sleep\r\nimmediately.\r\n\r\nA smile twinkled in Hendon\xe2\x80\x99s eye, and he said to himself--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the mass, the little beggar takes to one\xe2\x80\x99s quarters and usurps one\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbed with as natural and easy a grace as if he owned them--with never\r\na by-your-leave or so-please-it-you, or anything of the sort. \xc2\xa0In his\r\ndiseased ravings he called himself the Prince of Wales, and bravely doth\r\nhe keep up the character. \xc2\xa0Poor little friendless rat, doubtless his\r\nmind has been disordered with ill-usage. \xc2\xa0Well, I will be his friend;\r\nI have saved him, and it draweth me strongly to him; already I love the\r\nbold-tongued little rascal. \xc2\xa0How soldier-like he faced the smutty rabble\r\nand flung back his high defiance! \xc2\xa0And what a comely, sweet and gentle\r\nface he hath, now that sleep hath conjured away its troubles and its\r\ngriefs. I will teach him; I will cure his malady; yea, I will be his\r\nelder brother, and care for him and watch over him; and whoso would\r\nshame him or do him hurt may order his shroud, for though I be burnt for\r\nit he shall need it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe bent over the boy and contemplated him with kind and pitying\r\ninterest, tapping the young cheek tenderly and smoothing back the\r\ntangled curls with his great brown hand. \xc2\xa0A slight shiver passed over\r\nthe boy\xe2\x80\x99s form. Hendon muttered--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSee, now, how like a man it was to let him lie here uncovered and fill\r\nhis body with deadly rheums. \xc2\xa0Now what shall I do? \xe2\x80\x99twill wake him to\r\ntake him up and put him within the bed, and he sorely needeth sleep.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe looked about for extra covering, but finding none, doffed his doublet\r\nand wrapped the lad in it, saying, \xe2\x80\x9cI am used to nipping air and scant\r\napparel, \xe2\x80\x99tis little I shall mind the cold!\xe2\x80\x9d--then walked up and down\r\nthe room, to keep his blood in motion, soliloquising as before.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis injured mind persuades him he is Prince of Wales; \xe2\x80\x99twill be odd to\r\nhave a Prince of Wales still with us, now that he that _was_ the prince\r\nis prince no more, but king--for this poor mind is set upon the one\r\nfantasy, and will not reason out that now it should cast by the prince\r\nand call itself the king. . . If my father liveth still, after these\r\nseven years that I have heard nought from home in my foreign dungeon, he\r\nwill welcome the poor lad and give him generous shelter for my sake; so\r\nwill my good elder brother, Arthur; my other brother, Hugh--but I will\r\ncrack his crown an _he_ interfere, the fox-hearted, ill-conditioned\r\nanimal! Yes, thither will we fare--and straightway, too.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA servant entered with a smoking meal, disposed it upon a small deal\r\ntable, placed the chairs, and took his departure, leaving such cheap\r\nlodgers as these to wait upon themselves. \xc2\xa0The door slammed after him,\r\nand the noise woke the boy, who sprang to a sitting posture, and shot\r\na glad glance about him; then a grieved look came into his face and he\r\nmurmured to himself, with a deep sigh, \xe2\x80\x9cAlack, it was but a dream, woe\r\nis me!\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0Next he noticed Miles Hendon\xe2\x80\x99s doublet--glanced from that to\r\nHendon, comprehended the sacrifice that had been made for him, and said,\r\ngently--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art good to me, yes, thou art very good to me. \xc2\xa0Take it and put it\r\non--I shall not need it more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he got up and walked to the washstand in the corner and stood\r\nthere, waiting. \xc2\xa0Hendon said in a cheery voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe\xe2\x80\x99ll have a right hearty sup and bite, now, for everything is savoury\r\nand smoking hot, and that and thy nap together will make thee a little\r\nman again, never fear!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boy made no answer, but bent a steady look, that was filled with\r\ngrave surprise, and also somewhat touched with impatience, upon the tall\r\nknight of the sword. \xc2\xa0Hendon was puzzled, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat\xe2\x80\x99s amiss?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood sir, I would wash me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, is that all? \xc2\xa0Ask no permission of Miles Hendon for aught thou\r\ncravest. \xc2\xa0Make thyself perfectly free here, and welcome, with all that\r\nare his belongings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nStill the boy stood, and moved not; more, he tapped the floor once or\r\ntwice with his small impatient foot. \xc2\xa0Hendon was wholly perplexed. \xc2\xa0Said\r\nhe--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBless us, what is it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrithee pour the water, and make not so many words!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHendon, suppressing a horse-laugh, and saying to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cBy all the\r\nsaints, but this is admirable!\xe2\x80\x9d stepped briskly forward and did the\r\nsmall insolent\xe2\x80\x99s bidding; then stood by, in a sort of stupefaction,\r\nuntil the command, \xe2\x80\x9cCome--the towel!\xe2\x80\x9d woke him sharply up. \xc2\xa0He took up a\r\ntowel, from under the boy\xe2\x80\x99s nose, and handed it to him without comment.\r\n\xc2\xa0He now proceeded to comfort his own face with a wash, and while he was\r\nat it his adopted child seated himself at the table and prepared to fall\r\nto. Hendon despatched his ablutions with alacrity, then drew back the\r\nother chair and was about to place himself at table, when the boy said,\r\nindignantly--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cForbear! \xc2\xa0Wouldst sit in the presence of the King?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis blow staggered Hendon to his foundations. \xc2\xa0He muttered to himself,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLo, the poor thing\xe2\x80\x99s madness is up with the time! \xc2\xa0It hath changed\r\nwith the great change that is come to the realm, and now in fancy is\r\nhe _king_! Good lack, I must humour the conceit, too--there is no other\r\nway--faith, he would order me to the Tower, else!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd pleased with this jest, he removed the chair from the table,\r\ntook his stand behind the King, and proceeded to wait upon him in the\r\ncourtliest way he was capable of.\r\n\r\nWhile the King ate, the rigour of his royal dignity relaxed a little,\r\nand with his growing contentment came a desire to talk. He said--\xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nthink thou callest thyself Miles Hendon, if I heard thee aright?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, Sire,\xe2\x80\x9d Miles replied; then observed to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cIf I _must_\r\nhumour the poor lad\xe2\x80\x99s madness, I must \xe2\x80\x98Sire\xe2\x80\x99 him, I must \xe2\x80\x98Majesty\xe2\x80\x99 him,\r\nI must not go by halves, I must stick at nothing that belongeth to the\r\npart I play, else shall I play it ill and work evil to this charitable\r\nand kindly cause.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King warmed his heart with a second glass of wine, and said--\xe2\x80\x9cI\r\nwould know thee--tell me thy story. \xc2\xa0Thou hast a gallant way with thee,\r\nand a noble--art nobly born?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe are of the tail of the nobility, good your Majesty. \xc2\xa0My father is\r\na baronet--one of the smaller lords by knight service {2}--Sir Richard\r\nHendon of Hendon Hall, by Monk\xe2\x80\x99s Holm in Kent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe name has escaped my memory. \xc2\xa0Go on--tell me thy story.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Tis not much, your Majesty, yet perchance it may beguile a short\r\nhalf-hour for want of a better. \xc2\xa0My father, Sir Richard, is very rich,\r\nand of a most generous nature. \xc2\xa0My mother died whilst I was yet a\r\nboy. \xc2\xa0I have two brothers: \xc2\xa0Arthur, my elder, with a soul like to\r\nhis father\xe2\x80\x99s; and Hugh, younger than I, a mean spirit, covetous,\r\ntreacherous, vicious, underhanded--a reptile. \xc2\xa0Such was he from the\r\ncradle; such was he ten years past, when I last saw him--a ripe rascal\r\nat nineteen, I being twenty then, and Arthur twenty-two. \xc2\xa0There is\r\nnone other of us but the Lady Edith, my cousin--she was sixteen\r\nthen--beautiful, gentle, good, the daughter of an earl, the last of her\r\nrace, heiress of a great fortune and a lapsed title. \xc2\xa0My father was her\r\nguardian. \xc2\xa0I loved her and she loved me; but she was betrothed to Arthur\r\nfrom the cradle, and Sir Richard would not suffer the contract to be\r\nbroken. \xc2\xa0Arthur loved another maid, and bade us be of good cheer and\r\nhold fast to the hope that delay and luck together would some day give\r\nsuccess to our several causes. \xc2\xa0Hugh loved the Lady Edith\xe2\x80\x99s fortune,\r\nthough in truth he said it was herself he loved--but then \xe2\x80\x99twas his way,\r\nalway, to say the one thing and mean the other. \xc2\xa0But he lost his arts\r\nupon the girl; he could deceive my father, but none else. \xc2\xa0My father\r\nloved him best of us all, and trusted and believed him; for he was the\r\nyoungest child, and others hated him--these qualities being in all\r\nages sufficient to win a parent\xe2\x80\x99s dearest love; and he had a smooth\r\npersuasive tongue, with an admirable gift of lying--and these be\r\nqualities which do mightily assist a blind affection to cozen itself.\r\n\xc2\xa0I was wild--in troth I might go yet farther and say _very_ wild, though\r\n\xe2\x80\x98twas a wildness of an innocent sort, since it hurt none but me, brought\r\nshame to none, nor loss, nor had in it any taint of crime or baseness,\r\nor what might not beseem mine honourable degree.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet did my brother Hugh turn these faults to good account--he seeing\r\nthat our brother Arthur\xe2\x80\x99s health was but indifferent, and hoping the\r\nworst might work him profit were I swept out of the path--so--but \xe2\x80\x99twere\r\na long tale, good my liege, and little worth the telling. \xc2\xa0Briefly,\r\nthen, this brother did deftly magnify my faults and make them\r\ncrimes; ending his base work with finding a silken ladder in mine\r\napartments--conveyed thither by his own means--and did convince my\r\nfather by this, and suborned evidence of servants and other lying\r\nknaves, that I was minded to carry off my Edith and marry with her in\r\nrank defiance of his will.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree years of banishment from home and England might make a soldier\r\nand a man of me, my father said, and teach me some degree of wisdom.\r\n\xc2\xa0I fought out my long probation in the continental wars, tasting\r\nsumptuously of hard knocks, privation, and adventure; but in my last\r\nbattle I was taken captive, and during the seven years that have waxed\r\nand waned since then, a foreign dungeon hath harboured me. \xc2\xa0Through wit\r\nand courage I won to the free air at last, and fled hither straight; and\r\nam but just arrived, right poor in purse and raiment, and poorer still\r\nin knowledge of what these dull seven years have wrought at Hendon Hall,\r\nits people and belongings. \xc2\xa0So please you, sir, my meagre tale is told.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou hast been shamefully abused!\xe2\x80\x9d said the little King, with a\r\nflashing eye.  \xe2\x80\x9cBut I will right thee--by the cross will I! \xc2\xa0The King\r\nhath said it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen, fired by the story of Miles\xe2\x80\x99s wrongs, he loosed his tongue and\r\npoured the history of his own recent misfortunes into the ears of his\r\nastonished listener. \xc2\xa0When he had finished, Miles said to himself--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLo, what an imagination he hath! \xc2\xa0Verily, this is no common mind; else,\r\ncrazed or sane, it could not weave so straight and gaudy a tale as this\r\nout of the airy nothings wherewith it hath wrought this curious romaunt.\r\nPoor ruined little head, it shall not lack friend or shelter whilst I\r\nbide with the living. \xc2\xa0He shall never leave my side; he shall be my\r\npet, my little comrade. \xc2\xa0And he shall be cured!--ay, made whole and\r\nsound--then will he make himself a name--and proud shall I be to say,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Yes, he is mine--I took him, a homeless little ragamuffin, but I saw\r\nwhat was in him, and I said his name would be heard some day--behold\r\nhim, observe him--was I right?\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King spoke--in a thoughtful, measured voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou didst save me injury and shame, perchance my life, and so my\r\ncrown. Such service demandeth rich reward. \xc2\xa0Name thy desire, and so it\r\nbe within the compass of my royal power, it is thine.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis fantastic suggestion startled Hendon out of his reverie. \xc2\xa0He was\r\nabout to thank the King and put the matter aside with saying he had only\r\ndone his duty and desired no reward, but a wiser thought came into his\r\nhead, and he asked leave to be silent a few moments and consider the\r\ngracious offer--an idea which the King gravely approved, remarking that\r\nit was best to be not too hasty with a thing of such great import.\r\n\r\nMiles reflected during some moments, then said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cYes, that is\r\nthe thing to do--by any other means it were impossible to get at it--and\r\ncertes, this hour\xe2\x80\x99s experience has taught me \xe2\x80\x99twould be most wearing and\r\ninconvenient to continue it as it is. Yes, I will propose it; \xe2\x80\x99twas a\r\nhappy accident that I did not throw the chance away.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0Then he dropped\r\nupon one knee and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy poor service went not beyond the limit of a subject\xe2\x80\x99s simple duty,\r\nand therefore hath no merit; but since your Majesty is pleased to hold\r\nit worthy some reward, I take heart of grace to make petition to this\r\neffect. \xc2\xa0Near four hundred years ago, as your grace knoweth, there being\r\nill blood betwixt John, King of England, and the King of France, it was\r\ndecreed that two champions should fight together in the lists, and so\r\nsettle the dispute by what is called the arbitrament of God. \xc2\xa0These two\r\nkings, and the Spanish king, being assembled to witness and judge the\r\nconflict, the French champion appeared; but so redoubtable was he, that\r\nour English knights refused to measure weapons with him. \xc2\xa0So the matter,\r\nwhich was a weighty one, was like to go against the English monarch by\r\ndefault. \xc2\xa0Now in the Tower lay the Lord de Courcy, the mightiest arm in\r\nEngland, stripped of his honours and possessions, and wasting with\r\nlong captivity. \xc2\xa0Appeal was made to him; he gave assent, and came forth\r\narrayed for battle; but no sooner did the Frenchman glimpse his huge\r\nframe and hear his famous name but he fled away, and the French king\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncause was lost. \xc2\xa0King John restored De Courcy\xe2\x80\x99s titles and possessions,\r\nand said, \xe2\x80\x98Name thy wish and thou shalt have it, though it cost me half\r\nmy kingdom;\xe2\x80\x99 whereat De Courcy, kneeling, as I do now, made answer,\r\n\xe2\x80\x98This, then, I ask, my liege; that I and my successors may have and\r\nhold the privilege of remaining covered in the presence of the kings of\r\nEngland, henceforth while the throne shall last.\xe2\x80\x99 The boon was granted,\r\nas your Majesty knoweth; and there hath been no time, these four hundred\r\nyears, that that line has failed of an heir; and so, even unto this day,\r\nthe head of that ancient house still weareth his hat or helm before the\r\nKing\xe2\x80\x99s Majesty, without let or hindrance, and this none other may do.\r\n{3} Invoking this precedent in aid of my prayer, I beseech the King to\r\ngrant to me but this one grace and privilege--to my more than sufficient\r\nreward--and none other, to wit: \xc2\xa0that I and my heirs, for ever, may\r\n_sit_ in the presence of the Majesty of England!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRise, Sir Miles Hendon, Knight,\xe2\x80\x9d said the King, gravely--giving the\r\naccolade with Hendon\xe2\x80\x99s sword--\xe2\x80\x9crise, and seat thyself. \xc2\xa0Thy petition is\r\ngranted. \xc2\xa0Whilst England remains, and the crown continues, the privilege\r\nshall not lapse.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis Majesty walked apart, musing, and Hendon dropped into a chair at\r\ntable, observing to himself, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Twas a brave thought, and hath wrought\r\nme a mighty deliverance; my legs are grievously wearied. An I had not\r\nthought of that, I must have had to stand for weeks, till my poor lad\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwits are cured.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0After a little, he went on, \xe2\x80\x9cAnd so I am become a\r\nknight of the Kingdom of Dreams and Shadows! A most odd and strange\r\nposition, truly, for one so matter-of-fact as I. \xc2\xa0I will not laugh--no,\r\nGod forbid, for this thing which is so substanceless to me is _real_ to\r\nhim. \xc2\xa0And to me, also, in one way, it is not a falsity, for it reflects\r\nwith truth the sweet and generous spirit that is in him.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0After\r\na pause: \xe2\x80\x9cAh, what if he should call me by my fine title before\r\nfolk!--there\xe2\x80\x99d be a merry contrast betwixt my glory and my raiment! \xc2\xa0But\r\nno matter, let him call me what he will, so it please him; I shall be\r\ncontent.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIII. The disappearance of the Prince.\r\n\r\nA heavy drowsiness presently fell upon the two comrades. \xc2\xa0The King\r\nsaid--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRemove these rags.\xe2\x80\x9d--meaning his clothing.\r\n\r\nHendon disapparelled the boy without dissent or remark, tucked him up in\r\nbed, then glanced about the room, saying to himself, ruefully, \xe2\x80\x9cHe hath\r\ntaken my bed again, as before--marry, what shall _I_ do?\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0The little\r\nKing observed his perplexity, and dissipated it with a word. \xc2\xa0He said,\r\nsleepily--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou wilt sleep athwart the door, and guard it.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0In a moment more he\r\nwas out of his troubles, in a deep slumber.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDear heart, he should have been born a king!\xe2\x80\x9d muttered Hendon,\r\nadmiringly; \xe2\x80\x9che playeth the part to a marvel.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he stretched himself across the door, on the floor, saying\r\ncontentedly--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have lodged worse for seven years; \xe2\x80\x99twould be but ill gratitude to\r\nHim above to find fault with this.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe dropped asleep as the dawn appeared. \xc2\xa0Toward noon he rose, uncovered\r\nhis unconscious ward--a section at a time--and took his measure with a\r\nstring. \xc2\xa0The King awoke, just as he had completed his work, complained\r\nof the cold, and asked what he was doing.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Tis done, now, my liege,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hendon; \xe2\x80\x9cI have a bit of business\r\noutside, but will presently return; sleep thou again--thou needest it.\r\nThere--let me cover thy head also--thou\xe2\x80\x99lt be warm the sooner.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King was back in dreamland before this speech was ended. Miles\r\nslipped softly out, and slipped as softly in again, in the course of\r\nthirty or forty minutes, with a complete second-hand suit of boy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nclothing, of cheap material, and showing signs of wear; but tidy, and\r\nsuited to the season of the year. \xc2\xa0He seated himself, and began to\r\noverhaul his purchase, mumbling to himself--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA longer purse would have got a better sort, but when one has not the\r\nlong purse one must be content with what a short one may do--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98There was a woman in our town, In our town did dwell--\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe stirred, methinks--I must sing in a less thunderous key; \xe2\x80\x99tis not\r\ngood to mar his sleep, with this journey before him, and he so wearied\r\nout, poor chap . . . This garment--\xe2\x80\x98tis well enough--a stitch here and\r\nanother one there will set it aright. \xc2\xa0This other is better, albeit a\r\nstitch or two will not come amiss in it, likewise . . . _These_ be very\r\ngood and sound, and will keep his small feet warm and dry--an odd new\r\nthing to him, belike, since he has doubtless been used to foot it bare,\r\nwinters and summers the same . . . Would thread were bread, seeing one\r\ngetteth a year\xe2\x80\x99s sufficiency for a farthing, and such a brave big needle\r\nwithout cost, for mere love. \xc2\xa0Now shall I have the demon\xe2\x80\x99s own time to\r\nthread it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd so he had. \xc2\xa0He did as men have always done, and probably always will\r\ndo, to the end of time--held the needle still, and tried to thrust the\r\nthread through the eye, which is the opposite of a woman\xe2\x80\x99s way. \xc2\xa0Time\r\nand time again the thread missed the mark, going sometimes on one side\r\nof the needle, sometimes on the other, sometimes doubling up against the\r\nshaft; but he was patient, having been through these experiences before,\r\nwhen he was soldiering. \xc2\xa0He succeeded at last, and took up the garment\r\nthat had lain waiting, meantime, across his lap, and began his work.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe inn is paid--the breakfast that is to come, included--and there is\r\nwherewithal left to buy a couple of donkeys and meet our little costs\r\nfor the two or three days betwixt this and the plenty that awaits us at\r\nHendon Hall--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98She loved her hus--\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBody o\xe2\x80\x99 me! \xc2\xa0I have driven the needle under my nail! . . . It matters\r\nlittle--\xe2\x80\x98tis not a novelty--yet \xe2\x80\x99tis not a convenience, neither. . . .\r\nWe shall be merry there, little one, never doubt it! Thy troubles will\r\nvanish there, and likewise thy sad distemper--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98She loved her husband dearilee, But another man--\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese be noble large stitches!\xe2\x80\x9d--holding the garment up and viewing\r\nit admiringly--\xe2\x80\x9cthey have a grandeur and a majesty that do cause\r\nthese small stingy ones of the tailor-man to look mightily paltry and\r\nplebeian--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98She loved her husband dearilee, But another man he loved she,--\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarry, \xe2\x80\x99tis done--a goodly piece of work, too, and wrought with\r\nexpedition. \xc2\xa0Now will I wake him, apparel him, pour for him, feed him,\r\nand then will we hie us to the mart by the Tabard Inn in Southwark\r\nand--be pleased to rise, my liege!--he answereth not--what ho, my\r\nliege!--of a truth must I profane his sacred person with a touch, sith\r\nhis slumber is deaf to speech. \xc2\xa0What!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe threw back the covers--the boy was gone!\r\n\r\nHe stared about him in speechless astonishment for a moment; noticed for\r\nthe first time that his ward\xe2\x80\x99s ragged raiment was also missing; then he\r\nbegan to rage and storm and shout for the innkeeper. \xc2\xa0At that moment a\r\nservant entered with the breakfast.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExplain, thou limb of Satan, or thy time is come!\xe2\x80\x9d roared the man of\r\nwar, and made so savage a spring toward the waiter that this latter\r\ncould not find his tongue, for the instant, for fright and surprise.\r\n \xe2\x80\x9cWhere is the boy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn disjointed and trembling syllables the man gave the information\r\ndesired.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou were hardly gone from the place, your worship, when a youth came\r\nrunning and said it was your worship\xe2\x80\x99s will that the boy come to you\r\nstraight, at the bridge-end on the Southwark side. \xc2\xa0I brought him\r\nhither; and when he woke the lad and gave his message, the lad did\r\ngrumble some little for being disturbed \xe2\x80\x98so early,\xe2\x80\x99 as he called it, but\r\nstraightway trussed on his rags and went with the youth, only saying\r\nit had been better manners that your worship came yourself, not sent a\r\nstranger--and so--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd so thou\xe2\x80\x99rt a fool!--a fool and easily cozened--hang all thy breed!\r\nYet mayhap no hurt is done. \xc2\xa0Possibly no harm is meant the boy. \xc2\xa0I will\r\ngo fetch him. \xc2\xa0Make the table ready. \xc2\xa0Stay! the coverings of the bed\r\nwere disposed as if one lay beneath them--happened that by accident?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know not, good your worship. \xc2\xa0I saw the youth meddle with them--he\r\nthat came for the boy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThousand deaths!  \xe2\x80\x98Twas done to deceive me--\xe2\x80\x98tis plain \xe2\x80\x99twas done to\r\ngain time. \xc2\xa0Hark ye! \xc2\xa0Was that youth alone?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll alone, your worship.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArt sure?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSure, your worship.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCollect thy scattered wits--bethink thee--take time, man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAfter a moment\xe2\x80\x99s thought, the servant said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhen he came, none came with him; but now I remember me that as the two\r\nstepped into the throng of the Bridge, a ruffian-looking man plunged out\r\nfrom some near place; and just as he was joining them--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat _then_?--out with it!\xe2\x80\x9d thundered the impatient Hendon,\r\ninterrupting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cJust then the crowd lapped them up and closed them in, and I saw no\r\nmore, being called by my master, who was in a rage because a joint that\r\nthe scrivener had ordered was forgot, though I take all the saints to\r\nwitness that to blame _me_ for that miscarriage were like holding the\r\nunborn babe to judgment for sins com--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOut of my sight, idiot! \xc2\xa0Thy prating drives me mad! \xc2\xa0Hold! Whither art\r\nflying? \xc2\xa0Canst not bide still an instant? \xc2\xa0Went they toward Southwark?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEven so, your worship--for, as I said before, as to that detestable\r\njoint, the babe unborn is no whit more blameless than--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArt here _yet_! \xc2\xa0And prating still! \xc2\xa0Vanish, lest I throttle thee!\xe2\x80\x9d The\r\nservitor vanished. \xc2\xa0Hendon followed after him, passed him, and plunged\r\ndown the stairs two steps at a stride, muttering, \xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Tis that scurvy\r\nvillain that claimed he was his son. \xc2\xa0I have lost thee, my poor little\r\nmad master--it is a bitter thought--and I had come to love thee so! \xc2\xa0No!\r\nby book and bell, _not_ lost! \xc2\xa0Not lost, for I will ransack the land\r\ntill I find thee again. \xc2\xa0Poor child, yonder is his breakfast--and mine,\r\nbut I have no hunger now; so, let the rats have it--speed, speed! that\r\nis the word!\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0As he wormed his swift way through the noisy multitudes\r\nupon the Bridge he several times said to himself--clinging to the\r\nthought as if it were a particularly pleasing one--\xe2\x80\x9cHe grumbled, but he\r\n_went_--he went, yes, because he thought Miles Hendon asked it, sweet\r\nlad--he would ne\xe2\x80\x99er have done it for another, I know it well.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIV. \xe2\x80\x98Le Roi est mort--vive le Roi.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nToward daylight of the same morning, Tom Canty stirred out of a heavy\r\nsleep and opened his eyes in the dark. \xc2\xa0He lay silent a few moments,\r\ntrying to analyse his confused thoughts and impressions, and get some\r\nsort of meaning out of them; then suddenly he burst out in a rapturous\r\nbut guarded voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see it all, I see it all! \xc2\xa0Now God be thanked, I am indeed awake at\r\nlast! \xc2\xa0Come, joy! vanish, sorrow! \xc2\xa0Ho, Nan! Bet! kick off your straw and\r\nhie ye hither to my side, till I do pour into your unbelieving ears the\r\nwildest madcap dream that ever the spirits of night did conjure up to\r\nastonish the soul of man withal! . . . Ho, Nan, I say! \xc2\xa0Bet!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA dim form appeared at his side, and a voice said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWilt deign to deliver thy commands?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCommands? . . . O, woe is me, I know thy voice! \xc2\xa0Speak thou--who am I?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou? \xc2\xa0In sooth, yesternight wert thou the Prince of Wales; to-day art\r\nthou my most gracious liege, Edward, King of England.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom buried his head among his pillows, murmuring plaintively--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlack, it was no dream! \xc2\xa0Go to thy rest, sweet sir--leave me to my\r\nsorrows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom slept again, and after a time he had this pleasant dream. \xc2\xa0He\r\nthought it was summer, and he was playing, all alone, in the fair meadow\r\ncalled Goodman\xe2\x80\x99s Fields, when a dwarf only a foot high, with long red\r\nwhiskers and a humped back, appeared to him suddenly and said, \xe2\x80\x9cDig by\r\nthat stump.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0He did so, and found twelve bright new pennies--wonderful\r\nriches! \xc2\xa0Yet this was not the best of it; for the dwarf said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI know thee. \xc2\xa0Thou art a good lad, and a deserving; thy distresses\r\nshall end, for the day of thy reward is come. \xc2\xa0Dig here every seventh\r\nday, and thou shalt find always the same treasure, twelve bright new\r\npennies. Tell none--keep the secret.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen the dwarf vanished, and Tom flew to Offal Court with his prize,\r\nsaying to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cEvery night will I give my father a penny; he\r\nwill think I begged it, it will glad his heart, and I shall no more\r\nbe beaten. One penny every week the good priest that teacheth me shall\r\nhave; mother, Nan, and Bet the other four. We be done with hunger and\r\nrags, now, done with fears and frets and savage usage.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIn his dream he reached his sordid home all out of breath, but with\r\neyes dancing with grateful enthusiasm; cast four of his pennies into his\r\nmother\xe2\x80\x99s lap and cried out--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are for thee!--all of them, every one!--for thee and Nan and\r\nBet--and honestly come by, not begged nor stolen!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe happy and astonished mother strained him to her breast and\r\nexclaimed--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt waxeth late--may it please your Majesty to rise?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAh! that was not the answer he was expecting. \xc2\xa0The dream had snapped\r\nasunder--he was awake.\r\n\r\nHe opened his eyes--the richly clad First Lord of the Bedchamber was\r\nkneeling by his couch. \xc2\xa0The gladness of the lying dream faded away--the\r\npoor boy recognised that he was still a captive and a king. \xc2\xa0The room\r\nwas filled with courtiers clothed in purple mantles--the mourning\r\ncolour--and with noble servants of the monarch. \xc2\xa0Tom sat up in bed and\r\ngazed out from the heavy silken curtains upon this fine company.\r\n\r\nThe weighty business of dressing began, and one courtier after another\r\nknelt and paid his court and offered to the little King his condolences\r\nupon his heavy loss, whilst the dressing proceeded. \xc2\xa0In the beginning, a\r\nshirt was taken up by the Chief Equerry in Waiting, who passed it to the\r\nFirst Lord of the Buckhounds, who passed it to the Second Gentleman of\r\nthe Bedchamber, who passed it to the Head Ranger of Windsor Forest,\r\nwho passed it to the Third Groom of the Stole, who passed it to the\r\nChancellor Royal of the Duchy of Lancaster, who passed it to the Master\r\nof the Wardrobe, who passed it to Norroy King-at-Arms, who passed it to\r\nthe Constable of the Tower, who passed it to the Chief Steward of the\r\nHousehold, who passed it to the Hereditary Grand Diaperer, who passed it\r\nto the Lord High Admiral of England, who passed it to the Archbishop of\r\nCanterbury, who passed it to the First Lord of the Bedchamber, who took\r\nwhat was left of it and put it on Tom. \xc2\xa0Poor little wondering chap, it\r\nreminded him of passing buckets at a fire.\r\n\r\nEach garment in its turn had to go through this slow and solemn process;\r\nconsequently Tom grew very weary of the ceremony; so weary that he felt\r\nan almost gushing gratefulness when he at last saw his long silken hose\r\nbegin the journey down the line and knew that the end of the matter\r\nwas drawing near. \xc2\xa0But he exulted too soon. \xc2\xa0The First Lord of the\r\nBedchamber received the hose and was about to encase Tom\xe2\x80\x99s legs in them,\r\nwhen a sudden flush invaded his face and he hurriedly hustled the things\r\nback into the hands of the Archbishop of Canterbury with an astounded\r\nlook and a whispered, \xe2\x80\x9cSee, my lord!\xe2\x80\x9d pointing to a something connected\r\nwith the hose. \xc2\xa0The Archbishop paled, then flushed, and passed the\r\nhose to the Lord High Admiral, whispering, \xe2\x80\x9cSee, my lord!\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0The Admiral\r\npassed the hose to the Hereditary Grand Diaperer, and had hardly breath\r\nenough in his body to ejaculate, \xe2\x80\x9cSee, my lord!\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0The hose drifted\r\nbackward along the line, to the Chief Steward of the Household, the\r\nConstable of the Tower, Norroy King-at-Arms, the Master of the Wardrobe,\r\nthe Chancellor Royal of the Duchy of Lancaster, the Third Groom of the\r\nStole, the Head Ranger of Windsor Forest, the Second Gentleman of the\r\nBedchamber, the First Lord of the Buckhounds,--accompanied always with\r\nthat amazed and
