frightened \xe2\x80\x9cSee! see!\xe2\x80\x9d--till they finally reached the\r\nhands of the Chief Equerry in Waiting, who gazed a moment, with a pallid\r\nface, upon what had caused all this dismay, then hoarsely whispered,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBody of my life, a tag gone from a truss-point!--to the Tower with\r\nthe Head Keeper of the King\xe2\x80\x99s Hose!\xe2\x80\x9d--after which he leaned upon the\r\nshoulder of the First Lord of the Buckhounds to regather his vanished\r\nstrength whilst fresh hose, without any damaged strings to them, were\r\nbrought.\r\n\r\nBut all things must have an end, and so in time Tom Canty was in a\r\ncondition to get out of bed. \xc2\xa0The proper official poured water, the\r\nproper official engineered the washing, the proper official stood by\r\nwith a towel, and by-and-by Tom got safely through the purifying stage\r\nand was ready for the services of the Hairdresser-royal. \xc2\xa0When he at\r\nlength emerged from this master\xe2\x80\x99s hands, he was a gracious figure and\r\nas pretty as a girl, in his mantle and trunks of purple satin, and\r\npurple-plumed cap. \xc2\xa0He now moved in state toward his breakfast-room,\r\nthrough the midst of the courtly assemblage; and as he passed, these\r\nfell back, leaving his way free, and dropped upon their knees.\r\n\r\nAfter breakfast he was conducted, with regal ceremony, attended by his\r\ngreat officers and his guard of fifty Gentlemen Pensioners bearing gilt\r\nbattle-axes, to the throne-room, where he proceeded to transact business\r\nof state. \xc2\xa0His \xe2\x80\x98uncle,\xe2\x80\x99 Lord Hertford, took his stand by the throne, to\r\nassist the royal mind with wise counsel.\r\n\r\nThe body of illustrious men named by the late King as his executors\r\nappeared, to ask Tom\xe2\x80\x99s approval of certain acts of theirs--rather a\r\nform, and yet not wholly a form, since there was no Protector as yet.\r\n\xc2\xa0The Archbishop of Canterbury made report of the decree of the Council\r\nof Executors concerning the obsequies of his late most illustrious\r\nMajesty, and finished by reading the signatures of the Executors, to\r\nwit: \xc2\xa0the Archbishop of Canterbury; the Lord Chancellor of England;\r\nWilliam Lord St. John; John Lord Russell; Edward Earl of Hertford; John\r\nViscount Lisle; Cuthbert Bishop of Durham--\r\n\r\nTom was not listening--an earlier clause of the document was puzzling\r\nhim. \xc2\xa0At this point he turned and whispered to Lord Hertford--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat day did he say the burial hath been appointed for?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe sixteenth of the coming month, my liege.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Tis a strange folly. \xc2\xa0Will he keep?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nPoor chap, he was still new to the customs of royalty; he was used to\r\nseeing the forlorn dead of Offal Court hustled out of the way with a\r\nvery different sort of expedition. \xc2\xa0However, the Lord Hertford set his\r\nmind at rest with a word or two.\r\n\r\nA secretary of state presented an order of the Council appointing the\r\nmorrow at eleven for the reception of the foreign ambassadors, and\r\ndesired the King\xe2\x80\x99s assent.\r\n\r\nTom turned an inquiring look toward Hertford, who whispered--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYour Majesty will signify consent. \xc2\xa0They come to testify their royal\r\nmasters\xe2\x80\x99 sense of the heavy calamity which hath visited your Grace and\r\nthe realm of England.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom did as he was bidden. \xc2\xa0Another secretary began to read a preamble\r\nconcerning the expenses of the late King\xe2\x80\x99s household, which had amounted\r\nto 28,000 pounds during the preceding six months--a sum so vast that it\r\nmade Tom Canty gasp; he gasped again when the fact appeared that 20,000\r\npounds of this money was still owing and unpaid; {4} and once more when\r\nit appeared that the King\xe2\x80\x99s coffers were about empty, and his twelve\r\nhundred servants much embarrassed for lack of the wages due them. \xc2\xa0Tom\r\nspoke out, with lively apprehension--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe be going to the dogs, \xe2\x80\x99tis plain.  \xe2\x80\x98Tis meet and necessary that we\r\ntake a smaller house and set the servants at large, sith they be of no\r\nvalue but to make delay, and trouble one with offices that harass the\r\nspirit and shame the soul, they misbecoming any but a doll, that hath\r\nnor brains nor hands to help itself withal. \xc2\xa0I remember me of a small\r\nhouse that standeth over against the fish-market, by Billingsgate--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA sharp pressure upon Tom\xe2\x80\x99s arm stopped his foolish tongue and sent a\r\nblush to his face; but no countenance there betrayed any sign that this\r\nstrange speech had been remarked or given concern.\r\n\r\nA secretary made report that forasmuch as the late King had provided in\r\nhis will for conferring the ducal degree upon the Earl of Hertford and\r\nraising his brother, Sir Thomas Seymour, to the peerage, and likewise\r\nHertford\xe2\x80\x99s son to an earldom, together with similar aggrandisements to\r\nother great servants of the Crown, the Council had resolved to hold a\r\nsitting on the 16th of February for the delivering and confirming of\r\nthese honours, and that meantime, the late King not having granted,\r\nin writing, estates suitable to the support of these dignities, the\r\nCouncil, knowing his private wishes in that regard, had thought proper\r\nto grant to Seymour \xe2\x80\x98500 pound lands,\xe2\x80\x99 and to Hertford\xe2\x80\x99s son \xe2\x80\x98800\r\npound lands, and 300 pound of the next bishop\xe2\x80\x99s lands which should fall\r\nvacant,\xe2\x80\x99--his present Majesty being willing. {5}\r\n\r\nTom was about to blurt out something about the propriety of paying the\r\nlate King\xe2\x80\x99s debts first, before squandering all this money, but a\r\ntimely touch upon his arm, from the thoughtful Hertford, saved him\r\nthis indiscretion; wherefore he gave the royal assent, without spoken\r\ncomment, but with much inward discomfort. \xc2\xa0While he sat reflecting a\r\nmoment over the ease with which he was doing strange and glittering\r\nmiracles, a happy thought shot into his mind: \xc2\xa0why not make his mother\r\nDuchess of Offal Court, and give her an estate? \xc2\xa0But a sorrowful\r\nthought swept it instantly away: he was only a king in name, these grave\r\nveterans and great nobles were his masters; to them his mother was only\r\nthe creature of a diseased mind; they would simply listen to his project\r\nwith unbelieving ears, then send for the doctor.\r\n\r\nThe dull work went tediously on. \xc2\xa0Petitions were read, and\r\nproclamations, patents, and all manner of wordy, repetitious, and\r\nwearisome papers relating to the public business; and at last Tom sighed\r\npathetically and murmured to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cIn what have I offended, that the\r\ngood God should take me away from the fields and the free air and the\r\nsunshine, to shut me up here and make me a king and afflict me so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n \xc2\xa0Then his poor muddled head nodded a while and presently drooped to his\r\nshoulder; and the business of the empire came to a standstill for want\r\nof that august factor, the ratifying power. \xc2\xa0Silence ensued around\r\nthe slumbering child, and the sages of the realm ceased from their\r\ndeliberations.\r\n\r\nDuring the forenoon, Tom had an enjoyable hour, by permission of his\r\nkeepers, Hertford and St. John, with the Lady Elizabeth and the little\r\nLady Jane Grey; though the spirits of the princesses were rather subdued\r\nby the mighty stroke that had fallen upon the royal house; and at the\r\nend of the visit his \xe2\x80\x98elder sister\xe2\x80\x99--afterwards the \xe2\x80\x98Bloody Mary\xe2\x80\x99 of\r\nhistory--chilled him with a solemn interview which had but one merit in\r\nhis eyes, its brevity. \xc2\xa0He had a few moments to himself, and then a slim\r\nlad of about twelve years of age was admitted to his presence, whose\r\nclothing, except his snowy ruff and the laces about his wrists, was of\r\nblack,--doublet, hose, and all. \xc2\xa0He bore no badge of mourning but a knot\r\nof purple ribbon on his shoulder. \xc2\xa0He advanced hesitatingly, with head\r\nbowed and bare, and dropped upon one knee in front of Tom. Tom sat still\r\nand contemplated him soberly a moment. \xc2\xa0Then he said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRise, lad. \xc2\xa0Who art thou. \xc2\xa0What wouldst have?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boy rose, and stood at graceful ease, but with an aspect of concern\r\nin his face. \xc2\xa0He said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf a surety thou must remember me, my lord. \xc2\xa0I am thy whipping-boy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy _whipping_-boy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe same, your Grace. \xc2\xa0I am Humphrey--Humphrey Marlow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom perceived that here was someone whom his keepers ought to have\r\nposted him about. \xc2\xa0The situation was delicate. \xc2\xa0What should he\r\ndo?--pretend he knew this lad, and then betray by his every utterance\r\nthat he had never heard of him before? \xc2\xa0No, that would not do. \xc2\xa0An idea\r\ncame to his relief: accidents like this might be likely to happen with\r\nsome frequency, now that business urgencies would often call Hertford\r\nand St. John from his side, they being members of the Council of\r\nExecutors; therefore perhaps it would be well to strike out a plan\r\nhimself to meet the requirements of such emergencies. \xc2\xa0Yes, that would\r\nbe a wise course--he would practise on this boy, and see what sort of\r\nsuccess he might achieve. \xc2\xa0So he stroked his brow perplexedly a moment\r\nor two, and presently said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow I seem to remember thee somewhat--but my wit is clogged and dim\r\nwith suffering--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlack, my poor master!\xe2\x80\x9d ejaculated the whipping-boy, with feeling;\r\nadding, to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cIn truth \xe2\x80\x99tis as they said--his mind is gone--alas,\r\npoor soul! \xc2\xa0But misfortune catch me, how am I forgetting! \xc2\xa0They said one\r\nmust not seem to observe that aught is wrong with him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Tis strange how my memory doth wanton with me these days,\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut mind it not--I mend apace--a little clue doth often serve to bring\r\nme back again the things and names which had escaped me. \xc2\xa0(And not they,\r\nonly, forsooth, but e\xe2\x80\x99en such as I ne\xe2\x80\x99er heard before--as this lad shall\r\nsee.) \xc2\xa0Give thy business speech.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Tis matter of small weight, my liege, yet will I touch upon it, an\xe2\x80\x99 it\r\nplease your Grace. \xc2\xa0Two days gone by, when your Majesty faulted thrice\r\nin your Greek--in the morning lessons,--dost remember it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cY-e-s--methinks I do. \xc2\xa0(It is not much of a lie--an\xe2\x80\x99 I had meddled with\r\nthe Greek at all, I had not faulted simply thrice, but forty times.)\r\nYes, I do recall it, now--go on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe master, being wroth with what he termed such slovenly and doltish\r\nwork, did promise that he would soundly whip me for it--and--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhip _thee_!\xe2\x80\x9d said Tom, astonished out of his presence of mind. \xe2\x80\x9cWhy\r\nshould he whip _thee_ for faults of mine?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, your Grace forgetteth again. \xc2\xa0He always scourgeth me when thou dost\r\nfail in thy lessons.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrue, true--I had forgot. \xc2\xa0Thou teachest me in private--then if I fail,\r\nhe argueth that thy office was lamely done, and--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my liege, what words are these? \xc2\xa0I, the humblest of thy servants,\r\npresume to teach _thee_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen where is thy blame? \xc2\xa0What riddle is this? \xc2\xa0Am I in truth gone mad,\r\nor is it thou? \xc2\xa0Explain--speak out.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut, good your Majesty, there\xe2\x80\x99s nought that needeth simplifying.--None\r\nmay visit the sacred person of the Prince of Wales with blows;\r\nwherefore, when he faulteth, \xe2\x80\x99tis I that take them; and meet it is and\r\nright, for that it is mine office and my livelihood.\xe2\x80\x9d {1}\r\n\r\nTom stared at the tranquil boy, observing to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cLo, it is a\r\nwonderful thing,--a most strange and curious trade; I marvel they have\r\nnot hired a boy to take my combings and my dressings for me--would\r\nheaven they would!--an\xe2\x80\x99 they will do this thing, I will take my lashings\r\nin mine own person, giving God thanks for the change.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he said\r\naloud--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd hast thou been beaten, poor friend, according to the promise?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, good your Majesty, my punishment was appointed for this day, and\r\nperadventure it may be annulled, as unbefitting the season of mourning\r\nthat is come upon us; I know not, and so have made bold to come hither\r\nand remind your Grace about your gracious promise to intercede in my\r\nbehalf--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWith the master? \xc2\xa0To save thee thy whipping?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, thou dost remember!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy memory mendeth, thou seest. \xc2\xa0Set thy mind at ease--thy back shall go\r\nunscathed--I will see to it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, thanks, my good lord!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the boy, dropping upon his knee again.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMayhap I have ventured far enow; and yet--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSeeing Master Humphrey hesitate, Tom encouraged him to go on, saying he\r\nwas \xe2\x80\x9cin the granting mood.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen will I speak it out, for it lieth near my heart. \xc2\xa0Sith thou art\r\nno more Prince of Wales but King, thou canst order matters as thou wilt,\r\nwith none to say thee nay; wherefore it is not in reason that thou wilt\r\nlonger vex thyself with dreary studies, but wilt burn thy books and\r\nturn thy mind to things less irksome. Then am I ruined, and mine orphan\r\nsisters with me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRuined? \xc2\xa0Prithee how?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy back is my bread, O my gracious liege! if it go idle, I starve. \xc2\xa0An\xe2\x80\x99\r\nthou cease from study mine office is gone thou\xe2\x80\x99lt need no whipping-boy.\r\nDo not turn me away!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom was touched with this pathetic distress. \xc2\xa0He said, with a right\r\nroyal burst of generosity--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDiscomfort thyself no further, lad. \xc2\xa0Thine office shall be permanent in\r\nthee and thy line for ever.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0Then he struck the boy a light blow on the\r\nshoulder with the flat of his sword, exclaiming, \xe2\x80\x9cRise, Humphrey Marlow,\r\nHereditary Grand Whipping-Boy to the Royal House of England! \xc2\xa0Banish\r\nsorrow--I will betake me to my books again, and study so ill that they\r\nmust in justice treble thy wage, so mightily shall the business of thine\r\noffice be augmented.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe grateful Humphrey responded fervidly--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThanks, O most noble master, this princely lavishness doth far surpass\r\nmy most distempered dreams of fortune. \xc2\xa0Now shall I be happy all my\r\ndays, and all the house of Marlow after me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom had wit enough to perceive that here was a lad who could be useful\r\nto him. \xc2\xa0He encouraged Humphrey to talk, and he was nothing loath.\r\n\xc2\xa0He was delighted to believe that he was helping in Tom\xe2\x80\x99s \xe2\x80\x98cure\xe2\x80\x99; for\r\nalways, as soon as he had finished calling back to Tom\xe2\x80\x99s diseased mind\r\nthe various particulars of his experiences and adventures in the royal\r\nschool-room and elsewhere about the palace, he noticed that Tom was then\r\nable to \xe2\x80\x98recall\xe2\x80\x99 the circumstances quite clearly. \xc2\xa0At the end of an\r\nhour Tom found himself well freighted with very valuable information\r\nconcerning personages and matters pertaining to the Court; so he\r\nresolved to draw instruction from this source daily; and to this end he\r\nwould give order to admit Humphrey to the royal closet whenever he might\r\ncome, provided the Majesty of England was not engaged with other people.\r\n\xc2\xa0Humphrey had hardly been dismissed when my Lord Hertford arrived with\r\nmore trouble for Tom.\r\n\r\nHe said that the Lords of the Council, fearing that some overwrought\r\nreport of the King\xe2\x80\x99s damaged health might have leaked out and got\r\nabroad, they deemed it wise and best that his Majesty should begin to\r\ndine in public after a day or two--his wholesome complexion and vigorous\r\nstep, assisted by a carefully guarded repose of manner and ease and\r\ngrace of demeanour, would more surely quiet the general pulse--in case\r\nany evil rumours _had_ gone about--than any other scheme that could be\r\ndevised.\r\n\r\nThen the Earl proceeded, very delicately, to instruct Tom as to the\r\nobservances proper to the stately occasion, under the rather thin\r\ndisguise of \xe2\x80\x98reminding\xe2\x80\x99 him concerning things already known to him; but\r\nto his vast gratification it turned out that Tom needed very little help\r\nin this line--he had been making use of Humphrey in that direction, for\r\nHumphrey had mentioned that within a few days he was to begin to dine\r\nin public; having gathered it from the swift-winged gossip of the Court.\r\nTom kept these facts to himself, however.\r\n\r\nSeeing the royal memory so improved, the Earl ventured to apply a\r\nfew tests to it, in an apparently casual way, to find out how far its\r\namendment had progressed. \xc2\xa0The results were happy, here and there, in\r\nspots--spots where Humphrey\xe2\x80\x99s tracks remained--and on the whole my lord\r\nwas greatly pleased and encouraged. \xc2\xa0So encouraged was he, indeed, that\r\nhe spoke up and said in a quite hopeful voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow am I persuaded that if your Majesty will but tax your memory yet\r\na little further, it will resolve the puzzle of the Great Seal--a loss\r\nwhich was of moment yesterday, although of none to-day, since its term\r\nof service ended with our late lord\xe2\x80\x99s life. May it please your Grace to\r\nmake the trial?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom was at sea--a Great Seal was something which he was totally\r\nunacquainted with. \xc2\xa0After a moment\xe2\x80\x99s hesitation he looked up innocently\r\nand asked--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat was it like, my lord?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Earl started, almost imperceptibly, muttering to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cAlack,\r\nhis wits are flown again!--it was ill wisdom to lead him on to strain\r\nthem\xe2\x80\x9d--then he deftly turned the talk to other matters, with the purpose\r\nof sweeping the unlucky seal out of Tom\xe2\x80\x99s thoughts--a purpose which\r\neasily succeeded.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XV. Tom as King.\r\n\r\nThe next day the foreign ambassadors came, with their gorgeous trains;\r\nand Tom, throned in awful state, received them. \xc2\xa0The splendours of the\r\nscene delighted his eye and fired his imagination at first, but\r\nthe audience was long and dreary, and so were most of the\r\naddresses--wherefore, what began as a pleasure grew into weariness and\r\nhome-sickness by-and-by. \xc2\xa0Tom said the words which Hertford put into\r\nhis mouth from time to time, and tried hard to acquit himself\r\nsatisfactorily, but he was too new to such things, and too ill at ease\r\nto accomplish more than a tolerable success. \xc2\xa0He looked sufficiently\r\nlike a king, but he was ill able to feel like one. \xc2\xa0He was cordially\r\nglad when the ceremony was ended.\r\n\r\nThe larger part of his day was \xe2\x80\x98wasted\xe2\x80\x99--as he termed it, in his own\r\nmind--in labours pertaining to his royal office. \xc2\xa0Even the two hours\r\ndevoted to certain princely pastimes and recreations were rather a\r\nburden to him than otherwise, they were so fettered by restrictions\r\nand ceremonious observances. \xc2\xa0However, he had a private hour with\r\nhis whipping-boy which he counted clear gain, since he got both\r\nentertainment and needful information out of it.\r\n\r\nThe third day of Tom Canty\xe2\x80\x99s kingship came and went much as the others\r\nhad done, but there was a lifting of his cloud in one way--he felt\r\nless uncomfortable than at first; he was getting a little used to his\r\ncircumstances and surroundings; his chains still galled, but not all the\r\ntime; he found that the presence and homage of the great afflicted and\r\nembarrassed him less and less sharply with every hour that drifted over\r\nhis head.\r\n\r\nBut for one single dread, he could have seen the fourth day approach\r\nwithout serious distress--the dining in public; it was to begin that\r\nday. There were greater matters in the programme--for on that day\r\nhe would have to preside at a council which would take his views and\r\ncommands concerning the policy to be pursued toward various foreign\r\nnations scattered far and near over the great globe; on that day, too,\r\nHertford would be formally chosen to the grand office of Lord Protector;\r\nother things of note were appointed for that fourth day, also; but to\r\nTom they were all insignificant compared with the ordeal of dining all\r\nby himself with a multitude of curious eyes fastened upon him and a\r\nmultitude of mouths whispering comments upon his performance,--and upon\r\nhis mistakes, if he should be so unlucky as to make any.\r\n\r\nStill, nothing could stop that fourth day, and so it came. \xc2\xa0It found\r\npoor Tom low-spirited and absent-minded, and this mood continued; he\r\ncould not shake it off. \xc2\xa0The ordinary duties of the morning dragged upon\r\nhis hands, and wearied him. \xc2\xa0Once more he felt the sense of captivity\r\nheavy upon him.\r\n\r\nLate in the forenoon he was in a large audience-chamber, conversing\r\nwith the Earl of Hertford and dully awaiting the striking of the hour\r\nappointed for a visit of ceremony from a considerable number of great\r\nofficials and courtiers.\r\n\r\nAfter a little while, Tom, who had wandered to a window and become\r\ninterested in the life and movement of the great highway beyond the\r\npalace gates--and not idly interested, but longing with all his heart\r\nto take part in person in its stir and freedom--saw the van of a hooting\r\nand shouting mob of disorderly men, women, and children of the lowest\r\nand poorest degree approaching from up the road.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI would I knew what \xe2\x80\x99tis about!\xe2\x80\x9d he exclaimed, with all a boy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncuriosity in such happenings.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art the King!\xe2\x80\x9d solemnly responded the Earl, with a reverence.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave I your Grace\xe2\x80\x99s leave to act?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO blithely, yes! \xc2\xa0O gladly, yes!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Tom excitedly, adding to\r\nhimself with a lively sense of satisfaction, \xe2\x80\x9cIn truth, being a king is\r\nnot all dreariness--it hath its compensations and conveniences.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Earl called a page, and sent him to the captain of the guard with\r\nthe order--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet the mob be halted, and inquiry made concerning the occasion of its\r\nmovement. \xc2\xa0By the King\xe2\x80\x99s command!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA few seconds later a long rank of the royal guards, cased in flashing\r\nsteel, filed out at the gates and formed across the highway in front\r\nof the multitude. \xc2\xa0A messenger returned, to report that the crowd were\r\nfollowing a man, a woman, and a young girl to execution for crimes\r\ncommitted against the peace and dignity of the realm.\r\n\r\nDeath--and a violent death--for these poor unfortunates! \xc2\xa0The thought\r\nwrung Tom\xe2\x80\x99s heart-strings. \xc2\xa0The spirit of compassion took control of\r\nhim, to the exclusion of all other considerations; he never thought of\r\nthe offended laws, or of the grief or loss which these three criminals\r\nhad inflicted upon their victims; he could think of nothing but the\r\nscaffold and the grisly fate hanging over the heads of the condemned.\r\n\xc2\xa0His concern made him even forget, for the moment, that he was but the\r\nfalse shadow of a king, not the substance; and before he knew it he had\r\nblurted out the command--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBring them here!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he blushed scarlet, and a sort of apology sprung to his lips; but\r\nobserving that his order had wrought no sort of surprise in the Earl or\r\nthe waiting page, he suppressed the words he was about to utter. \xc2\xa0The\r\npage, in the most matter-of-course way, made a profound obeisance\r\nand retired backwards out of the room to deliver the command. \xc2\xa0Tom\r\nexperienced a glow of pride and a renewed sense of the compensating\r\nadvantages of the kingly office. He said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cTruly it is like\r\nwhat I was used to feel when I read the old priest\xe2\x80\x99s tales, and did\r\nimagine mine own self a prince, giving law and command to all, saying\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Do this, do that,\xe2\x80\x99 whilst none durst offer let or hindrance to my\r\nwill.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow the doors swung open; one high-sounding title after another was\r\nannounced, the personages owning them followed, and the place was\r\nquickly half-filled with noble folk and finery. \xc2\xa0But Tom was hardly\r\nconscious of the presence of these people, so wrought up was he and so\r\nintensely absorbed in that other and more interesting matter. \xc2\xa0He seated\r\nhimself absently in his chair of state, and turned his eyes upon the\r\ndoor with manifestations of impatient expectancy; seeing which, the\r\ncompany forbore to trouble him, and fell to chatting a mixture of public\r\nbusiness and court gossip one with another.\r\n\r\nIn a little while the measured tread of military men was heard\r\napproaching, and the culprits entered the presence in charge of an\r\nunder-sheriff and escorted by a detail of the king\xe2\x80\x99s guard. \xc2\xa0The civil\r\nofficer knelt before Tom, then stood aside; the three doomed persons\r\nknelt, also, and remained so; the guard took position behind Tom\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nchair. \xc2\xa0Tom scanned the prisoners curiously. Something about the dress\r\nor appearance of the man had stirred a vague memory in him.  \xe2\x80\x9cMethinks\r\nI have seen this man ere now . . . but the when or the where fail\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d--Such was Tom\xe2\x80\x99s thought. Just then the man glanced quickly up and\r\nquickly dropped his face again, not being able to endure the awful port\r\nof sovereignty; but the one full glimpse of the face which Tom got was\r\nsufficient. \xc2\xa0He said to himself: \xe2\x80\x9cNow is the matter clear; this is the\r\nstranger that plucked Giles Witt out of the Thames, and saved his life,\r\nthat windy, bitter, first day of the New Year--a brave good deed--pity\r\nhe hath been doing baser ones and got himself in this sad case . . . I\r\nhave not forgot the day, neither the hour; by reason that an hour after,\r\nupon the stroke of eleven, I did get a hiding by the hand of Gammer\r\nCanty which was of so goodly and admired severity that all that\r\nwent before or followed after it were but fondlings and caresses by\r\ncomparison.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom now ordered that the woman and the girl be removed from the presence\r\nfor a little time; then addressed himself to the under-sheriff, saying--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood sir, what is this man\xe2\x80\x99s offence?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe officer knelt, and answered--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo please your Majesty, he hath taken the life of a subject by poison.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s compassion for the prisoner, and admiration of him as the daring\r\nrescuer of a drowning boy, experienced a most damaging shock.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe thing was proven upon him?\xe2\x80\x9d he asked.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMost clearly, sire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom sighed, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake him away--he hath earned his death.  \xe2\x80\x98Tis a pity, for he was a\r\nbrave heart--na--na, I mean he hath the _look_ of it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe prisoner clasped his hands together with sudden energy, and wrung\r\nthem despairingly, at the same time appealing imploringly to the \xe2\x80\x98King\xe2\x80\x99\r\nin broken and terrified phrases--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO my lord the King, an\xe2\x80\x99 thou canst pity the lost, have pity upon me! \xc2\xa0I\r\nam innocent--neither hath that wherewith I am charged been more than\r\nbut lamely proved--yet I speak not of that; the judgment is gone forth\r\nagainst me and may not suffer alteration; yet in mine extremity I beg a\r\nboon, for my doom is more than I can bear. A grace, a grace, my lord the\r\nKing! in thy royal compassion grant my prayer--give commandment that I\r\nbe hanged!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom was amazed. \xc2\xa0This was not the outcome he had looked for.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOdds my life, a strange _boon_! \xc2\xa0Was it not the fate intended thee?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO good my liege, not so! \xc2\xa0It is ordered that I be _boiled alive_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe hideous surprise of these words almost made Tom spring from his\r\nchair. \xc2\xa0As soon as he could recover his wits he cried out--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave thy wish, poor soul! an\xe2\x80\x99 thou had poisoned a hundred men thou\r\nshouldst not suffer so miserable a death.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe prisoner bowed his face to the ground and burst into passionate\r\nexpressions of gratitude--ending with--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf ever thou shouldst know misfortune--which God forefend!--may thy\r\ngoodness to me this day be remembered and requited!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom turned to the Earl of Hertford, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy lord, is it believable that there was warrant for this man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nferocious doom?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is the law, your Grace--for poisoners. \xc2\xa0In Germany coiners be boiled\r\nto death in _oil_--not cast in of a sudden, but by a rope let down into\r\nthe oil by degrees, and slowly; first the feet, then the legs, then--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO prithee no more, my lord, I cannot bear it!\xe2\x80\x9d cried Tom, covering\r\nhis eyes with his hands to shut out the picture.  \xe2\x80\x9cI beseech your good\r\nlordship that order be taken to change this law--oh, let no more poor\r\ncreatures be visited with its tortures.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Earl\xe2\x80\x99s face showed profound gratification, for he was a man of\r\nmerciful and generous impulses--a thing not very common with his class\r\nin that fierce age. \xc2\xa0He said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese your Grace\xe2\x80\x99s noble words have sealed its doom. \xc2\xa0History will\r\nremember it to the honour of your royal house.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe under-sheriff was about to remove his prisoner; Tom gave him a sign\r\nto wait; then he said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood sir, I would look into this matter further. \xc2\xa0The man has said his\r\ndeed was but lamely proved. \xc2\xa0Tell me what thou knowest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf the King\xe2\x80\x99s grace please, it did appear upon the trial that this\r\nman entered into a house in the hamlet of Islington where one lay\r\nsick--three witnesses say it was at ten of the clock in the morning, and\r\ntwo say it was some minutes later--the sick man being alone at the time,\r\nand sleeping--and presently the man came forth again and went his\r\nway. \xc2\xa0The sick man died within the hour, being torn with spasms and\r\nretchings.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDid any see the poison given? \xc2\xa0Was poison found?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarry, no, my liege.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen how doth one know there was poison given at all?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease your Majesty, the doctors testified that none die with such\r\nsymptoms but by poison.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWeighty evidence, this, in that simple age. \xc2\xa0Tom recognised its\r\nformidable nature, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe doctor knoweth his trade--belike they were right. \xc2\xa0The matter hath\r\nan ill-look for this poor man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYet was not this all, your Majesty; there is more and worse. Many\r\ntestified that a witch, since gone from the village, none know whither,\r\ndid foretell, and speak it privately in their ears, that the sick\r\nman _would die by poison_--and more, that a stranger would give it--a\r\nstranger with brown hair and clothed in a worn and common garb; and\r\nsurely this prisoner doth answer woundily to the bill. \xc2\xa0Please your\r\nMajesty to give the circumstance that solemn weight which is its due,\r\nseeing it was _foretold_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was an argument of tremendous force in that superstitious day. \xc2\xa0Tom\r\nfelt that the thing was settled; if evidence was worth anything, this\r\npoor fellow\xe2\x80\x99s guilt was proved. \xc2\xa0Still he offered the prisoner a chance,\r\nsaying--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf thou canst say aught in thy behalf, speak.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNought that will avail, my King. \xc2\xa0I am innocent, yet cannot I make\r\nit appear. \xc2\xa0I have no friends, else might I show that I was not in\r\nIslington that day; so also might I show that at that hour they name I\r\nwas above a league away, seeing I was at Wapping Old Stairs; yea more,\r\nmy King, for I could show, that whilst they say I was _taking_ life, I\r\nwas _saving_ it. \xc2\xa0A drowning boy--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeace! \xc2\xa0Sheriff, name the day the deed was done!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAt ten in the morning, or some minutes later, the first day of the New\r\nYear, most illustrious--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet the prisoner go free--it is the King\xe2\x80\x99s will!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnother blush followed this unregal outburst, and he covered his\r\nindecorum as well as he could by adding--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt enrageth me that a man should be hanged upon such idle, hare-brained\r\nevidence!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA low buzz of admiration swept through the assemblage. \xc2\xa0It was not\r\nadmiration of the decree that had been delivered by Tom, for the\r\npropriety or expediency of pardoning a convicted poisoner was a thing\r\nwhich few there would have felt justified in either admitting or\r\nadmiring--no, the admiration was for the intelligence and spirit which\r\nTom had displayed. \xc2\xa0Some of the low-voiced remarks were to this effect--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis is no mad king--he hath his wits sound.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow sanely he put his questions--how like his former natural self was\r\nthis abrupt imperious disposal of the matter!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod be thanked, his infirmity is spent! \xc2\xa0This is no weakling, but a\r\nking. \xc2\xa0He hath borne himself like to his own father.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe air being filled with applause, Tom\xe2\x80\x99s ear necessarily caught a\r\nlittle of it. \xc2\xa0The effect which this had upon him was to put him\r\ngreatly at his ease, and also to charge his system with very gratifying\r\nsensations.\r\n\r\nHowever, his juvenile curiosity soon rose superior to these pleasant\r\nthoughts and feelings; he was eager to know what sort of deadly mischief\r\nthe woman and the little girl could have been about; so, by his command,\r\nthe two terrified and sobbing creatures were brought before him.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat is it that these have done?\xe2\x80\x9d he inquired of the sheriff.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPlease your Majesty, a black crime is charged upon them, and clearly\r\nproven; wherefore the judges have decreed, according to the law, that\r\nthey be hanged. \xc2\xa0They sold themselves to the devil--such is their\r\ncrime.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom shuddered. \xc2\xa0He had been taught to abhor people who did this wicked\r\nthing. \xc2\xa0Still, he was not going to deny himself the pleasure of feeding\r\nhis curiosity for all that; so he asked--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere was this done?--and when?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOn a midnight in December, in a ruined church, your Majesty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom shuddered again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho was there present?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly these two, your grace--and _that other_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave these confessed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, not so, sire--they do deny it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen prithee, how was it known?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertain witness did see them wending thither, good your Majesty; this\r\nbred the suspicion, and dire effects have since confirmed and justified\r\nit. \xc2\xa0In particular, it is in evidence that through the wicked power so\r\nobtained, they did invoke and bring about a storm that wasted all the\r\nregion round about. \xc2\xa0Above forty witnesses have proved the storm; and\r\nsooth one might have had a thousand, for all had reason to remember it,\r\nsith all had suffered by it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCertes this is a serious matter.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0Tom turned this dark piece of\r\nscoundrelism over in his mind a while, then asked--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSuffered the woman also by the storm?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSeveral old heads among the assemblage nodded their recognition of\r\nthe wisdom of this question. \xc2\xa0The sheriff, however, saw nothing\r\nconsequential in the inquiry; he answered, with simple directness--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed did she, your Majesty, and most righteously, as all aver. Her\r\nhabitation was swept away, and herself and child left shelterless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMethinks the power to do herself so ill a turn was dearly bought. She\r\nhad been cheated, had she paid but a farthing for it; that she paid\r\nher soul, and her child\xe2\x80\x99s, argueth that she is mad; if she is mad she\r\nknoweth not what she doth, therefore sinneth not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe elderly heads nodded recognition of Tom\xe2\x80\x99s wisdom once more, and one\r\nindividual murmured, \xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 the King be mad himself, according to report,\r\nthen is it a madness of a sort that would improve the sanity of some I\r\nwot of, if by the gentle providence of God they could but catch it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat age hath the child?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Tom.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNine years, please your Majesty.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy the law of England may a child enter into covenant and sell itself,\r\nmy lord?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Tom, turning to a learned judge.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe law doth not permit a child to make or meddle in any weighty\r\nmatter, good my liege, holding that its callow wit unfitteth it to cope\r\nwith the riper wit and evil schemings of them that are its elders. \xc2\xa0The\r\n_Devil_ may buy a child, if he so choose, and the child agree thereto,\r\nbut not an Englishman--in this latter case the contract would be null\r\nand void.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt seemeth a rude unchristian thing, and ill contrived, that English\r\nlaw denieth privileges to Englishmen to waste them on the devil!\xe2\x80\x9d cried\r\nTom, with honest heat.\r\n\r\nThis novel view of the matter excited many smiles, and was stored\r\naway in many heads to be repeated about the Court as evidence of Tom\xe2\x80\x99s\r\noriginality as well as progress toward mental health.\r\n\r\nThe elder culprit had ceased from sobbing, and was hanging upon Tom\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nwords with an excited interest and a growing hope. \xc2\xa0Tom noticed this,\r\nand it strongly inclined his sympathies toward her in her perilous and\r\nunfriended situation. \xc2\xa0Presently he asked--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow wrought they to bring the storm?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_By pulling off their stockings_, sire.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis astonished Tom, and also fired his curiosity to fever heat. He\r\nsaid, eagerly--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is wonderful! \xc2\xa0Hath it always this dread effect?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlways, my liege--at least if the woman desire it, and utter the\r\nneedful words, either in her mind or with her tongue.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom turned to the woman, and said with impetuous zeal--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cExert thy power--I would see a storm!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a sudden paling of cheeks in the superstitious assemblage, and\r\na general, though unexpressed, desire to get out of the place--all of\r\nwhich was lost upon Tom, who was dead to everything but the proposed\r\ncataclysm. \xc2\xa0Seeing a puzzled and astonished look in the woman\xe2\x80\x99s face, he\r\nadded, excitedly--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNever fear--thou shalt be blameless. \xc2\xa0More--thou shalt go free--none\r\nshall touch thee. \xc2\xa0Exert thy power.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my lord the King, I have it not--I have been falsely accused.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThy fears stay thee. \xc2\xa0Be of good heart, thou shalt suffer no harm.\r\n\xc2\xa0Make a storm--it mattereth not how small a one--I require nought great\r\nor harmful, but indeed prefer the opposite--do this and thy life is\r\nspared--thou shalt go out free, with thy child, bearing the King\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npardon, and safe from hurt or malice from any in the realm.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe woman prostrated herself, and protested, with tears, that she had\r\nno power to do the miracle, else she would gladly win her child\xe2\x80\x99s life\r\nalone, and be content to lose her own, if by obedience to the King\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ncommand so precious a grace might be acquired.\r\n\r\nTom urged--the woman still adhered to her declarations. \xc2\xa0Finally he\r\nsaid--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI think the woman hath said true. \xc2\xa0An\xe2\x80\x99 _my_ mother were in her place\r\nand gifted with the devil\xe2\x80\x99s functions, she had not stayed a moment to\r\ncall her storms and lay the whole land in ruins, if the saving of my\r\nforfeit life were the price she got! \xc2\xa0It is argument that other\r\nmothers are made in like mould. \xc2\xa0Thou art free, goodwife--thou and thy\r\nchild--for I do think thee innocent. \xc2\xa0_Now_ thou\xe2\x80\x99st nought to fear,\r\nbeing pardoned--pull off thy stockings!--an\xe2\x80\x99 thou canst make me a storm,\r\nthou shalt be rich!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe redeemed creature was loud in her gratitude, and proceeded to\r\nobey, whilst Tom looked on with eager expectancy, a little marred\r\nby apprehension; the courtiers at the same time manifesting decided\r\ndiscomfort and uneasiness. \xc2\xa0The woman stripped her own feet and her\r\nlittle girl\xe2\x80\x99s also, and plainly did her best to reward the King\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngenerosity with an earthquake, but it was all a failure and a\r\ndisappointment. \xc2\xa0Tom sighed, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, good soul, trouble thyself no further, thy power is departed\r\nout of thee. \xc2\xa0Go thy way in peace; and if it return to thee at any time,\r\nforget me not, but fetch me a storm.\xe2\x80\x9d {13}\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVI. The State Dinner.\r\n\r\nThe dinner hour drew near--yet strangely enough, the thought brought\r\nbut slight discomfort to Tom, and hardly any terror. \xc2\xa0The morning\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nexperiences had wonderfully built up his confidence; the poor little\r\nash-cat was already more wonted to his strange garret, after four\r\ndays\xe2\x80\x99 habit, than a mature person could have become in a full month. \xc2\xa0A\r\nchild\xe2\x80\x99s facility in accommodating itself to circumstances was never more\r\nstrikingly illustrated.\r\n\r\nLet us privileged ones hurry to the great banqueting-room and have a\r\nglance at matters there whilst Tom is being made ready for the\r\nimposing occasion. \xc2\xa0It is a spacious apartment, with gilded pillars\r\nand pilasters, and pictured walls and ceilings. \xc2\xa0At the door stand tall\r\nguards, as rigid as statues, dressed in rich and picturesque costumes,\r\nand bearing halberds. \xc2\xa0In a high gallery which runs all around the place\r\nis a band of musicians and a packed company of citizens of both sexes,\r\nin brilliant attire. \xc2\xa0In the centre of the room, upon a raised platform,\r\nis Tom\xe2\x80\x99s table. Now let the ancient chronicler speak:\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA gentleman enters the room bearing a rod, and along with him another\r\nbearing a tablecloth, which, after they have both kneeled three times\r\nwith the utmost veneration, he spreads upon the table, and after\r\nkneeling again they both retire; then come two others, one with the rod\r\nagain, the other with a salt-cellar, a plate, and bread; when they have\r\nkneeled as the others had done, and placed what was brought upon the\r\ntable, they too retire with the same ceremonies performed by the first;\r\nat last come two nobles, richly clothed, one bearing a tasting-knife,\r\nwho, after prostrating themselves three times in the most graceful\r\nmanner, approach and rub the table with bread and salt, with as much awe\r\nas if the King had been present.\xe2\x80\x9d {6}\r\n\r\nSo end the solemn preliminaries. \xc2\xa0Now, far down the echoing corridors\r\nwe hear a bugle-blast, and the indistinct cry, \xe2\x80\x9cPlace for the King!\r\n\xc2\xa0Way for the King\xe2\x80\x99s most excellent majesty!\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0These sounds are momently\r\nrepeated--they grow nearer and nearer--and presently, almost in our\r\nfaces, the martial note peals and the cry rings out, \xe2\x80\x9cWay for the King!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n \xc2\xa0At this instant the shining pageant appears, and files in at the door,\r\nwith a measured march. Let the chronicler speak again:--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFirst come Gentlemen, Barons, Earls, Knights of the Garter, all richly\r\ndressed and bareheaded; next comes the Chancellor, between two, one of\r\nwhich carries the royal sceptre, the other the Sword of State in a red\r\nscabbard, studded with golden fleurs-de-lis, the point upwards; next\r\ncomes the King himself--whom, upon his appearing, twelve trumpets and\r\nmany drums salute with a great burst of welcome, whilst all in the\r\ngalleries rise in their places, crying \xe2\x80\x98God save the King!\xe2\x80\x99 \xc2\xa0After him\r\ncome nobles attached to his person, and on his right and left march his\r\nguard of honour, his fifty Gentlemen Pensioners, with gilt battle-axes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis was all fine and pleasant. \xc2\xa0Tom\xe2\x80\x99s pulse beat high, and a glad light\r\nwas in his eye. \xc2\xa0He bore himself right gracefully, and all the more\r\nso because he was not thinking of how he was doing it, his mind being\r\ncharmed and occupied with the blithe sights and sounds about him--and\r\nbesides, nobody can be very ungraceful in nicely-fitting beautiful\r\nclothes after he has grown a little used to them--especially if he is\r\nfor the moment unconscious of them. Tom remembered his instructions, and\r\nacknowledged his greeting with a slight inclination of his plumed head,\r\nand a courteous \xe2\x80\x9cI thank ye, my good people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe seated himself at table, without removing his cap; and did it without\r\nthe least embarrassment; for to eat with one\xe2\x80\x99s cap on was the one\r\nsolitary royal custom upon which the kings and the Cantys met upon\r\ncommon ground, neither party having any advantage over the other in the\r\nmatter of old familiarity with it. \xc2\xa0The pageant broke up and grouped\r\nitself picturesquely, and remained bareheaded.\r\n\r\nNow to the sound of gay music the Yeomen of the Guard entered,--\xe2\x80\x9cthe\r\ntallest and mightiest men in England, they being carefully selected in\r\nthis regard\xe2\x80\x9d--but we will let the chronicler tell about it:--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe Yeomen of the Guard entered, bareheaded, clothed in scarlet, with\r\ngolden roses upon their backs; and these went and came, bringing in each\r\nturn a course of dishes, served in plate. \xc2\xa0These dishes were received\r\nby a gentleman in the same order they were brought, and placed upon\r\nthe table, while the taster gave to each guard a mouthful to eat of the\r\nparticular dish he had brought, for fear of any poison.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nTom made a good dinner, notwithstanding he was conscious that hundreds\r\nof eyes followed each morsel to his mouth and watched him eat it with an\r\ninterest which could not have been more intense if it had been a deadly\r\nexplosive and was expected to blow him up and scatter him all about\r\nthe place. \xc2\xa0He was careful not to hurry, and equally careful not to do\r\nanything whatever for himself, but wait till the proper official knelt\r\ndown and did it for him. \xc2\xa0He got through without a mistake--flawless and\r\nprecious triumph.\r\n\r\nWhen the meal was over at last and he marched away in the midst of his\r\nbright pageant, with the happy noises in his ears of blaring bugles,\r\nrolling drums, and thundering acclamations, he felt that if he had seen\r\nthe worst of dining in public it was an ordeal which he would be glad\r\nto endure several times a day if by that means he could but buy himself\r\nfree from some of the more formidable requirements of his royal office.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVII. Foo-foo the First.\r\n\r\nMiles Hendon hurried along toward the Southwark end of the bridge,\r\nkeeping a sharp look-out for the persons he sought, and hoping and\r\nexpecting to overtake them presently. \xc2\xa0He was disappointed in this,\r\nhowever. \xc2\xa0By asking questions, he was enabled to track them part of the\r\nway through Southwark; then all traces ceased, and he was perplexed as\r\nto how to proceed. \xc2\xa0Still, he continued his efforts as best he\r\ncould during the rest of the day. \xc2\xa0Nightfall found him leg-weary,\r\nhalf-famished, and his desire as far from accomplishment as ever; so\r\nhe supped at the Tabard Inn and went to bed, resolved to make an early\r\nstart in the morning, and give the town an exhaustive search. \xc2\xa0As he lay\r\nthinking and planning, he presently began to reason thus: \xc2\xa0The boy would\r\nescape from the ruffian, his reputed father, if possible; would he go\r\nback to London and seek his former haunts? \xc2\xa0No, he would not do that,\r\nhe would avoid recapture. What, then, would he do? \xc2\xa0Never having had a\r\nfriend in the world, or a protector, until he met Miles Hendon, he would\r\nnaturally try to find that friend again, provided the effort did not\r\nrequire him to go toward London and danger. \xc2\xa0He would strike for Hendon\r\nHall, that is what he would do, for he knew Hendon was homeward bound\r\nand there he might expect to find him. \xc2\xa0Yes, the case was plain to\r\nHendon--he must lose no more time in Southwark, but move at once through\r\nKent, toward Monk\xe2\x80\x99s Holm, searching the wood and inquiring as he went.\r\n\xc2\xa0Let us return to the vanished little King now.\r\n\r\nThe ruffian whom the waiter at the inn on the bridge saw \xe2\x80\x98about to join\xe2\x80\x99\r\nthe youth and the King did not exactly join them, but fell in close\r\nbehind them and followed their steps. \xc2\xa0He said nothing. His left arm was\r\nin a sling, and he wore a large green patch over his left eye; he limped\r\nslightly, and used an oaken staff as a support. \xc2\xa0The youth led the King\r\na crooked course through Southwark, and by-and-by struck into the\r\nhigh road beyond. \xc2\xa0The King was irritated, now, and said he would stop\r\nhere--it was Hendon\xe2\x80\x99s place to come to him, not his to go to Hendon. \xc2\xa0He\r\nwould not endure such insolence; he would stop where he was. \xc2\xa0The youth\r\nsaid--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou\xe2\x80\x99lt tarry here, and thy friend lying wounded in the wood yonder?\r\n\xc2\xa0So be it, then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King\xe2\x80\x99s manner changed at once. \xc2\xa0He cried out--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWounded? \xc2\xa0And who hath dared to do it? \xc2\xa0But that is apart; lead on,\r\nlead on! \xc2\xa0Faster, sirrah! \xc2\xa0Art shod with lead? \xc2\xa0Wounded, is he? \xc2\xa0Now\r\nthough the doer of it be a duke\xe2\x80\x99s son he shall rue it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was some distance to the wood, but the space was speedily traversed.\r\nThe youth looked about him, discovered a bough sticking in the ground,\r\nwith a small bit of rag tied to it, then led the way into the forest,\r\nwatching for similar boughs and finding them at intervals; they were\r\nevidently guides to the point he was aiming at. \xc2\xa0By-and-by an open place\r\nwas reached, where were the charred remains of a farm-house, and near\r\nthem a barn which was falling to ruin and decay. \xc2\xa0There was no sign of\r\nlife anywhere, and utter silence prevailed. \xc2\xa0The youth entered the barn,\r\nthe King following eagerly upon his heels. \xc2\xa0No one there! The King shot\r\na surprised and suspicious glance at the youth, and asked--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhere is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA mocking laugh was his answer. \xc2\xa0The King was in a rage in a moment; he\r\nseized a billet of wood and was in the act of charging upon the youth\r\nwhen another mocking laugh fell upon his ear. \xc2\xa0It was from the lame\r\nruffian who had been following at a distance. The King turned and said\r\nangrily--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho art thou? \xc2\xa0What is thy business here?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLeave thy foolery,\xe2\x80\x9d said the man, \xe2\x80\x9cand quiet thyself. \xc2\xa0My disguise is\r\nnone so good that thou canst pretend thou knowest not thy father through\r\nit.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art not my father. \xc2\xa0I know thee not. \xc2\xa0I am the King. \xc2\xa0If thou hast\r\nhid my servant, find him for me, or thou shalt sup sorrow for what thou\r\nhast done.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nJohn Canty replied, in a stern and measured voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is plain thou art mad, and I am loath to punish thee; \xc2\xa0but if thou\r\nprovoke me, I must. \xc2\xa0Thy prating doth no harm here, where there are\r\nno ears that need to mind thy follies; yet it is well to practise thy\r\ntongue to wary speech, that it may do no hurt when our quarters change.\r\n\xc2\xa0I have done a murder, and may not tarry at home--neither shalt thou,\r\nseeing I need thy service. \xc2\xa0My name is changed, for wise reasons; it is\r\nHobbs--John Hobbs; thine is Jack--charge thy memory accordingly. \xc2\xa0Now,\r\nthen, speak. \xc2\xa0Where is thy mother? \xc2\xa0Where are thy sisters? \xc2\xa0They came\r\nnot to the place appointed--knowest thou whither they went?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King answered sullenly--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrouble me not with these riddles. \xc2\xa0My mother is dead; my sisters are\r\nin the palace.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe youth near by burst into a derisive laugh, and the King would have\r\nassaulted him, but Canty--or Hobbs, as he now called himself--prevented\r\nhim, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeace, Hugo, vex him not; his mind is astray, and thy ways fret him.\r\nSit thee down, Jack, and quiet thyself; thou shalt have a morsel to eat,\r\nanon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHobbs and Hugo fell to talking together, in low voices, and the King\r\nremoved himself as far as he could from their disagreeable company.\r\n\xc2\xa0He withdrew into the twilight of the farther end of the barn, where\r\nhe found the earthen floor bedded a foot deep with straw. \xc2\xa0He lay down\r\nhere, drew straw over himself in lieu of blankets, and was soon absorbed\r\nin thinking. \xc2\xa0He had many griefs, but the minor ones were swept almost\r\ninto forgetfulness by the supreme one, the loss of his father. \xc2\xa0To\r\nthe rest of the world the name of Henry VIII. brought a shiver, and\r\nsuggested an ogre whose nostrils breathed destruction and whose hand\r\ndealt scourgings and death; but to this boy the name brought only\r\nsensations of pleasure; the figure it invoked wore a countenance that\r\nwas all gentleness and affection. \xc2\xa0He called to mind a long succession\r\nof loving passages between his father and himself, and dwelt fondly upon\r\nthem, his unstinted tears attesting how deep and real was the grief that\r\npossessed his heart. As the afternoon wasted away, the lad, wearied with\r\nhis troubles, sank gradually into a tranquil and healing slumber.\r\n\r\nAfter a considerable time--he could not tell how long--his senses\r\nstruggled to a half-consciousness, and as he lay with closed eyes\r\nvaguely wondering where he was and what had been happening, he noted a\r\nmurmurous sound, the sullen beating of rain upon the roof. A snug sense\r\nof comfort stole over him, which was rudely broken, the next moment,\r\nby a chorus of piping cackles and coarse laughter. \xc2\xa0It startled him\r\ndisagreeably, and he unmuffled his head to see whence this interruption\r\nproceeded. \xc2\xa0A grim and unsightly picture met his eye. \xc2\xa0A bright fire was\r\nburning in the middle of the floor, at the other end of the barn; and\r\naround it, and lit weirdly up by the red glare, lolled and sprawled the\r\nmotliest company of tattered gutter-scum and ruffians, of both sexes, he\r\nhad ever read or dreamed of. \xc2\xa0There were huge stalwart men, brown\r\nwith exposure, long-haired, and clothed in fantastic rags; there were\r\nmiddle-sized youths, of truculent countenance, and similarly clad; there\r\nwere blind mendicants, with patched or bandaged eyes; crippled ones,\r\nwith wooden legs and crutches; diseased ones, with running sores peeping\r\nfrom ineffectual wrappings; there was a villain-looking pedlar with\r\nhis pack; a knife-grinder, a tinker, and a barber-surgeon, with the\r\nimplements of their trades; some of the females were hardly-grown girls,\r\nsome were at prime, some were old and wrinkled hags, and all were loud,\r\nbrazen, foul-mouthed; and all soiled and slatternly; there were three\r\nsore-faced babies; there were a couple of starveling curs, with strings\r\nabout their necks, whose office was to lead the blind.\r\n\r\nThe night was come, the gang had just finished feasting, an orgy was\r\nbeginning; the can of liquor was passing from mouth to mouth. A general\r\ncry broke forth--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA song! a song from the Bat and Dick and Dot-and-go-One!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOne of the blind men got up, and made ready by casting aside the patches\r\nthat sheltered his excellent eyes, and the pathetic placard which\r\nrecited the cause of his calamity. \xc2\xa0Dot-and-go-One disencumbered himself\r\nof his timber leg and took his place, upon sound and healthy limbs,\r\nbeside his fellow-rascal; then they roared out a rollicking ditty,\r\nand were reinforced by the whole crew, at the end of each stanza, in\r\na rousing chorus. \xc2\xa0By the time the last stanza was reached, the\r\nhalf-drunken enthusiasm had risen to such a pitch, that everybody joined\r\nin and sang it clear through from the beginning, producing a volume of\r\nvillainous sound that made the rafters quake. \xc2\xa0These were the inspiring\r\nwords:--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Bien Darkman\xe2\x80\x99s then, Bouse Mort and Ken, The bien Coves bings awast, On\r\nChates to trine by Rome Coves dine For his long lib at last. Bing\xe2\x80\x99d out\r\nbien Morts and toure, and toure, Bing out of the Rome vile bine, And\r\ntoure the Cove that cloy\xe2\x80\x99d your duds, Upon the Chates to trine.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n(From\xe2\x80\x99The English Rogue.\xe2\x80\x99 London, 1665.)\r\n\r\nConversation followed; not in the thieves\xe2\x80\x99 dialect of the song, for that\r\nwas only used in talk when unfriendly ears might be listening. \xc2\xa0In the\r\ncourse of it, it appeared that \xe2\x80\x98John Hobbs\xe2\x80\x99 was not altogether a new\r\nrecruit, but had trained in the gang at some former time. \xc2\xa0His later\r\nhistory was called for, and when he said he had \xe2\x80\x98accidentally\xe2\x80\x99 killed a\r\nman, considerable satisfaction was expressed; when he added that the\r\nman was a priest, he was roundly applauded, and had to take a drink with\r\neverybody. \xc2\xa0Old acquaintances welcomed him joyously, and new ones were\r\nproud to shake him by the hand. \xc2\xa0He was asked why he had \xe2\x80\x99tarried away\r\nso many months.\xe2\x80\x99 \xc2\xa0He answered--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLondon is better than the country, and safer, these late years, the\r\nlaws be so bitter and so diligently enforced. \xc2\xa0An\xe2\x80\x99 I had not had that\r\naccident, I had stayed there. \xc2\xa0I had resolved to stay, and never more\r\nventure country-wards--but the accident has ended that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe inquired how many persons the gang numbered now. \xc2\xa0The \xe2\x80\x98ruffler,\xe2\x80\x99 or\r\nchief, answered--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFive and twenty sturdy budges, bulks, files, clapperdogeons and\r\nmaunders, counting the dells and doxies and other morts. {7} \xc2\xa0Most are\r\nhere, the rest are wandering eastward, along the winter lay. We follow\r\nat dawn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not see the Wen among the honest folk about me. \xc2\xa0Where may he be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPoor lad, his diet is brimstone, now, and over hot for a delicate\r\ntaste. He was killed in a brawl, somewhere about midsummer.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI sorrow to hear that; the Wen was a capable man, and brave.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat was he, truly. \xc2\xa0Black Bess, his dell, is of us yet, but absent on\r\nthe eastward tramp; a fine lass, of nice ways and orderly conduct, none\r\never seeing her drunk above four days in the seven.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was ever strict--I remember it well--a goodly wench and worthy\r\nall commendation. \xc2\xa0Her mother was more free and less particular; a\r\ntroublesome and ugly-tempered beldame, but furnished with a wit above\r\nthe common.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWe lost her through it. \xc2\xa0Her gift of palmistry and other sorts of\r\nfortune-telling begot for her at last a witch\xe2\x80\x99s name and fame. The\r\nlaw roasted her to death at a slow fire. \xc2\xa0It did touch me to a sort of\r\ntenderness to see the gallant way she met her lot--cursing and reviling\r\nall the crowd that gaped and gazed around her, whilst the flames licked\r\nupward toward her face and catched her thin locks and crackled about\r\nher old gray head--cursing them! why an\xe2\x80\x99 thou should\xe2\x80\x99st live a thousand\r\nyears thoud\xe2\x80\x99st never hear so masterful a cursing. \xc2\xa0Alack, her art died\r\nwith her. \xc2\xa0There be base and weakling imitations left, but no true\r\nblasphemy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe Ruffler sighed; the listeners sighed in sympathy; a general\r\ndepression fell upon the company for a moment, for even hardened\r\noutcasts like these are not wholly dead to sentiment, but are able to\r\nfeel a fleeting sense of loss and affliction at wide intervals and\r\nunder peculiarly favouring circumstances--as in cases like to this, for\r\ninstance, when genius and culture depart and leave no heir. \xc2\xa0However, a\r\ndeep drink all round soon restored the spirits of the mourners.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave any others of our friends fared hardly?\xe2\x80\x9d asked Hobbs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSome--yes. \xc2\xa0Particularly new comers--such as small husbandmen turned\r\nshiftless and hungry upon the world because their farms were taken from\r\nthem to be changed to sheep ranges. \xc2\xa0They begged, and were whipped at\r\nthe cart\xe2\x80\x99s tail, naked from the girdle up, till the blood ran; then set\r\nin the stocks to be pelted; they begged again, were whipped again, and\r\ndeprived of an ear; they begged a third time--poor devils, what else\r\ncould they do?--and were branded on the cheek with a red-hot iron, then\r\nsold for slaves; they ran away, were hunted down, and hanged.  \xe2\x80\x98Tis\r\na brief tale, and quickly told. \xc2\xa0Others of us have fared less hardly.\r\nStand forth, Yokel, Burns, and Hodge--show your adornments!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThese stood up and stripped away some of their rags, exposing their\r\nbacks, criss-crossed with ropy old welts left by the lash; one turned\r\nup his hair and showed the place where a left ear had once been; another\r\nshowed a brand upon his shoulder--the letter V--and a mutilated ear; the\r\nthird said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am Yokel, once a farmer and prosperous, with loving wife and\r\nkids--now am I somewhat different in estate and calling; and the wife\r\nand kids are gone; mayhap they are in heaven, mayhap in--in the other\r\nplace--but the kindly God be thanked, they bide no more in _England_!\r\n\xc2\xa0My good old blameless mother strove to earn bread by nursing the sick;\r\none of these died, the doctors knew not how, so my mother was burnt for\r\na witch, whilst my babes looked on and wailed. \xc2\xa0English law!--up,\r\nall, with your cups!--now all together and with a cheer!--drink to the\r\nmerciful English law that delivered _her_ from the English hell! \xc2\xa0Thank\r\nyou, mates, one and all. \xc2\xa0I begged, from house to house--I and the\r\nwife--bearing with us the hungry kids--but it was crime to be hungry in\r\nEngland--so they stripped us and lashed us through three towns. \xc2\xa0Drink\r\nye all again to the merciful English law!--for its lash drank deep of my\r\nMary\xe2\x80\x99s blood and its blessed deliverance came quick. \xc2\xa0She lies there, in\r\nthe potter\xe2\x80\x99s field, safe from all harms. \xc2\xa0And the kids--well, whilst\r\nthe law lashed me from town to town, they starved. Drink, lads--only\r\na drop--a drop to the poor kids, that never did any creature harm.\r\n\xc2\xa0I begged again--begged, for a crust, and got the stocks and lost an\r\near--see, here bides the stump; I begged again, and here is the stump\r\nof the other to keep me minded of it. And still I begged again, and was\r\nsold for a slave--here on my cheek under this stain, if I washed it off,\r\nye might see the red S the branding-iron left there! \xc2\xa0A _slave_! \xc2\xa0Do\r\nyou understand that word? \xc2\xa0An English _slave_!--that is he that stands\r\nbefore ye. \xc2\xa0I have run from my master, and when I am found--the heavy\r\ncurse of heaven fall on the law of the land that hath commanded it!--I\r\nshall hang!\xe2\x80\x9d {1}\r\n\r\nA ringing voice came through the murky air--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou shalt _not_!--and this day the end of that law is come!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll turned, and saw the fantastic figure of the little King approaching\r\nhurriedly; as it emerged into the light and was clearly revealed, a\r\ngeneral explosion of inquiries broke out--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho is it? \xc2\xa0_What_ is it? \xc2\xa0Who art thou, manikin?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boy stood unconfused in the midst of all those surprised and\r\nquestioning eyes, and answered with princely dignity--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am Edward, King of England.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA wild burst of laughter followed, partly of derision and partly of\r\ndelight in the excellence of the joke. \xc2\xa0The King was stung. \xc2\xa0He said\r\nsharply--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe mannerless vagrants, is this your recognition of the royal boon I\r\nhave promised?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe said more, with angry voice and excited gesture, but it was lost in\r\na whirlwind of laughter and mocking exclamations.  \xe2\x80\x98John Hobbs\xe2\x80\x99 made\r\nseveral attempts to make himself heard above the din, and at last\r\nsucceeded--saying--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMates, he is my son, a dreamer, a fool, and stark mad--mind him not--he\r\nthinketh he _is_ the King.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI _am_ the King,\xe2\x80\x9d said Edward, turning toward him, \xe2\x80\x9cas thou shalt know\r\nto thy cost, in good time. \xc2\xa0Thou hast confessed a murder--thou shalt\r\nswing for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Thou\xe2\x80\x99lt_ betray me?--_thou_? \xc2\xa0An\xe2\x80\x99 I get my hands upon thee--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTut-tut!\xe2\x80\x9d said the burley Ruffler, interposing in time to save the\r\nKing, and emphasising this service by knocking Hobbs down with his fist,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9chast respect for neither Kings _nor_ Rufflers? \xc2\xa0An\xe2\x80\x99 thou insult my\r\npresence so again, I\xe2\x80\x99ll hang thee up myself.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0Then he said to his\r\nMajesty, \xe2\x80\x9cThou must make no threats against thy mates, lad; and thou\r\nmust guard thy tongue from saying evil of them elsewhere. \xc2\xa0_Be king_, if\r\nit please thy mad humour, but be not harmful in it. \xc2\xa0Sink the title thou\r\nhast uttered--\xe2\x80\x98tis treason; we be bad men in some few trifling ways, but\r\nnone among us is so base as to be traitor to his King; we be loving\r\nand loyal hearts, in that regard. \xc2\xa0Note if I speak truth. \xc2\xa0Now--all\r\ntogether:  \xe2\x80\x98Long live Edward, King of England!\xe2\x80\x99\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLONG LIVE EDWARD, KING OF ENGLAND!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe response came with such a thundergust from the motley crew that the\r\ncrazy building vibrated to the sound. \xc2\xa0The little King\xe2\x80\x99s face lighted\r\nwith pleasure for an instant, and he slightly inclined his head, and\r\nsaid with grave simplicity--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thank you, my good people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis unexpected result threw the company into convulsions of merriment.\r\nWhen something like quiet was presently come again, the Ruffler said,\r\nfirmly, but with an accent of good nature--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDrop it, boy, \xe2\x80\x99tis not wise, nor well. \xc2\xa0Humour thy fancy, if thou must,\r\nbut choose some other title.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA tinker shrieked out a suggestion--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFoo-foo the First, King of the Mooncalves!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe title \xe2\x80\x99took,\xe2\x80\x99 at once, every throat responded, and a roaring shout\r\nwent up, of--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLong live Foo-foo the First, King of the Mooncalves!\xe2\x80\x9d followed by\r\nhootings, cat-calls, and peals of laughter.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHale him forth, and crown him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRobe him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSceptre him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThrone him!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThese and twenty other cries broke out at once! and almost before the\r\npoor little victim could draw a breath he was crowned with a tin basin,\r\nrobed in a tattered blanket, throned upon a barrel, and sceptred with\r\nthe tinker\xe2\x80\x99s soldering-iron. \xc2\xa0Then all flung themselves upon their\r\nknees about him and sent up a chorus of ironical wailings, and mocking\r\nsupplications, whilst they swabbed their eyes with their soiled and\r\nragged sleeves and aprons--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBe gracious to us, O sweet King!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTrample not upon thy beseeching worms, O noble Majesty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPity thy slaves, and comfort them with a royal kick!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCheer us and warm us with thy gracious rays, O flaming sun of\r\nsovereignty!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSanctify the ground with the touch of thy foot, that we may eat the\r\ndirt and be ennobled!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDeign to spit upon us, O Sire, that our children\xe2\x80\x99s children may tell of\r\nthy princely condescension, and be proud and happy for ever!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the humorous tinker made the \xe2\x80\x98hit\xe2\x80\x99 of the evening and carried off\r\nthe honours. \xc2\xa0Kneeling, he pretended to kiss the King\xe2\x80\x99s foot, and was\r\nindignantly spurned; whereupon he went about begging for a rag to paste\r\nover the place upon his face which had been touched by the foot, saying\r\nit must be preserved from contact with the vulgar air, and that he\r\nshould make his fortune by going on the highway and exposing it to\r\nview at the rate of a hundred shillings a sight. \xc2\xa0He made himself so\r\nkillingly funny that he was the envy and admiration of the whole mangy\r\nrabble.\r\n\r\nTears of shame and indignation stood in the little monarch\xe2\x80\x99s eyes; and\r\nthe thought in his heart was, \xe2\x80\x9cHad I offered them a deep wrong they\r\ncould not be more cruel--yet have I proffered nought but to do them a\r\nkindness--and it is thus they use me for it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XVIII. The Prince with the Tramps.\r\n\r\nThe troop of vagabonds turned out at early dawn, and set forward on\r\ntheir march. \xc2\xa0There was a lowering sky overhead, sloppy ground under\r\nfoot, and a winter chill in the air. \xc2\xa0All gaiety was gone from the\r\ncompany; some were sullen and silent, some were irritable and petulant,\r\nnone were gentle-humoured, all were thirsty.\r\n\r\nThe Ruffler put \xe2\x80\x98Jack\xe2\x80\x99 in Hugo\xe2\x80\x99s charge, with some brief instructions,\r\nand commanded John Canty to keep away from him and let him alone; he\r\nalso warned Hugo not to be too rough with the lad.\r\n\r\nAfter a while the weather grew milder, and the clouds lifted somewhat.\r\nThe troop ceased to shiver, and their spirits began to improve. \xc2\xa0They\r\ngrew more and more cheerful, and finally began to chaff each other and\r\ninsult passengers along the highway. \xc2\xa0This showed that they were awaking\r\nto an appreciation of life and its joys once more. \xc2\xa0The dread in which\r\ntheir sort was held was apparent in the fact that everybody gave them\r\nthe road, and took their ribald insolences meekly, without venturing\r\nto talk back. They snatched linen from the hedges, occasionally in full\r\nview of the owners, who made no protest, but only seemed grateful that\r\nthey did not take the hedges, too.\r\n\r\nBy-and-by they invaded a small farmhouse and made themselves at home\r\nwhile the trembling farmer and his people swept the larder clean to\r\nfurnish a breakfast for them. \xc2\xa0They chucked the housewife and her\r\ndaughters under the chin whilst receiving the food from their hands, and\r\nmade coarse jests about them, accompanied with insulting epithets and\r\nbursts of horse-laughter. \xc2\xa0They threw bones and vegetables at the farmer\r\nand his sons, kept them dodging all the time, and applauded uproariously\r\nwhen a good hit was made. They ended by buttering the head of one of\r\nthe daughters who resented some of their familiarities. \xc2\xa0When they took\r\ntheir leave they threatened to come back and burn the house over the\r\nheads of the family if any report of their doings got to the ears of the\r\nauthorities.\r\n\r\nAbout noon, after a long and weary tramp, the gang came to a halt behind\r\na hedge on the outskirts of a considerable village. \xc2\xa0An hour was allowed\r\nfor rest, then the crew scattered themselves abroad to enter the village\r\nat different points to ply their various trades--\xe2\x80\x98Jack\xe2\x80\x99 was sent with\r\nHugo. \xc2\xa0They wandered hither and thither for some time, Hugo watching\r\nfor opportunities to do a stroke of business, but finding none--so he\r\nfinally said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see nought to steal; it is a paltry place. \xc2\xa0Wherefore we will beg.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_We_, forsooth! \xc2\xa0Follow thy trade--it befits thee. \xc2\xa0But _I_ will not\r\nbeg.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou\xe2\x80\x99lt not beg!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Hugo, eyeing the King with surprise.\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPrithee, since when hast thou reformed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat dost thou mean?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMean? \xc2\xa0Hast thou not begged the streets of London all thy life?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI? \xc2\xa0Thou idiot!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSpare thy compliments--thy stock will last the longer. \xc2\xa0Thy father says\r\nthou hast begged all thy days. \xc2\xa0Mayhap he lied. Peradventure you will\r\neven make so bold as to _say_ he lied,\xe2\x80\x9d scoffed Hugo.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHim _you_ call my father? \xc2\xa0Yes, he lied.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cCome, play not thy merry game of madman so far, mate; use it for thy\r\namusement, not thy hurt. \xc2\xa0An\xe2\x80\x99 I tell him this, he will scorch thee\r\nfinely for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSave thyself the trouble. \xc2\xa0I will tell him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI like thy spirit, I do in truth; but I do not admire thy judgment.\r\nBone-rackings and bastings be plenty enow in this life, without going\r\nout of one\xe2\x80\x99s way to invite them. \xc2\xa0But a truce to these matters; _I_\r\nbelieve your father. \xc2\xa0I doubt not he can lie; I doubt not he _doth_\r\nlie, upon occasion, for the best of us do that; but there is no occasion\r\nhere. \xc2\xa0A wise man does not waste so good a commodity as lying for\r\nnought. \xc2\xa0But come; sith it is thy humour to give over begging,\r\nwherewithal shall we busy ourselves? \xc2\xa0With robbing kitchens?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King said, impatiently--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHave done with this folly--you weary me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHugo replied, with temper--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow harkee, mate; you will not beg, you will not rob; so be it. But I\r\nwill tell you what you _will_ do. \xc2\xa0You will play decoy whilst _I_ beg.\r\nRefuse, an\xe2\x80\x99 you think you may venture!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King was about to reply contemptuously, when Hugo said,\r\ninterrupting--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeace! \xc2\xa0Here comes one with a kindly face. \xc2\xa0Now will I fall down in\r\na fit. \xc2\xa0When the stranger runs to me, set you up a wail, and fall upon\r\nyour knees, seeming to weep; then cry out as all the devils of misery\r\nwere in your belly, and say, \xe2\x80\x98Oh, sir, it is my poor afflicted brother,\r\nand we be friendless; o\xe2\x80\x99 God\xe2\x80\x99s name cast through your merciful eyes one\r\npitiful look upon a sick, forsaken, and most miserable wretch; bestow\r\none little penny out of thy riches upon one smitten of God and ready\r\nto perish!\xe2\x80\x99--and mind you, keep you _on_ wailing, and abate not till we\r\nbilk him of his penny, else shall you rue it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen immediately Hugo began to moan, and groan, and roll his eyes, and\r\nreel and totter about; and when the stranger was close at hand, down he\r\nsprawled before him, with a shriek, and began to writhe and wallow in\r\nthe dirt, in seeming agony.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO, dear, O dear!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the benevolent stranger, \xe2\x80\x9cO poor soul, poor\r\nsoul, how he doth suffer! \xc2\xa0There--let me help thee up.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO noble sir, forbear, and God love you for a princely gentleman--but it\r\ngiveth me cruel pain to touch me when I am taken so. \xc2\xa0My brother there\r\nwill tell your worship how I am racked with anguish when these fits be\r\nupon me. \xc2\xa0A penny, dear sir, a penny, to buy a little food;
