then leave\r\nme to my sorrows.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA penny! thou shalt have three, thou hapless creature,\xe2\x80\x9d--and he fumbled\r\nin his pocket with nervous haste and got them out. \xe2\x80\x9cThere, poor lad,\r\ntake them and most welcome. \xc2\xa0Now come hither, my boy, and help me carry\r\nthy stricken brother to yon house, where--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am not his brother,\xe2\x80\x9d said the King, interrupting.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! not his brother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, hear him!\xe2\x80\x9d groaned Hugo, then privately ground his teeth. \xe2\x80\x9cHe\r\ndenies his own brother--and he with one foot in the grave!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBoy, thou art indeed hard of heart, if this is thy brother. \xc2\xa0For\r\nshame!--and he scarce able to move hand or foot. \xc2\xa0If he is not thy\r\nbrother, who is he, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA beggar and a thief! \xc2\xa0He has got your money and has picked your pocket\r\nlikewise. \xc2\xa0An\xe2\x80\x99 thou would\xe2\x80\x99st do a healing miracle, lay thy staff over\r\nhis shoulders and trust Providence for the rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Hugo did not tarry for the miracle. \xc2\xa0In a moment he was up and off\r\nlike the wind, the gentleman following after and raising the hue and cry\r\nlustily as he went. \xc2\xa0The King, breathing deep gratitude to Heaven for\r\nhis own release, fled in the opposite direction, and did not slacken\r\nhis pace until he was out of harm\xe2\x80\x99s reach. \xc2\xa0He took the first road that\r\noffered, and soon put the village behind him. \xc2\xa0He hurried along, as\r\nbriskly as he could, during several hours, keeping a nervous watch over\r\nhis shoulder for pursuit; but his fears left him at last, and a grateful\r\nsense of security took their place. \xc2\xa0He recognised, now, that he was\r\nhungry, and also very tired. \xc2\xa0So he halted at a farmhouse; but when\r\nhe was about to speak, he was cut short and driven rudely away. \xc2\xa0His\r\nclothes were against him.\r\n\r\nHe wandered on, wounded and indignant, and was resolved to put himself\r\nin the way of like treatment no more. \xc2\xa0But hunger is pride\xe2\x80\x99s master; so,\r\nas the evening drew near, he made an attempt at another farmhouse; but\r\nhere he fared worse than before; for he was called hard names and was\r\npromised arrest as a vagrant except he moved on promptly.\r\n\r\nThe night came on, chilly and overcast; and still the footsore monarch\r\nlaboured slowly on. \xc2\xa0He was obliged to keep moving, for every time he\r\nsat down to rest he was soon penetrated to the bone with the cold. \xc2\xa0All\r\nhis sensations and experiences, as he moved through the solemn gloom\r\nand the empty vastness of the night, were new and strange to him. \xc2\xa0At\r\nintervals he heard voices approach, pass by, and fade into silence; and\r\nas he saw nothing more of the bodies they belonged to than a sort of\r\nformless drifting blur, there was something spectral and uncanny about\r\nit all that made him shudder. \xc2\xa0Occasionally he caught the twinkle of a\r\nlight--always far away, apparently--almost in another world; if he heard\r\nthe tinkle of a sheep\xe2\x80\x99s bell, it was vague, distant, indistinct;\r\nthe muffled lowing of the herds floated to him on the night wind in\r\nvanishing cadences, a mournful sound; now and then came the complaining\r\nhowl of a dog over viewless expanses of field and forest; all sounds\r\nwere remote; they made the little King feel that all life and activity\r\nwere far removed from him, and that he stood solitary, companionless, in\r\nthe centre of a measureless solitude.\r\n\r\nHe stumbled along, through the gruesome fascinations of this new\r\nexperience, startled occasionally by the soft rustling of the dry leaves\r\noverhead, so like human whispers they seemed to sound; and by-and-by he\r\ncame suddenly upon the freckled light of a tin lantern near at hand. \xc2\xa0He\r\nstepped back into the shadows and waited. \xc2\xa0The lantern stood by the\r\nopen door of a barn. \xc2\xa0The King waited some time--there was no sound,\r\nand nobody stirring. \xc2\xa0He got so cold, standing still, and the hospitable\r\nbarn looked so enticing, that at last he resolved to risk everything and\r\nenter. He started swiftly and stealthily, and just as he was crossing\r\nthe threshold he heard voices behind him. \xc2\xa0He darted behind a cask,\r\nwithin the barn, and stooped down. \xc2\xa0Two farm-labourers came in, bringing\r\nthe lantern with them, and fell to work, talking meanwhile. \xc2\xa0Whilst they\r\nmoved about with the light, the King made good use of his eyes and took\r\nthe bearings of what seemed to be a good-sized stall at the further end\r\nof the place, purposing to grope his way to it when he should be left to\r\nhimself. \xc2\xa0He also noted the position of a pile of horse blankets, midway\r\nof the route, with the intent to levy upon them for the service of the\r\ncrown of England for one night.\r\n\r\nBy-and-by the men finished and went away, fastening the door behind\r\nthem and taking the lantern with them. \xc2\xa0The shivering King made for the\r\nblankets, with as good speed as the darkness would allow; gathered them\r\nup, and then groped his way safely to the stall. \xc2\xa0Of two of the blankets\r\nhe made a bed, then covered himself with the remaining two. \xc2\xa0He was a\r\nglad monarch, now, though the blankets were old and thin, and not quite\r\nwarm enough; and besides gave out a pungent horsey odour that was almost\r\nsuffocatingly powerful.\r\n\r\nAlthough the King was hungry and chilly, he was also so tired and so\r\ndrowsy that these latter influences soon began to get the advantage\r\nof the former, and he presently dozed off into a state of\r\nsemi-consciousness. \xc2\xa0Then, just as he was on the point of losing himself\r\nwholly, he distinctly felt something touch him! \xc2\xa0He was broad awake in\r\na moment, and gasping for breath. \xc2\xa0The cold horror of that mysterious\r\ntouch in the dark almost made his heart stand still. \xc2\xa0He lay motionless,\r\nand listened, scarcely breathing. But nothing stirred, and there was\r\nno sound. \xc2\xa0He continued to listen, and wait, during what seemed a long\r\ntime, but still nothing stirred, and there was no sound. \xc2\xa0So he began\r\nto drop into a drowse once more, at last; and all at once he felt that\r\nmysterious touch again! \xc2\xa0It was a grisly thing, this light touch from\r\nthis noiseless and invisible presence; it made the boy sick with ghostly\r\nfears. \xc2\xa0What should he do? \xc2\xa0That was the question; but he did not know\r\nhow to answer it. \xc2\xa0Should he leave these reasonably comfortable quarters\r\nand fly from this inscrutable horror? \xc2\xa0But fly whither? \xc2\xa0He could\r\nnot get out of the barn; and the idea of scurrying blindly hither and\r\nthither in the dark, within the captivity of the four walls, with this\r\nphantom gliding after him, and visiting him with that soft hideous touch\r\nupon cheek or shoulder at every turn, was intolerable. \xc2\xa0But to stay\r\nwhere he was, and endure this living death all night--was that better?\r\n\xc2\xa0No. \xc2\xa0What, then, was there left to do? \xc2\xa0Ah, there was but one course;\r\nhe knew it well--he must put out his hand and find that thing!\r\n\r\nIt was easy to think this; but it was hard to brace himself up to try\r\nit. Three times he stretched his hand a little way out into the dark,\r\ngingerly; and snatched it suddenly back, with a gasp--not because it\r\nhad encountered anything, but because he had felt so sure it was just\r\n_going_ to. \xc2\xa0But the fourth time, he groped a little further, and his\r\nhand lightly swept against something soft and warm. \xc2\xa0This petrified him,\r\nnearly, with fright; his mind was in such a state that he could imagine\r\nthe thing to be nothing else than a corpse, newly dead and still warm.\r\nHe thought he would rather die than touch it again. \xc2\xa0But he thought this\r\nfalse thought because he did not know the immortal strength of\r\nhuman curiosity. In no long time his hand was tremblingly groping\r\nagain--against his judgment, and without his consent--but groping\r\npersistently on, just the same. \xc2\xa0It encountered a bunch of long hair; he\r\nshuddered, but followed up the hair and found what seemed to be a warm\r\nrope; followed up the rope and found an innocent calf!--for the rope was\r\nnot a rope at all, but the calf\xe2\x80\x99s tail.\r\n\r\nThe King was cordially ashamed of himself for having gotten all that\r\nfright and misery out of so paltry a matter as a slumbering calf; but he\r\nneed not have felt so about it, for it was not the calf that frightened\r\nhim, but a dreadful non-existent something which the calf stood for; and\r\nany other boy, in those old superstitious times, would have acted and\r\nsuffered just as he had done.\r\n\r\nThe King was not only delighted to find that the creature was only a\r\ncalf, but delighted to have the calf\xe2\x80\x99s company; for he had been feeling\r\nso lonesome and friendless that the company and comradeship of even\r\nthis humble animal were welcome. \xc2\xa0And he had been so buffeted, so rudely\r\nentreated by his own kind, that it was a real comfort to him to feel\r\nthat he was at last in the society of a fellow-creature that had at\r\nleast a soft heart and a gentle spirit, whatever loftier attributes\r\nmight be lacking. \xc2\xa0So he resolved to waive rank and make friends with\r\nthe calf.\r\n\r\nWhile stroking its sleek warm back--for it lay near him and within easy\r\nreach--it occurred to him that this calf might be utilised in more ways\r\nthan one. \xc2\xa0Whereupon he re-arranged his bed, spreading it down close to\r\nthe calf; then he cuddled himself up to the calf\xe2\x80\x99s back, drew the covers\r\nup over himself and his friend, and in a minute or two was as warm and\r\ncomfortable as he had ever been in the downy couches of the regal palace\r\nof Westminster.\r\n\r\nPleasant thoughts came at once; life took on a cheerfuller seeming. \xc2\xa0He\r\nwas free of the bonds of servitude and crime, free of the companionship\r\nof base and brutal outlaws; he was warm; he was sheltered; in a word, he\r\nwas happy. \xc2\xa0The night wind was rising; it swept by in fitful gusts\r\nthat made the old barn quake and rattle, then its forces died down\r\nat intervals, and went moaning and wailing around corners and\r\nprojections--but it was all music to the King, now that he was snug and\r\ncomfortable: let it blow and rage, let it batter and bang, let it moan\r\nand wail, he minded it not, he only enjoyed it. \xc2\xa0He merely snuggled\r\nthe closer to his friend, in a luxury of warm contentment, and drifted\r\nblissfully out of consciousness into a deep and dreamless sleep that\r\nwas full of serenity and peace. \xc2\xa0The distant dogs howled, the melancholy\r\nkine complained, and the winds went on raging, whilst furious sheets\r\nof rain drove along the roof; but the Majesty of England slept on,\r\nundisturbed, and the calf did the same, it being a simple creature, and\r\nnot easily troubled by storms or embarrassed by sleeping with a king.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XIX. The Prince with the peasants.\r\n\r\nWhen the King awoke in the early morning, he found that a wet but\r\nthoughtful rat had crept into the place during the night and made a cosy\r\nbed for itself in his bosom. \xc2\xa0Being disturbed now, it scampered away.\r\nThe boy smiled, and said, \xe2\x80\x9cPoor fool, why so fearful? \xc2\xa0I am as forlorn\r\nas thou.  \xe2\x80\x98Twould be a sham in me to hurt the helpless, who am myself so\r\nhelpless. \xc2\xa0Moreover, I owe you thanks for a good omen; for when a king\r\nhas fallen so low that the very rats do make a bed of him, it surely\r\nmeaneth that his fortunes be upon the turn, since it is plain he can no\r\nlower go.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe got up and stepped out of the stall, and just then he heard the sound\r\nof children\xe2\x80\x99s voices. \xc2\xa0The barn door opened and a couple of little girls\r\ncame in. \xc2\xa0As soon as they saw him their talking and laughing ceased, and\r\nthey stopped and stood still, gazing at him with strong curiosity; they\r\npresently began to whisper together, then they approached nearer, and\r\nstopped again to gaze and whisper. \xc2\xa0By-and-by they gathered courage and\r\nbegan to discuss him aloud. \xc2\xa0One said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe hath a comely face.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe other added--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd pretty hair.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut is ill clothed enow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd how starved he looketh.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey came still nearer, sidling shyly around and about him, examining\r\nhim minutely from all points, as if he were some strange new kind of\r\nanimal, but warily and watchfully the while, as if they half feared he\r\nmight be a sort of animal that would bite, upon occasion. \xc2\xa0Finally they\r\nhalted before him, holding each other\xe2\x80\x99s hands for protection, and took a\r\ngood satisfying stare with their innocent eyes; then one of them plucked\r\nup all her courage and inquired with honest directness--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho art thou, boy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am the King,\xe2\x80\x9d was the grave answer.\r\n\r\nThe children gave a little start, and their eyes spread themselves wide\r\nopen and remained so during a speechless half minute. \xc2\xa0Then curiosity\r\nbroke the silence--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe _King_? \xc2\xa0What King?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe King of England.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe children looked at each other--then at him--then at each other\r\nagain--wonderingly, perplexedly; then one said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDidst hear him, Margery?--he said he is the King. \xc2\xa0Can that be true?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow can it be else but true, Prissy? \xc2\xa0Would he say a lie? \xc2\xa0For look\r\nyou, Prissy, an\xe2\x80\x99 it were not true, it _would_ be a lie. \xc2\xa0It surely would\r\nbe. Now think on\xe2\x80\x99t. \xc2\xa0For all things that be not true, be lies--thou\r\ncanst make nought else out of it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was a good tight argument, without a leak in it anywhere; and it left\r\nPrissy\xe2\x80\x99s half-doubts not a leg to stand on. \xc2\xa0She considered a moment,\r\nthen put the King upon his honour with the simple remark--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIf thou art truly the King, then I believe thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am truly the King.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis settled the matter. \xc2\xa0His Majesty\xe2\x80\x99s royalty was accepted without\r\nfurther question or discussion, and the two little girls began at once\r\nto inquire into how he came to be where he was, and how he came to be so\r\nunroyally clad, and whither he was bound, and all about his affairs. \xc2\xa0It\r\nwas a mighty relief to him to pour out his troubles where they would not\r\nbe scoffed at or doubted; so he told his tale with feeling, forgetting\r\neven his hunger for the time; and it was received with the deepest and\r\ntenderest sympathy by the gentle little maids. \xc2\xa0But when he got down\r\nto his latest experiences and they learned how long he had been without\r\nfood, they cut him short and hurried him away to the farmhouse to find a\r\nbreakfast for him.\r\n\r\nThe King was cheerful and happy now, and said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cWhen I\r\nam come to mine own again, I will always honour little children,\r\nremembering how that these trusted me and believed in me in my time\r\nof trouble; whilst they that were older, and thought themselves wiser,\r\nmocked at me and held me for a liar.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe children\xe2\x80\x99s mother received the King kindly, and was full of pity;\r\nfor his forlorn condition and apparently crazed intellect touched her\r\nwomanly heart. \xc2\xa0She was a widow, and rather poor; consequently she had\r\nseen trouble enough to enable her to feel for the unfortunate. \xc2\xa0She\r\nimagined that the demented boy had wandered away from his friends or\r\nkeepers; so she tried to find out whence he had come, in order that\r\nshe might take measures to return him; but all her references to\r\nneighbouring towns and villages, and all her inquiries in the same line\r\nwent for nothing--the boy\xe2\x80\x99s face, and his answers, too, showed that the\r\nthings she was talking of were not familiar to him. \xc2\xa0He spoke earnestly\r\nand simply about court matters, and broke down, more than once, when\r\nspeaking of the late King \xe2\x80\x98his father\xe2\x80\x99; but whenever the conversation\r\nchanged to baser topics, he lost interest and became silent.\r\n\r\nThe woman was mightily puzzled; but she did not give up. \xc2\xa0As she\r\nproceeded with her cooking, she set herself to contriving devices to\r\nsurprise the boy into betraying his real secret. \xc2\xa0She talked about\r\ncattle--he showed no concern; then about sheep--the same result: \xc2\xa0so\r\nher guess that he had been a shepherd boy was an error; she talked about\r\nmills; and about weavers, tinkers, smiths, trades and tradesmen of all\r\nsorts; and about Bedlam, and jails, and charitable retreats: \xc2\xa0but no\r\nmatter, she was baffled at all points. \xc2\xa0Not altogether, either; for she\r\nargued that she had narrowed the thing down to domestic service. \xc2\xa0Yes,\r\nshe was sure she was on the right track, now; he must have been a house\r\nservant. \xc2\xa0So she led up to that. \xc2\xa0But the result was discouraging. The\r\nsubject of sweeping appeared to weary him; fire-building failed to stir\r\nhim; scrubbing and scouring awoke no enthusiasm. The goodwife touched,\r\nwith a perishing hope, and rather as a matter of form, upon the subject\r\nof cooking. \xc2\xa0To her surprise, and her vast delight, the King\xe2\x80\x99s face\r\nlighted at once! \xc2\xa0Ah, she had hunted him down at last, she thought; and\r\nshe was right proud, too, of the devious shrewdness and tact which had\r\naccomplished it.\r\n\r\nHer tired tongue got a chance to rest, now; for the King\xe2\x80\x99s, inspired\r\nby gnawing hunger and the fragrant smells that came from the sputtering\r\npots and pans, turned itself loose and delivered itself up to such an\r\neloquent dissertation upon certain toothsome dishes, that within three\r\nminutes the woman said to herself, \xe2\x80\x9cOf a truth I was right--he hath\r\nholpen in a kitchen!\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0Then he broadened his bill of fare, and discussed\r\nit with such appreciation and animation, that the goodwife said to\r\nherself, \xe2\x80\x9cGood lack! how can he know so many dishes, and so fine ones\r\nwithal? \xc2\xa0For these belong only upon the tables of the rich and great.\r\n\xc2\xa0Ah, now I see! ragged outcast as he is, he must have served in the\r\npalace before his reason went astray; yes, he must have helped in the\r\nvery kitchen of the King himself! \xc2\xa0I will test him.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nFull of eagerness to prove her sagacity, she told the King to mind the\r\ncooking a moment--hinting that he might manufacture and add a dish or\r\ntwo, if he chose; then she went out of the room and gave her children a\r\nsign to follow after. \xc2\xa0The King muttered--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnother English king had a commission like to this, in a bygone\r\ntime--it is nothing against my dignity to undertake an office which the\r\ngreat Alfred stooped to assume. \xc2\xa0But I will try to better serve my trust\r\nthan he; for he let the cakes burn.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe intent was good, but the performance was not answerable to it, for\r\nthis King, like the other one, soon fell into deep thinkings concerning\r\nhis vast affairs, and the same calamity resulted--the cookery got\r\nburned. The woman returned in time to save the breakfast from entire\r\ndestruction; and she promptly brought the King out of his dreams with a\r\nbrisk and cordial tongue-lashing. Then, seeing how troubled he was\r\nover his violated trust, she softened at once, and was all goodness and\r\ngentleness toward him.\r\n\r\nThe boy made a hearty and satisfying meal, and was greatly refreshed and\r\ngladdened by it. \xc2\xa0It was a meal which was distinguished by this curious\r\nfeature, that rank was waived on both sides; yet neither recipient\r\nof the favour was aware that it had been extended. \xc2\xa0The goodwife had\r\nintended to feed this young tramp with broken victuals in a corner,\r\nlike any other tramp or like a dog; but she was so remorseful for the\r\nscolding she had given him, that she did what she could to atone for it\r\nby allowing him to sit at the family table and eat with his betters, on\r\nostensible terms of equality with them; and the King, on his side, was\r\nso remorseful for having broken his trust, after the family had been so\r\nkind to him, that he forced himself to atone for it by humbling himself\r\nto the family level, instead of requiring the woman and her children to\r\nstand and wait upon him, while he occupied their table in the solitary\r\nstate due to his birth and dignity. \xc2\xa0It does us all good to unbend\r\nsometimes. \xc2\xa0This good woman was made happy all the day long by the\r\napplauses which she got out of herself for her magnanimous condescension\r\nto a tramp; and the King was just as self-complacent over his gracious\r\nhumility toward a humble peasant woman.\r\n\r\nWhen breakfast was over, the housewife told the King to wash up the\r\ndishes. \xc2\xa0This command was a staggerer, for a moment, and the King came\r\nnear rebelling; but then he said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cAlfred the Great watched\r\nthe cakes; doubtless he would have washed the dishes too--therefore will\r\nI essay it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe made a sufficiently poor job of it; and to his surprise too, for the\r\ncleaning of wooden spoons and trenchers had seemed an easy thing to do.\r\nIt was a tedious and troublesome piece of work, but he finished it\r\nat last. \xc2\xa0He was becoming impatient to get away on his journey now;\r\nhowever, he was not to lose this thrifty dame\xe2\x80\x99s society so easily. \xc2\xa0She\r\nfurnished him some little odds and ends of employment, which he got\r\nthrough with after a fair fashion and with some credit. \xc2\xa0Then she set\r\nhim and the little girls to paring some winter apples; but he was so\r\nawkward at this service that she retired him from it and gave him a\r\nbutcher knife to grind.\r\n\r\nAfterwards she kept him carding wool until he began to think he had laid\r\nthe good King Alfred about far enough in the shade for the present in\r\nthe matter of showy menial heroisms that would read picturesquely in\r\nstory-books and histories, and so he was half-minded to resign. \xc2\xa0And\r\nwhen, just after the noonday dinner, the goodwife gave him a basket\r\nof kittens to drown, he did resign. \xc2\xa0At least he was just going to\r\nresign--for he felt that he must draw the line somewhere, and it\r\nseemed to him that to draw it at kitten-drowning was about the right\r\nthing--when there was an interruption. \xc2\xa0The interruption was John\r\nCanty--with a peddler\xe2\x80\x99s pack on his back--and Hugo.\r\n\r\nThe King discovered these rascals approaching the front gate before they\r\nhad had a chance to see him; so he said nothing about drawing the line,\r\nbut took up his basket of kittens and stepped quietly out the back way,\r\nwithout a word. \xc2\xa0He left the creatures in an out-house, and hurried on,\r\ninto a narrow lane at the rear.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XX. The Prince and the hermit.\r\n\r\nThe high hedge hid him from the house, now; and so, under the impulse of\r\na deadly fright, he let out all his forces and sped toward a wood in the\r\ndistance. \xc2\xa0He never looked back until he had almost gained the shelter\r\nof the forest; then he turned and descried two figures in the distance.\r\nThat was sufficient; he did not wait to scan them critically, but\r\nhurried on, and never abated his pace till he was far within the\r\ntwilight depths of the wood. Then he stopped; being persuaded that he\r\nwas now tolerably safe. He listened intently, but the stillness was\r\nprofound and solemn--awful, even, and depressing to the spirits. \xc2\xa0At\r\nwide intervals his straining ear did detect sounds, but they were so\r\nremote, and hollow, and mysterious, that they seemed not to be real\r\nsounds, but only the moaning and complaining ghosts of departed\r\nones. \xc2\xa0So the sounds were yet more dreary than the silence which they\r\ninterrupted.\r\n\r\nIt was his purpose, in the beginning, to stay where he was the rest of\r\nthe day; but a chill soon invaded his perspiring body, and he was at\r\nlast obliged to resume movement in order to get warm. He struck straight\r\nthrough the forest, hoping to pierce to a road presently, but he was\r\ndisappointed in this. \xc2\xa0He travelled on and on; but the farther he went,\r\nthe denser the wood became, apparently. \xc2\xa0The gloom began to thicken,\r\nby-and-by, and the King realised that the night was coming on. \xc2\xa0It made\r\nhim shudder to think of spending it in such an uncanny place; so he\r\ntried to hurry faster, but he only made the less speed, for he could\r\nnot now see well enough to choose his steps judiciously; consequently he\r\nkept tripping over roots and tangling himself in vines and briers.\r\n\r\nAnd how glad he was when at last he caught the glimmer of a light! He\r\napproached it warily, stopping often to look about him and listen. \xc2\xa0It\r\ncame from an unglazed window-opening in a shabby little hut. \xc2\xa0He heard\r\na voice, now, and felt a disposition to run and hide; but he changed his\r\nmind at once, for this voice was praying, evidently. \xc2\xa0He glided to the\r\none window of the hut, raised himself on tiptoe, and stole a glance\r\nwithin. \xc2\xa0The room was small; its floor was the natural earth, beaten\r\nhard by use; in a corner was a bed of rushes and a ragged blanket or\r\ntwo; near it was a pail, a cup, a basin, and two or three pots and pans;\r\nthere was a short bench and a three-legged stool; on the hearth the\r\nremains of a faggot fire were smouldering; before a shrine, which was\r\nlighted by a single candle, knelt an aged man, and on an old wooden box\r\nat his side lay an open book and a human skull. \xc2\xa0The man was of large,\r\nbony frame; his hair and whiskers were very long and snowy white; he\r\nwas clothed in a robe of sheepskins which reached from his neck to his\r\nheels.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cA holy hermit!\xe2\x80\x9d said the King to himself; \xe2\x80\x9cnow am I indeed fortunate.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe hermit rose from his knees; the King knocked. \xc2\xa0A deep voice\r\nresponded--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEnter!--but leave sin behind, for the ground whereon thou shalt stand\r\nis holy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King entered, and paused. \xc2\xa0The hermit turned a pair of gleaming,\r\nunrestful eyes upon him, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWho art thou?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am the King,\xe2\x80\x9d came the answer, with placid simplicity.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWelcome, King!\xe2\x80\x9d cried the hermit, with enthusiasm. \xc2\xa0Then, bustling\r\nabout with feverish activity, and constantly saying, \xe2\x80\x9cWelcome, welcome,\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n he arranged his bench, seated the King on it, by the hearth, threw some\r\nfaggots on the fire, and finally fell to pacing the floor with a nervous\r\nstride.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWelcome! \xc2\xa0Many have sought sanctuary here, but they were not worthy,\r\nand were turned away. \xc2\xa0But a King who casts his crown away, and despises\r\nthe vain splendours of his office, and clothes his body in rags, to\r\ndevote his life to holiness and the mortification of the flesh--he is\r\nworthy, he is welcome!--here shall he abide all his days till death\r\ncome.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0The King hastened to interrupt and explain, but the hermit paid\r\nno attention to him--did not even hear him, apparently, but went right\r\non with his talk, with a raised voice and a growing energy.  \xe2\x80\x9cAnd thou\r\nshalt be at peace here. \xc2\xa0None shall find out thy refuge to disquiet thee\r\nwith supplications to return to that empty and foolish life which God\r\nhath moved thee to abandon. \xc2\xa0Thou shalt pray here; thou shalt study the\r\nBook; thou shalt meditate upon the follies and delusions of this world,\r\nand upon the sublimities of the world to come; thou shalt feed upon\r\ncrusts and herbs, and scourge thy body with whips, daily, to the\r\npurifying of thy soul. Thou shalt wear a hair shirt next thy skin;\r\nthou shalt drink water only; and thou shalt be at peace; yes, wholly at\r\npeace; for whoso comes to seek thee shall go his way again, baffled; he\r\nshall not find thee, he shall not molest thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old man, still pacing back and forth, ceased to speak aloud, and\r\nbegan to mutter. \xc2\xa0The King seized this opportunity to state his case;\r\nand he did it with an eloquence inspired by uneasiness and apprehension.\r\n\xc2\xa0But the hermit went on muttering, and gave no heed. \xc2\xa0And still\r\nmuttering, he approached the King and said impressively--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Sh! \xc2\xa0I will tell you a secret!\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0He bent down to impart it, but\r\nchecked himself, and assumed a listening attitude. \xc2\xa0After a moment\r\nor two he went on tiptoe to the window-opening, put his head out, and\r\npeered around in the gloaming, then came tiptoeing back again, put his\r\nface close down to the King\xe2\x80\x99s, and whispered--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI am an archangel!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King started violently, and said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cWould God I were with\r\nthe outlaws again; for lo, now am I the prisoner of a madman!\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0His\r\napprehensions were heightened, and they showed plainly in his face. \xc2\xa0In\r\na low excited voice the hermit continued--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see you feel my atmosphere! \xc2\xa0There\xe2\x80\x99s awe in your face! \xc2\xa0None may\r\nbe in this atmosphere and not be thus affected; for it is the very\r\natmosphere of heaven. \xc2\xa0I go thither and return, in the twinkling of an\r\neye. \xc2\xa0I was made an archangel on this very spot, it is five years ago,\r\nby angels sent from heaven to confer that awful dignity. \xc2\xa0Their presence\r\nfilled this place with an intolerable brightness. \xc2\xa0And they knelt to me,\r\nKing! yes, they knelt to me! for I was greater than they. \xc2\xa0I have walked\r\nin the courts of heaven, and held speech with the patriarchs. \xc2\xa0Touch\r\nmy hand--be not afraid--touch it. \xc2\xa0There--now thou hast touched a hand\r\nwhich has been clasped by Abraham and Isaac and Jacob! \xc2\xa0For I have\r\nwalked in the golden courts; I have seen the Deity face to face!\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0He\r\npaused, to give this speech effect; then his face suddenly changed, and\r\nhe started to his feet again saying, with angry energy, \xe2\x80\x9cYes, I am an\r\narchangel; _a mere archangel!_--I that might have been pope! \xc2\xa0It is\r\nverily true. \xc2\xa0I was told it from heaven in a dream, twenty years ago;\r\nah, yes, I was to be pope!--and I _should_ have been pope, for Heaven\r\nhad said it--but the King dissolved my religious house, and I, poor\r\nobscure unfriended monk, was cast homeless upon the world, robbed of my\r\nmighty destiny!\xe2\x80\x9d Here he began to mumble again, and beat his forehead in\r\nfutile rage, with his fist; now and then articulating a venomous curse,\r\nand now and then a pathetic \xe2\x80\x9cWherefore I am nought but an archangel--I\r\nthat should have been pope!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo he went on, for an hour, whilst the poor little King sat and\r\nsuffered. Then all at once the old man\xe2\x80\x99s frenzy departed, and he became\r\nall gentleness. \xc2\xa0His voice softened, he came down out of his clouds, and\r\nfell to prattling along so simply and so humanly, that he soon won the\r\nKing\xe2\x80\x99s heart completely. \xc2\xa0The old devotee moved the boy nearer to the\r\nfire and made him comfortable; doctored his small bruises and abrasions\r\nwith a deft and tender hand; and then set about preparing and cooking a\r\nsupper--chatting pleasantly all the time, and occasionally stroking the\r\nlad\xe2\x80\x99s cheek or patting his head, in such a gently caressing way that in\r\na little while all the fear and repulsion inspired by the archangel were\r\nchanged to reverence and affection for the man.\r\n\r\nThis happy state of things continued while the two ate the supper; then,\r\nafter a prayer before the shrine, the hermit put the boy to bed, in a\r\nsmall adjoining room, tucking him in as snugly and lovingly as a mother\r\nmight; and so, with a parting caress, left him and sat down by the\r\nfire, and began to poke the brands about in an absent and aimless way.\r\nPresently he paused; then tapped his forehead several times with his\r\nfingers, as if trying to recall some thought which had escaped from his\r\nmind. \xc2\xa0Apparently he was unsuccessful. \xc2\xa0Now he started quickly up, and\r\nentered his guest\xe2\x80\x99s room, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art King?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes,\xe2\x80\x9d was the response, drowsily uttered.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat King?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf England.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOf England? \xc2\xa0Then Henry is gone!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAlack, it is so. \xc2\xa0I am his son.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA black frown settled down upon the hermit\xe2\x80\x99s face, and he clenched his\r\nbony hands with a vindictive energy. \xc2\xa0He stood a few moments, breathing\r\nfast and swallowing repeatedly, then said in a husky voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDost know it was he that turned us out into the world houseless and\r\nhomeless?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was no response. \xc2\xa0The old man bent down and scanned the boy\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nreposeful face and listened to his placid breathing.  \xe2\x80\x9cHe sleeps--sleeps\r\nsoundly;\xe2\x80\x9d and the frown vanished away and gave place to an expression of\r\nevil satisfaction. \xc2\xa0A smile flitted across the dreaming boy\xe2\x80\x99s features.\r\nThe hermit muttered, \xe2\x80\x9cSo--his heart is happy;\xe2\x80\x9d and he turned away. \xc2\xa0He\r\nwent stealthily about the place, seeking here and there for something;\r\nnow and then halting to listen, now and then jerking his head around\r\nand casting a quick glance toward the bed; and always muttering, always\r\nmumbling to himself. \xc2\xa0At last he found what he seemed to want--a rusty\r\nold butcher knife and a whetstone. \xc2\xa0Then he crept to his place by the\r\nfire, sat himself down, and began to whet the knife softly on the stone,\r\nstill muttering, mumbling, ejaculating. \xc2\xa0The winds sighed around the\r\nlonely place, the mysterious voices of the night floated by out of the\r\ndistances. \xc2\xa0The shining eyes of venturesome mice and rats peered out at\r\nthe old man from cracks and coverts, but he went on with his work, rapt,\r\nabsorbed, and noted none of these things.\r\n\r\nAt long intervals he drew his thumb along the edge of his knife, and\r\nnodded his head with satisfaction.  \xe2\x80\x9cIt grows sharper,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cyes,\r\nit grows sharper.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe took no note of the flight of time, but worked tranquilly on,\r\nentertaining himself with his thoughts, which broke out occasionally in\r\narticulate speech--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis father wrought us evil, he destroyed us--and is gone down into the\r\neternal fires! \xc2\xa0Yes, down into the eternal fires! \xc2\xa0He escaped us--but it\r\nwas God\xe2\x80\x99s will, yes it was God\xe2\x80\x99s will, we must not repine. \xc2\xa0But he\r\nhath not escaped the fires! \xc2\xa0No, he hath not escaped the fires, the\r\nconsuming, unpitying, remorseless fires--and _they_ are everlasting!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAnd so he wrought, and still wrought--mumbling, chuckling a low rasping\r\nchuckle at times--and at times breaking again into words--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was his father that did it all. \xc2\xa0I am but an archangel; but for him\r\nI should be pope!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King stirred. \xc2\xa0The hermit sprang noiselessly to the bedside, and\r\nwent down upon his knees, bending over the prostrate form with his knife\r\nuplifted. \xc2\xa0The boy stirred again; his eyes came open for an instant, but\r\nthere was no speculation in them, they saw nothing; the next moment his\r\ntranquil breathing showed that his sleep was sound once more.\r\n\r\nThe hermit watched and listened, for a time, keeping his position and\r\nscarcely breathing; then he slowly lowered his arms, and presently crept\r\naway, saying,--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is long past midnight; it is not best that he should cry out, lest\r\nby accident someone be passing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe glided about his hovel, gathering a rag here, a thong there, and\r\nanother one yonder; then he returned, and by careful and gentle handling\r\nhe managed to tie the King\xe2\x80\x99s ankles together without waking him. \xc2\xa0Next\r\nhe essayed to tie the wrists; he made several attempts to cross them,\r\nbut the boy always drew one hand or the other away, just as the cord was\r\nready to be applied; but at last, when the archangel was almost ready\r\nto despair, the boy crossed his hands himself, and the next moment\r\nthey were bound. Now a bandage was passed under the sleeper\xe2\x80\x99s chin and\r\nbrought up over his head and tied fast--and so softly, so gradually,\r\nand so deftly were the knots drawn together and compacted, that the boy\r\nslept peacefully through it all without stirring.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXI. Hendon to the rescue.\r\n\r\nThe old man glided away, stooping, stealthy, cat-like, and brought the\r\nlow bench. \xc2\xa0He seated himself upon it, half his body in the dim and\r\nflickering light, and the other half in shadow; and so, with his craving\r\neyes bent upon the slumbering boy, he kept his patient vigil there,\r\nheedless of the drift of time, and softly whetted his knife, and mumbled\r\nand chuckled; and in aspect and attitude he resembled nothing so much as\r\na grizzly, monstrous spider, gloating over some hapless insect that lay\r\nbound and helpless in his web.\r\n\r\nAfter a long while, the old man, who was still gazing,--yet not seeing,\r\nhis mind having settled into a dreamy abstraction,--observed, on a\r\nsudden, that the boy\xe2\x80\x99s eyes were open! wide open and staring!--staring\r\nup in frozen horror at the knife. \xc2\xa0The smile of a gratified devil crept\r\nover the old man\xe2\x80\x99s face, and he said, without changing his attitude or\r\nhis occupation--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSon of Henry the Eighth, hast thou prayed?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boy struggled helplessly in his bonds, and at the same time forced\r\na smothered sound through his closed jaws, which the hermit chose to\r\ninterpret as an affirmative answer to his question.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen pray again. \xc2\xa0Pray the prayer for the dying!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA shudder shook the boy\xe2\x80\x99s frame, and his face blenched. \xc2\xa0Then he\r\nstruggled again to free himself--turning and twisting himself this way\r\nand that; tugging frantically, fiercely, desperately--but uselessly--to\r\nburst his fetters; and all the while the old ogre smiled down upon him,\r\nand nodded his head, and placidly whetted his knife; mumbling, from time\r\nto time, \xe2\x80\x9cThe moments are precious, they are few and precious--pray the\r\nprayer for the dying!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe boy uttered a despairing groan, and ceased from his struggles,\r\npanting. \xc2\xa0The tears came, then, and trickled, one after the other, down\r\nhis face; but this piteous sight wrought no softening effect upon the\r\nsavage old man.\r\n\r\nThe dawn was coming now; the hermit observed it, and spoke up sharply,\r\nwith a touch of nervous apprehension in his voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI may not indulge this ecstasy longer! \xc2\xa0The night is already gone. \xc2\xa0It\r\nseems but a moment--only a moment; would it had endured a year! \xc2\xa0Seed of\r\nthe Church\xe2\x80\x99s spoiler, close thy perishing eyes, an\xe2\x80\x99 thou fearest to look\r\nupon--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe rest was lost in inarticulate mutterings. \xc2\xa0The old man sank upon his\r\nknees, his knife in his hand, and bent himself over the moaning boy.\r\n\r\nHark! \xc2\xa0There was a sound of voices near the cabin--the knife dropped\r\nfrom the hermit\xe2\x80\x99s hand; he cast a sheepskin over the boy and started up,\r\ntrembling. \xc2\xa0The sounds increased, and presently the voices became rough\r\nand angry; then came blows, and cries for help; then a clatter of swift\r\nfootsteps, retreating. \xc2\xa0Immediately came a succession of thundering\r\nknocks upon the cabin door, followed by--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHullo-o-o! \xc2\xa0Open! \xc2\xa0And despatch, in the name of all the devils!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nOh, this was the blessedest sound that had ever made music in the King\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nears; for it was Miles Hendon\xe2\x80\x99s voice!\r\n\r\nThe hermit, grinding his teeth in impotent rage, moved swiftly out of\r\nthe bedchamber, closing the door behind him; and straightway the King\r\nheard a talk, to this effect, proceeding from the \xe2\x80\x98chapel\xe2\x80\x99:--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHomage and greeting, reverend sir! \xc2\xa0Where is the boy--_my_ boy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat boy, friend?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat boy! \xc2\xa0Lie me no lies, sir priest, play me no deceptions!--I am not\r\nin the humour for it. \xc2\xa0Near to this place I caught the scoundrels who I\r\njudged did steal him from me, and I made them confess; they said he was\r\nat large again, and they had tracked him to your door. \xc2\xa0They showed me\r\nhis very footprints. \xc2\xa0Now palter no more; for look you, holy sir, an\xe2\x80\x99\r\nthou produce him not--Where is the boy?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO good sir, peradventure you mean the ragged regal vagrant that tarried\r\nhere the night. \xc2\xa0If such as you take an interest in such as he, know,\r\nthen, that I have sent him of an errand. \xc2\xa0He will be back anon.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow soon? \xc2\xa0How soon? \xc2\xa0Come, waste not the time--cannot I overtake him?\r\nHow soon will he be back?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou need\xe2\x80\x99st not stir; he will return quickly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSo be it, then. \xc2\xa0I will try to wait. \xc2\xa0But stop!--_you_ sent him of an\r\nerrand?--you! \xc2\xa0Verily this is a lie--he would not go. \xc2\xa0He would pull thy\r\nold beard, an\xe2\x80\x99 thou didst offer him such an insolence. Thou hast lied,\r\nfriend; thou hast surely lied! \xc2\xa0He would not go for thee, nor for any\r\nman.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor any _man_--no; haply not. \xc2\xa0But I am not a man.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_What_! \xc2\xa0Now o\xe2\x80\x99 God\xe2\x80\x99s name what art thou, then?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a secret--mark thou reveal it not. \xc2\xa0I am an archangel!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThere was a tremendous ejaculation from Miles Hendon--not altogether\r\nunprofane--followed by--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis doth well and truly account for his complaisance! \xc2\xa0Right well\r\nI knew he would budge nor hand nor foot in the menial service of any\r\nmortal; but, lord, even a king must obey when an archangel gives the\r\nword o\xe2\x80\x99 command! \xc2\xa0Let me--\xe2\x80\x98sh! \xc2\xa0What noise was that?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll this while the little King had been yonder, alternately quaking with\r\nterror and trembling with hope; and all the while, too, he had thrown\r\nall the strength he could into his anguished moanings, constantly\r\nexpecting them to reach Hendon\xe2\x80\x99s ear, but always realising, with\r\nbitterness, that they failed, or at least made no impression. \xc2\xa0So this\r\nlast remark of his servant came as comes a reviving breath from fresh\r\nfields to the dying; and he exerted himself once more, and with all his\r\nenergy, just as the hermit was saying--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNoise? \xc2\xa0I heard only the wind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMayhap it was. \xc2\xa0Yes, doubtless that was it. \xc2\xa0I have been hearing it\r\nfaintly all the--there it is again! \xc2\xa0It is not the wind! \xc2\xa0What an odd\r\nsound! \xc2\xa0Come, we will hunt it out!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nNow the King\xe2\x80\x99s joy was nearly insupportable. \xc2\xa0His tired lungs did\r\ntheir utmost--and hopefully, too--but the sealed jaws and the muffling\r\nsheepskin sadly crippled the effort. \xc2\xa0Then the poor fellow\xe2\x80\x99s heart sank,\r\nto hear the hermit say--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, it came from without--I think from the copse yonder. \xc2\xa0Come, I will\r\nlead the way.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King heard the two pass out, talking; heard their footsteps die\r\nquickly away--then he was alone with a boding, brooding, awful silence.\r\n\r\nIt seemed an age till he heard the steps and voices approaching\r\nagain--and this time he heard an added sound,--the trampling of hoofs,\r\napparently. \xc2\xa0Then he heard Hendon say--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will not wait longer. \xc2\xa0I _cannot_ wait longer. \xc2\xa0He has lost his way\r\nin this thick wood. \xc2\xa0Which direction took he? \xc2\xa0Quick--point it out to\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe--but wait; I will go with thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood--good! \xc2\xa0Why, truly thou art better than thy looks. \xc2\xa0Marry I do\r\nnot think there\xe2\x80\x99s not another archangel with so right a heart as thine.\r\n\xc2\xa0Wilt ride? \xc2\xa0Wilt take the wee donkey that\xe2\x80\x99s for my boy, or wilt thou\r\nfork thy holy legs over this ill-conditioned slave of a mule that I have\r\nprovided for myself?--and had been cheated in too, had he cost but the\r\nindifferent sum of a month\xe2\x80\x99s usury on a brass farthing let to a tinker\r\nout of work.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo--ride thy mule, and lead thine ass; I am surer on mine own feet, and\r\nwill walk.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen prithee mind the little beast for me while I take my life in my\r\nhands and make what success I may toward mounting the big one.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen followed a confusion of kicks, cuffs, tramplings and plungings,\r\naccompanied by a thunderous intermingling of volleyed curses, and\r\nfinally a bitter apostrophe to the mule, which must have broken its\r\nspirit, for hostilities seemed to cease from that moment.\r\n\r\nWith unutterable misery the fettered little King heard the voices and\r\nfootsteps fade away and die out. \xc2\xa0All hope forsook him, now, for the\r\nmoment, and a dull despair settled down upon his heart. \xe2\x80\x9cMy only friend\r\nis deceived and got rid of,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cthe hermit will return and--\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0He\r\nfinished with a gasp; and at once fell to struggling so frantically with\r\nhis bonds again, that he shook off the smothering sheepskin.\r\n\r\nAnd now he heard the door open! \xc2\xa0The sound chilled him to the\r\nmarrow--already he seemed to feel the knife at his throat. \xc2\xa0Horror made\r\nhim close his eyes; horror made him open them again--and before him\r\nstood John Canty and Hugo!\r\n\r\nHe would have said \xe2\x80\x9cThank God!\xe2\x80\x9d if his jaws had been free.\r\n\r\nA moment or two later his limbs were at liberty, and his captors, each\r\ngripping him by an arm, were hurrying him with all speed through the\r\nforest.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXII. A Victim of Treachery.\r\n\r\nOnce more \xe2\x80\x98King Foo-foo the First\xe2\x80\x99 was roving with the tramps and\r\noutlaws, a butt for their coarse jests and dull-witted railleries, and\r\nsometimes the victim of small spitefulness at the hands of Canty and\r\nHugo when the Ruffler\xe2\x80\x99s back was turned. \xc2\xa0None but Canty and Hugo really\r\ndisliked him. \xc2\xa0Some of the others liked him, and all admired his pluck\r\nand spirit. \xc2\xa0During two or three days, Hugo, in whose ward and charge\r\nthe King was, did what he covertly could to make the boy uncomfortable;\r\nand at night, during the customary orgies, he amused the company by\r\nputting small indignities upon him--always as if by accident. \xc2\xa0Twice he\r\nstepped upon the King\xe2\x80\x99s toes--accidentally--and the King, as became his\r\nroyalty, was contemptuously unconscious of it and indifferent to it; but\r\nthe third time Hugo entertained himself in that way, the King felled\r\nhim to the ground with a cudgel, to the prodigious delight of the tribe.\r\n\xc2\xa0Hugo, consumed with anger and shame, sprang up, seized a cudgel, and\r\ncame at his small adversary in a fury. \xc2\xa0Instantly a ring was formed\r\naround the gladiators, and the betting and cheering began.\r\n\r\nBut poor Hugo stood no chance whatever. \xc2\xa0His frantic and lubberly\r\n\xe2\x80\x98prentice-work found but a poor market for itself when pitted against\r\nan arm which had been trained by the first masters of Europe in\r\nsingle-stick, quarter-staff, and every art and trick of swordsmanship.\r\n\xc2\xa0The little King stood, alert but at graceful ease, and caught and\r\nturned aside the thick rain of blows with a facility and precision which\r\nset the motley on-lookers wild with admiration; and every now and then,\r\nwhen his practised eye detected an opening, and a lightning-swift rap\r\nupon Hugo\xe2\x80\x99s head followed as a result, the storm of cheers and laughter\r\nthat swept the place was something wonderful to hear. \xc2\xa0At the end of\r\nfifteen minutes, Hugo, all battered, bruised, and the target for\r\na pitiless bombardment of ridicule, slunk from the field; and the\r\nunscathed hero of the fight was seized and borne aloft upon the\r\nshoulders of the joyous rabble to the place of honour beside the\r\nRuffler, where with vast ceremony he was crowned King of the Game-Cocks;\r\nhis meaner title being at the same time solemnly cancelled and annulled,\r\nand a decree of banishment from the gang pronounced against any who\r\nshould thenceforth utter it.\r\n\r\nAll attempts to make the King serviceable to the troop had failed. He\r\nhad stubbornly refused to act; moreover, he was always trying to escape.\r\n\xc2\xa0He had been thrust into an unwatched kitchen, the first day of his\r\nreturn; he not only came forth empty-handed, but tried to rouse the\r\nhousemates. He was sent out with a tinker to help him at his work;\r\nhe would not work; moreover, he threatened the tinker with his own\r\nsoldering-iron; and finally both Hugo and the tinker found their\r\nhands full with the mere matter of keeping his from getting away. \xc2\xa0He\r\ndelivered the thunders of his royalty upon the heads of all who hampered\r\nhis liberties or tried to force him to service. \xc2\xa0He was sent out, in\r\nHugo\xe2\x80\x99s charge, in company with a slatternly woman and a diseased baby,\r\nto beg; but the result was not encouraging--he declined to plead for the\r\nmendicants, or be a party to their cause in any way.\r\n\r\nThus several days went by; and the miseries of this tramping life, and\r\nthe weariness and sordidness and meanness and vulgarity of it, became\r\ngradually and steadily so intolerable to the captive that he began at\r\nlast to feel that his release from the hermit\xe2\x80\x99s knife must prove only a\r\ntemporary respite from death, at best.\r\n\r\nBut at night, in his dreams, these things were forgotten, and he was\r\non his throne, and master again. \xc2\xa0This, of course, intensified the\r\nsufferings of the awakening--so the mortifications of each succeeding\r\nmorning of the few that passed between his return to bondage and the\r\ncombat with Hugo, grew bitterer and bitterer, and harder and harder to\r\nbear.\r\n\r\nThe morning after that combat, Hugo got up with a heart filled with\r\nvengeful purposes against the King. \xc2\xa0He had two plans, in particular.\r\nOne was to inflict upon the lad what would be, to his proud spirit\r\nand \xe2\x80\x98imagined\xe2\x80\x99 royalty, a peculiar humiliation; and if he failed to\r\naccomplish this, his other plan was to put a crime of some kind upon the\r\nKing, and then betray him into the implacable clutches of the law.\r\n\r\nIn pursuance of the first plan, he purposed to put a \xe2\x80\x98clime\xe2\x80\x99 upon the\r\nKing\xe2\x80\x99s leg; rightly judging that that would mortify him to the last and\r\nperfect degree; and as soon as the clime should operate, he meant to get\r\nCanty\xe2\x80\x99s help, and _force_ the King to expose his leg in the highway\r\nand beg for alms.  \xe2\x80\x98Clime\xe2\x80\x99 was the cant term for a sore, artificially\r\ncreated. To make a clime, the operator made a paste or poultice of\r\nunslaked lime, soap, and the rust of old iron, and spread it upon a\r\npiece of leather, which was then bound tightly upon the leg. \xc2\xa0This would\r\npresently fret off the skin, and make the flesh raw and angry-looking;\r\nblood was then rubbed upon the limb, which, being fully dried, took on a\r\ndark and repulsive colour. \xc2\xa0Then a bandage of soiled rags was put on in\r\na cleverly careless way which would allow the hideous ulcer to be seen,\r\nand move the compassion of the passer-by. {8}\r\n\r\nHugo got the help of the tinker whom the King had cowed with the\r\nsoldering-iron; they took the boy out on a tinkering tramp, and as soon\r\nas they were out of sight of the camp they threw him down and the tinker\r\nheld him while Hugo bound the poultice tight and fast upon his leg.\r\n\r\nThe King raged and stormed, and promised to hang the two the moment the\r\nsceptre was in his hand again; but they kept a firm grip upon him\r\nand enjoyed his impotent struggling and jeered at his threats. \xc2\xa0This\r\ncontinued until the poultice began to bite; and in no long time its work\r\nwould have been perfected, if there had been no interruption. \xc2\xa0But there\r\nwas; for about this time the \xe2\x80\x98slave\xe2\x80\x99 who had made the speech denouncing\r\nEngland\xe2\x80\x99s laws, appeared on the scene, and put an end to the enterprise,\r\nand stripped off the poultice and bandage.\r\n\r\nThe King wanted to borrow his deliverer\xe2\x80\x99s cudgel and warm the jackets\r\nof the two rascals on the spot; but the man said no, it would bring\r\ntrouble--leave the matter till night; the whole tribe being together,\r\nthen, the outside world would not venture to interfere or interrupt. \xc2\xa0He\r\nmarched the party back to camp and reported the affair to the Ruffler,\r\nwho listened, pondered, and then decided that the King should not be\r\nagain detailed to beg, since it was plain he was worthy of something\r\nhigher and better--wherefore, on the spot he promoted him from the\r\nmendicant rank and appointed him to steal!\r\n\r\nHugo was overjoyed. \xc2\xa0He had already tried to make the King steal, and\r\nfailed; but there would be no more trouble of that sort, now, for of\r\ncourse the King would not dream of defying a distinct command delivered\r\ndirectly from head-quarters. \xc2\xa0So he planned a raid for that very\r\nafternoon, purposing to get the King in the law\xe2\x80\x99s grip in the course of\r\nit; and to do it, too, with such ingenious strategy, that it should seem\r\nto be accidental and unintentional; for the King of the Game-Cocks was\r\npopular now, and the gang might not deal over-gently with an unpopular\r\nmember who played so serious a treachery upon him as the delivering him\r\nover to the common enemy, the law.\r\n\r\nVery well. \xc2\xa0All in good time Hugo strolled off to a neighbouring village\r\nwith his prey; and the two drifted slowly up and down one street after\r\nanother, the one watching sharply for a sure chance to achieve his evil\r\npurpose, and the other watching as sharply for a chance to dart away and\r\nget free of his infamous captivity for ever.\r\n\r\nBoth threw away some tolerably fair-looking opportunities; for both,\r\nin their secret hearts, were resolved to make absolutely sure work this\r\ntime, and neither meant to allow his fevered desires to seduce him into\r\nany venture that had much uncertainty about it.\r\n\r\nHugo\xe2\x80\x99s chance came first. \xc2\xa0For at last a woman approached who carried a\r\nfat package of some sort in a basket. \xc2\xa0Hugo\xe2\x80\x99s eyes sparkled with sinful\r\npleasure as he said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cBreath o\xe2\x80\x99 my life, an\xe2\x80\x99 I can but\r\nput _that_ upon him, \xe2\x80\x99tis good-den and God keep thee, King of the\r\nGame-Cocks!\xe2\x80\x9d He waited and watched--outwardly patient, but inwardly\r\nconsuming with excitement--till the woman had passed by, and the time\r\nwas ripe; then said, in a low voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTarry here till I come again,\xe2\x80\x9d and darted stealthily after the prey.\r\n\r\nThe King\xe2\x80\x99s heart was filled with joy--he could make his escape, now, if\r\nHugo\xe2\x80\x99s quest only carried him far enough away.\r\n\r\nBut he was to have no such luck. \xc2\xa0Hugo crept behind the woman, snatched\r\nthe package, and came running back, wrapping it in an old piece of\r\nblanket which he carried on his arm. \xc2\xa0The hue and cry was raised in a\r\nmoment, by the woman, who knew her loss by the lightening of her burden,\r\nalthough she had not seen the pilfering done. \xc2\xa0Hugo thrust the bundle\r\ninto the King\xe2\x80\x99s hands without halting, saying--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNow speed ye after me with the rest, and cry \xe2\x80\x98Stop thief!\xe2\x80\x99 but mind ye\r\nlead them astray!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe next moment Hugo turned a corner and darted down a crooked\r\nalley--and in another moment or two he lounged into view again, looking\r\ninnocent and indifferent, and took up a position behind a post to watch\r\nresults.\r\n\r\nThe insulted King threw the bundle on the ground; and the blanket fell\r\naway from it just as the woman arrived, with an augmenting crowd at her\r\nheels; she seized the King\xe2\x80\x99s wrist with one hand, snatched up her bundle\r\nwith the other, and began to pour out a tirade of abuse upon the boy\r\nwhile he struggled, without success, to free himself from her grip.\r\n\r\nHugo had seen enough--his enemy was captured and the law would get him,\r\nnow--so he slipped away, jubilant and chuckling, and wended campwards,\r\nframing a judicious version of the matter to give to the Ruffler\xe2\x80\x99s crew\r\nas he strode along.\r\n\r\nThe King continued to struggle in the woman\xe2\x80\x99s strong grasp, and now and\r\nthen cried out in vexation--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cUnhand me, thou foolish creature; it was not I that bereaved thee of\r\nthy paltry goods.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe crowd closed around, threatening the King and calling him names; a\r\nbrawny blacksmith in leather apron, and sleeves rolled to his elbows,\r\nmade a reach for him, saying he would trounce him well, for a lesson;\r\nbut just then a long sword flashed in the air and fell with convincing\r\nforce upon the man\xe2\x80\x99s arm, flat side down, the fantastic owner of it\r\nremarking pleasantly, at the same time--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarry, good souls, let us proceed gently, not with ill blood and\r\nuncharitable words. \xc2\xa0This is matter for the law\xe2\x80\x99s consideration,\r\nnot private and unofficial handling. \xc2\xa0Loose thy hold from the boy,\r\ngoodwife.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe blacksmith averaged the stalwart soldier with a glance, then went\r\nmuttering away, rubbing his arm; the woman released the boy\xe2\x80\x99s wrist\r\nreluctantly; the crowd eyed the stranger unlovingly, but prudently\r\nclosed their mouths. \xc2\xa0The King sprang to his deliverer\xe2\x80\x99s side, with\r\nflushed cheeks and sparkling eyes, exclaiming--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou hast lagged sorely, but thou comest in good season, now, Sir\r\nMiles; carve me this rabble to rags!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIII. The Prince a prisoner.\r\n\r\nHendon forced back a smile, and bent down and whispered in the King\xe2\x80\x99s\r\near--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSoftly, softly, my prince, wag thy tongue warily--nay, suffer it not to\r\nwag at all. \xc2\xa0Trust in me--all shall go well in the end.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he added\r\nto himself:  \xe2\x80\x9c_Sir_ Miles! \xc2\xa0Bless me, I had totally forgot I was a\r\nknight! Lord, how marvellous a thing it is, the grip his memory doth\r\ntake upon his quaint and crazy fancies! . . . An empty and foolish title\r\nis mine, and yet it is something to have deserved it; for I think it is\r\nmore honour to be held worthy to be a spectre-knight in his Kingdom of\r\nDreams and Shadows, than to be held base enough to be an earl in some of\r\nthe _real_ kingdoms of this world.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe crowd fell apart to admit a constable, who approached and was about\r\nto lay his hand upon the King\xe2\x80\x99s shoulder, when Hendon said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGently, good friend, withhold your hand--he shall go peaceably; I am\r\nresponsible for that. \xc2\xa0Lead on, we will follow.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe officer led, with the woman and her bundle; Miles and the King\r\nfollowed after, with the crowd at their heels. \xc2\xa0The King was inclined to\r\nrebel; but Hendon said to him in a low voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cReflect, Sire--your laws are the wholesome breath of your own royalty;\r\nshall their source resist them, yet require the branches to respect\r\nthem? Apparently one of these laws has been broken; when the King is on\r\nhis throne again, can it ever grieve him to remember that when he was\r\nseemingly a private person he loyally sank the king in the citizen and\r\nsubmitted to its authority?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThou art right; say no more; thou shalt see that whatsoever the King\r\nof England requires a subject to suffer, under the law, he will himself\r\nsuffer while he holdeth the station of a subject.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen the woman was called upon to testify before the justice of the\r\npeace, she swore that the small prisoner at the bar was the person who\r\nhad committed the theft; there was none able to show the contrary, so\r\nthe King stood convicted. \xc2\xa0The bundle was now unrolled, and when the\r\ncontents proved to be a plump little dressed pig, the judge looked\r\ntroubled, whilst Hendon turned pale, and his body was thrilled with an\r\nelectric shiver of dismay; but the King remained unmoved, protected\r\nby his ignorance. \xc2\xa0The judge meditated, during an ominous pause, then\r\nturned to the woman, with the question--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat dost thou hold this property to be worth?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe woman courtesied and replied--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThree shillings and eightpence, your worship--I could not abate a penny\r\nand set forth the value honestly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe justice glanced around uncomfortably upon the crowd, then nodded to\r\nthe constable, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cClear the court and close the doors.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nIt was done. \xc2\xa0None remained but the two officials, the accused, the\r\naccuser, and Miles Hendon. \xc2\xa0This latter was rigid and colourless, and\r\non his forehead big drops of cold sweat gathered, broke and blended\r\ntogether, and trickled down his face. \xc2\xa0The judge turned to the woman\r\nagain, and said, in a compassionate voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Tis a poor ignorant lad, and mayhap was driven hard by hunger, for\r\nthese be grievous times for the unfortunate; mark you, he hath not an\r\nevil face--but when hunger driveth--Good woman! dost know that when one\r\nsteals a thing above the value of thirteenpence ha\xe2\x80\x99penny the law saith\r\nhe shall _hang_ for it?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe little King started, wide-eyed with consternation, but controlled\r\nhimself and held his peace; but not so the woman. \xc2\xa0She sprang to her\r\nfeet, shaking with fright, and cried out--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, good lack, what have I done! \xc2\xa0God-a-mercy, I would not hang\r\nthe poor thing for the whole world! \xc2\xa0Ah, save me from this, your\r\nworship--what shall I do, what _can_ I do?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe justice maintained his judicial composure, and simply said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDoubtless it is allowable to revise the value, since it is not yet writ\r\nupon the record.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen in God\xe2\x80\x99s name call the pig eightpence, and heaven bless the day\r\nthat freed my conscience of this awesome thing!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiles Hendon forgot all decorum in his delight; and surprised the King\r\nand wounded his dignity, by throwing his arms around him and hugging\r\nhim. The woman made her grateful adieux and started away with her pig;\r\nand when the constable opened the door for her, he followed her out into\r\nthe narrow hall. \xc2\xa0The justice proceeded to write in his record book.\r\n\xc2\xa0Hendon, always alert, thought he would like to know why the officer\r\nfollowed the woman out; so he slipped softly into the dusky hall and\r\nlistened. \xc2\xa0He heard a conversation to this effect--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a fat pig, and promises good eating; I will buy it of thee; here\r\nis the eightpence.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEightpence, indeed! \xc2\xa0Thou\xe2\x80\x99lt do no such thing. \xc2\xa0It cost me three\r\nshillings and eightpence, good honest coin of the last reign, that old\r\nHarry that\xe2\x80\x99s just dead ne\xe2\x80\x99er touched or tampered with. \xc2\xa0A fig for thy\r\neightpence!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStands the wind in that quarter? \xc2\xa0Thou wast under oath, and so swore\r\nfalsely when thou saidst the value was but eightpence. \xc2\xa0Come straightway\r\nback with me before his worship, and answer for the crime!--and then the\r\nlad will hang.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere, there, dear heart, say no more, I am content. \xc2\xa0Give me the\r\neightpence, and hold thy peace about the matter.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe woman went off crying: \xc2\xa0Hendon slipped back into the court room,\r\nand the constable presently followed, after hiding his prize in some\r\nconvenient place. \xc2\xa0The justice wrote a while longer, then read the King\r\na wise and kindly lecture, and sentenced him to a short imprisonment\r\nin the common jail, to be followed by a public flogging. \xc2\xa0The astounded\r\nKing opened his mouth, and was probably going to order the good judge to\r\nbe beheaded on the spot; but he caught a warning sign from Hendon, and\r\nsucceeded in closing his mouth again before he lost anything out of it.\r\nHendon took him by the hand, now, made reverence to the justice, and the\r\ntwo departed in the wake of the constable toward the jail. \xc2\xa0The moment\r\nthe street was reached, the inflamed monarch halted, snatched away his\r\nhand, and exclaimed--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIdiot, dost imagine I will enter a common jail _alive_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHendon bent down and said, somewhat sharply--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_Will_ you trust in me? \xc2\xa0Peace! and forbear to worsen our chances with\r\ndangerous speech. \xc2\xa0What God wills, will happen; thou canst not hurry it,\r\nthou canst not alter it; therefore wait, and be patient--\xe2\x80\x98twill be time\r\nenow to rail or rejoice when what is to happen has happened.\xe2\x80\x9d {1}\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIV. The Escape.\r\n\r\nThe short winter day was nearly ended. \xc2\xa0The streets were deserted, save\r\nfor a few random stragglers, and these hurried straight along, with the\r\nintent look of people who were only anxious to accomplish their errands\r\nas quickly as possible, and then snugly house themselves from the rising\r\nwind and the gathering twilight. They looked neither to the right nor to\r\nthe left; they paid no attention to our party, they did not even seem\r\nto see them. Edward the Sixth wondered if the spectacle of a king on his\r\nway to jail had ever encountered such marvellous indifference before.\r\nBy-and-by the constable arrived at a deserted market-square, and\r\nproceeded to cross it. \xc2\xa0When he had reached the middle of it, Hendon\r\nlaid his hand upon his arm, and said in a low voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBide a moment, good sir, there is none in hearing, and I would say a\r\nword to thee.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy duty forbids it, sir; prithee hinder me not, the night comes on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cStay, nevertheless, for the matter concerns thee nearly. \xc2\xa0Turn thy back\r\na moment and seem not to see: \xc2\xa0_let this poor lad escape_.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis to me, sir! \xc2\xa0I arrest thee in--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNay, be not too hasty. \xc2\xa0See thou be careful and commit no foolish\r\nerror,\xe2\x80\x9d--then he shut his voice down to a whisper, and said in the man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\near--\xe2\x80\x9cthe pig thou hast purchased for eightpence may cost thee thy neck,\r\nman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe poor constable, taken by surprise, was speechless, at first, then\r\nfound his tongue and fell to blustering and threatening; but Hendon\r\nwas tranquil, and waited with patience till his breath was spent; then\r\nsaid--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have a liking to thee, friend, and would not willingly see thee\r\ncome to harm. \xc2\xa0Observe, I heard it all--every word. \xc2\xa0I will prove it to\r\nthee.\xe2\x80\x9d Then he repeated the conversation which the officer and the woman\r\nhad had together in the hall, word for word, and ended with--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere--have I set it forth correctly? \xc2\xa0Should not I be able to set it\r\nforth correctly before the judge, if occasion required?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man was dumb with fear and distress, for a moment; then he rallied,\r\nand said with forced lightness--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Tis making a mighty matter, indeed, out of a jest; I but plagued the\r\nwoman for mine amusement.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKept you the woman\xe2\x80\x99s pig for amusement?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe man answered sharply--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNought else, good sir--I tell thee \xe2\x80\x99twas but a jest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do begin to believe thee,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hendon, with a perplexing mixture of\r\nmockery and half-conviction in his tone; \xe2\x80\x9cbut tarry thou here a\r\nmoment whilst I run and ask his worship--for nathless, he being a man\r\nexperienced in law, in jests, in--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was moving away, still talking; the constable hesitated, fidgeted,\r\nspat out an oath or two, then cried out--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHold, hold, good sir--prithee wait a little--the judge! \xc2\xa0Why, man, he\r\nhath no more sympathy with a jest than hath a dead corpse!--come, and we\r\nwill speak further. \xc2\xa0Ods body! \xc2\xa0I seem to be in evil case--and all for\r\nan innocent and thoughtless pleasantry. I am a man of family; and my\r\nwife and little ones--List to reason, good your worship: what wouldst\r\nthou of me?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOnly that thou be blind and dumb and paralytic whilst one may count a\r\nhundred thousand--counting slowly,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hendon, with the expression of\r\na man who asks but a reasonable favour, and that a very little one.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is my destruction!\xe2\x80\x9d said the constable despairingly.  \xe2\x80\x9cAh, be\r\nreasonable, good sir; only look at this matter, on all its sides, and\r\nsee how mere a jest it is--how manifestly and how plainly it is so. \xc2\xa0And\r\neven if one granted it were not a jest, it is a fault so small that\r\ne\xe2\x80\x99en the grimmest penalty it could call forth would be but a rebuke and\r\nwarning from the judge\xe2\x80\x99s lips.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHendon replied with a solemnity which chilled the air about him--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThis jest of thine hath a name, in law,--wot you what it is?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI knew it not! \xc2\xa0Peradventure I have been unwise. \xc2\xa0I never dreamed it\r\nhad a name--ah, sweet heaven, I thought it was original.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYes, it hath a name. \xc2\xa0In the law this crime is called Non compos mentis\r\nlex talionis sic transit gloria mundi.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, my God!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd the penalty is death!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod be merciful to me a sinner!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBy advantage taken of one in fault, in dire peril, and at thy mercy,\r\nthou hast seized goods worth above thirteenpence ha\xe2\x80\x99penny, paying but\r\na trifle for the same; and this, in the eye of the law, is constructive\r\nbarratry, misprision of treason, malfeasance in office, ad hominem\r\nexpurgatis in statu quo--and the penalty is death by the halter, without\r\nransom, commutation, or benefit of clergy.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBear me up, bear me up, sweet sir, my legs do fail me! \xc2\xa0Be thou\r\nmerciful--spare me this doom, and I will turn my back and see nought\r\nthat shall happen.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGood! now thou\xe2\x80\x99rt wise and reasonable. \xc2\xa0And thou\xe2\x80\x99lt restore the pig?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI will, I will indeed--nor ever touch another, though heaven send it\r\nand an archangel fetch it. \xc2\xa0Go--I am blind for thy sake--I see nothing.\r\n\xc2\xa0I will say thou didst break in and wrest the prisoner from my hands by\r\nforce. \xc2\xa0It is but a crazy, ancient door--I will batter it down myself\r\nbetwixt midnight and the morning.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDo it, good soul, no harm will come of it; the judge hath a loving\r\ncharity for this poor lad, and will shed no tears and break no jailer\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbones for his escape.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXV. Hendon Hall.\r\n\r\nAs soon as Hendon and the King were out of sight of the constable, his\r\nMajesty was instructed to hurry to a certain place outside the town, and\r\nwait there, whilst Hendon should go to the inn and settle his account.\r\nHalf an hour later the two friends were blithely jogging eastward on\r\nHendon\xe2\x80\x99s sorry steeds. \xc2\xa0The King was warm and comfortable, now, for\r\nhe had cast his rags and clothed himself in the second-hand suit which\r\nHendon had bought on London Bridge.\r\n\r\nHendon wished to guard against over-fatiguing the boy; he judged that\r\nhard journeys, irregular meals, and illiberal measures of sleep would be\r\nbad for his crazed mind; whilst rest, regularity, and moderate exercise\r\nwould be pretty sure to hasten its cure; he longed to see the stricken\r\nintellect made well again and its diseased visions driven out of the\r\ntormented little head; therefore he resolved to move by easy stages\r\ntoward the home whence he had so long been banished, instead of obeying\r\nthe impulse of his impatience and hurrying along night and day.\r\n\r\nWhen he and the King had journeyed about ten miles, they reached a\r\nconsiderable village, and halted there for the night, at a good inn.\r\n\xc2\xa0The former relations were resumed; Hendon stood behind the King\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nchair, while he dined, and waited upon him; undressed him when he was\r\nready for bed; then took the floor for his own quarters, and slept\r\nathwart the door, rolled up in a blanket.\r\n\r\nThe next day, and the day after, they jogged lazily along talking\r\nover the adventures they had met since their separation, and mightily\r\nenjoying each other\xe2\x80\x99s narratives. \xc2\xa0Hendon detailed all his wide\r\nwanderings in search of the King, and described how the archangel had\r\nled him a fool\xe2\x80\x99s journey all over the forest, and taken him back to\r\nthe hut, finally, when he found he could not get rid of him. \xc2\xa0Then--he\r\nsaid--the old man went into the bedchamber and came staggering back\r\nlooking broken-hearted, and saying he had expected to find that the boy\r\nhad returned and laid down in there to rest, but it was not so. \xc2\xa0Hendon\r\nhad waited at the hut all day; hope of the King\xe2\x80\x99s return died out, then,\r\nand he departed upon the quest again.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd old Sanctum Sanctorum _was_ truly sorry your highness came not\r\nback,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hendon; \xe2\x80\x9cI saw it in his face.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarry I will never doubt _that_!\xe2\x80\x9d said the King--and then told his own\r\nstory; after which, Hendon was sorry he had not destroyed the archangel.\r\n\r\nDuring the last day of the trip, Hendon\xe2\x80\x99s spirits were soaring. His\r\ntongue ran constantly. \xc2\xa0He talked about his old father, and his brother\r\nArthur, and told of many things which illustrated their high and\r\ngenerous characters; he went into loving frenzies over his Edith,\r\nand was so glad-hearted that he was even able to say some gentle and\r\nbrotherly things about Hugh. \xc2\xa0He dwelt a deal on the coming meeting\r\nat Hendon Hall; what a surprise it would be to everybody, and what an\r\noutburst of thanksgiving and delight there would be.\r\n\r\nIt was a fair region, dotted with cottages and orchards, and the road\r\nled through broad pasture lands whose receding expanses, marked with\r\ngentle elevations and depressions, suggested the swelling and subsiding\r\nundulations of the sea. \xc2\xa0In the afternoon the returning prodigal made\r\nconstant deflections from his course to see if by ascending some hillock\r\nhe might not pierce the distance and catch a glimpse of his home. \xc2\xa0At\r\nlast he was successful, and cried out excitedly--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is the village, my Prince, and there is the Hall close by! You\r\nmay see the towers from here; and that wood there--that is my father\xe2\x80\x99s\r\npark. Ah, _now_ thou\xe2\x80\x99lt know what state and grandeur be! A house with\r\nseventy rooms--think of that!--and seven and twenty servants! \xc2\xa0A brave\r\nlodging for such as we, is it not so? \xc2\xa0Come, let us speed--my impatience\r\nwill not brook further delay.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAll possible hurry was made; still, it was after three o\xe2\x80\x99clock before\r\nthe village was reached. \xc2\xa0The travellers scampered through it, Hendon\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ntongue going all the time.  \xe2\x80\x9cHere is the church--covered with the same\r\nivy--none gone, none added.\xe2\x80\x9d  \xe2\x80\x9cYonder is the inn, the old Red Lion,--and\r\nyonder is the market-place.\xe2\x80\x9d  \xe2\x80\x9cHere is the Maypole, and here the\r\npump--nothing is altered; nothing but the people, at any rate; ten years\r\nmake a change in people; some of these I seem to know, but none know\r\nme.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0So his chat ran on.
