The end of the village was soon reached; then\r\nthe travellers struck into a crooked, narrow road, walled in with tall\r\nhedges, and hurried briskly along it for half a mile, then passed into a\r\nvast flower garden through an imposing gateway, whose huge stone pillars\r\nbore sculptured armorial devices. \xc2\xa0A noble mansion was before them.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWelcome to Hendon Hall, my King!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Miles.  \xe2\x80\x9cAh, \xe2\x80\x99tis a great\r\nday! \xc2\xa0My father and my brother, and the Lady Edith will be so mad with\r\njoy that they will have eyes and tongue for none but me in the first\r\ntransports of the meeting, and so thou\xe2\x80\x99lt seem but coldly welcomed--but\r\nmind it not; \xe2\x80\x99twill soon seem otherwise; for when I say thou art my\r\nward, and tell them how costly is my love for thee, thou\xe2\x80\x99lt see them\r\ntake thee to their breasts for Miles Hendon\xe2\x80\x99s sake, and make their house\r\nand hearts thy home for ever after!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe next moment Hendon sprang to the ground before the great door,\r\nhelped the King down, then took him by the hand and rushed within. A few\r\nsteps brought him to a spacious apartment; he entered, seated the King\r\nwith more hurry than ceremony, then ran toward a young man who sat at a\r\nwriting-table in front of a generous fire of logs.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEmbrace me, Hugh,\xe2\x80\x9d he cried, \xe2\x80\x9cand say thou\xe2\x80\x99rt glad I am come again! and\r\ncall our father, for home is not home till I shall touch his hand, and\r\nsee his face, and hear his voice once more!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Hugh only drew back, after betraying a momentary surprise, and bent\r\na grave stare upon the intruder--a stare which indicated somewhat of\r\noffended dignity, at first, then changed, in response to some inward\r\nthought or purpose, to an expression of marvelling curiosity, mixed with\r\na real or assumed compassion. \xc2\xa0Presently he said, in a mild voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThy wits seem touched, poor stranger; doubtless thou hast suffered\r\nprivations and rude buffetings at the world\xe2\x80\x99s hands; thy looks and dress\r\nbetoken it. \xc2\xa0Whom dost thou take me to be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTake thee? \xc2\xa0Prithee for whom else than whom thou art? \xc2\xa0I take thee to\r\nbe Hugh Hendon,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miles, sharply.\r\n\r\nThe other continued, in the same soft tone--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd whom dost thou imagine thyself to be?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cImagination hath nought to do with it! \xc2\xa0Dost thou pretend thou knowest\r\nme not for thy brother Miles Hendon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAn expression of pleased surprise flitted across Hugh\xe2\x80\x99s face, and he\r\nexclaimed--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat! thou art not jesting? can the dead come to life? \xc2\xa0God be praised\r\nif it be so! \xc2\xa0Our poor lost boy restored to our arms after all these\r\ncruel years! \xc2\xa0Ah, it seems too good to be true, it _is_ too good to be\r\ntrue--I charge thee, have pity, do not trifle with me! \xc2\xa0Quick--come to\r\nthe light--let me scan thee well!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe seized Miles by the arm, dragged him to the window, and began to\r\ndevour him from head to foot with his eyes, turning him this way and\r\nthat, and stepping briskly around him and about him to prove him\r\nfrom all points of view; whilst the returned prodigal, all aglow with\r\ngladness, smiled, laughed, and kept nodding his head and saying--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGo on, brother, go on, and fear not; thou\xe2\x80\x99lt find nor limb nor feature\r\nthat cannot bide the test. \xc2\xa0Scour and scan me to thy content, my good\r\nold Hugh--I am indeed thy old Miles, thy same old Miles, thy lost\r\nbrother, is\xe2\x80\x99t not so? \xc2\xa0Ah, \xe2\x80\x99tis a great day--I _said_ \xe2\x80\x99twas a great day!\r\n\xc2\xa0Give me thy hand, give me thy cheek--lord, I am like to die of very\r\njoy!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe was about to throw himself upon his brother; but Hugh put up his hand\r\nin dissent, then dropped his chin mournfully upon his breast, saying\r\nwith emotion--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, God of his mercy give me strength to bear this grievous\r\ndisappointment!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiles, amazed, could not speak for a moment; then he found his tongue,\r\nand cried out--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c_What_ disappointment? \xc2\xa0Am I not thy brother?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHugh shook his head sadly, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI pray heaven it may prove so, and that other eyes may find the\r\nresemblances that are hid from mine. \xc2\xa0Alack, I fear me the letter spoke\r\nbut too truly.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat letter?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne that came from over sea, some six or seven years ago. \xc2\xa0It said my\r\nbrother died in battle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt was a lie! \xc2\xa0Call thy father--he will know me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOne may not call the dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDead?\xe2\x80\x9d Miles\xe2\x80\x99s voice was subdued, and his lips trembled.  \xe2\x80\x9cMy father\r\ndead!--oh, this is heavy news. \xc2\xa0Half my new joy is withered now.\r\n\xc2\xa0Prithee let me see my brother Arthur--he will know me; he will know me\r\nand console me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe, also, is dead.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod be merciful to me, a stricken man! \xc2\xa0Gone,--both gone--the worthy\r\ntaken and the worthless spared, in me! \xc2\xa0Ah! I crave your mercy!--do not\r\nsay the Lady Edith--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs dead? \xc2\xa0No, she lives.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen, God be praised, my joy is whole again! \xc2\xa0Speed thee, brother--let\r\nher come to me! \xc2\xa0An\xe2\x80\x99 _she_ say I am not myself--but she will not; no,\r\nno, _she_ will know me, I were a fool to doubt it. Bring her--bring the\r\nold servants; they, too, will know me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAll are gone but five--Peter, Halsey, David, Bernard, and Margaret.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo saying, Hugh left the room. \xc2\xa0Miles stood musing a while, then began\r\nto walk the floor, muttering--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe five arch-villains have survived the two-and-twenty leal and\r\nhonest--\xe2\x80\x98tis an odd thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe continued walking back and forth, muttering to himself; he had\r\nforgotten the King entirely. \xc2\xa0By-and-by his Majesty said gravely, and\r\nwith a touch of genuine compassion, though the words themselves were\r\ncapable of being interpreted ironically--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMind not thy mischance, good man; there be others in the world whose\r\nidentity is denied, and whose claims are derided. \xc2\xa0Thou hast company.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAh, my King,\xe2\x80\x9d cried Hendon, colouring slightly, \xe2\x80\x9cdo not thou condemn\r\nme--wait, and thou shalt see. \xc2\xa0I am no impostor--she will say it; you\r\nshall hear it from the sweetest lips in England. \xc2\xa0I an impostor? \xc2\xa0Why, I\r\nknow this old hall, these pictures of my ancestors, and all these things\r\nthat are about us, as a child knoweth its own nursery. \xc2\xa0Here was I born\r\nand bred, my lord; I speak the truth; I would not deceive thee; and\r\nshould none else believe, I pray thee do not _thou_ doubt me--I could\r\nnot bear it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI do not doubt thee,\xe2\x80\x9d said the King, with a childlike simplicity and\r\nfaith.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI thank thee out of my heart!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Hendon with a fervency which\r\nshowed that he was touched. \xc2\xa0The King added, with the same gentle\r\nsimplicity--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cDost thou doubt _me_?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA guilty confusion seized upon Hendon, and he was grateful that the door\r\nopened to admit Hugh, at that moment, and saved him the necessity of\r\nreplying.\r\n\r\nA beautiful lady, richly clothed, followed Hugh, and after her came\r\nseveral liveried servants. \xc2\xa0The lady walked slowly, with her head bowed\r\nand her eyes fixed upon the floor. \xc2\xa0The face was unspeakably sad. \xc2\xa0Miles\r\nHendon sprang forward, crying out--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, my Edith, my darling--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Hugh waved him back, gravely, and said to the lady--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLook upon him. \xc2\xa0Do you know him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt the sound of Miles\xe2\x80\x99s voice the woman had started slightly, and her\r\ncheeks had flushed; she was trembling now. \xc2\xa0She stood still, during an\r\nimpressive pause of several moments; then slowly lifted up her head and\r\nlooked into Hendon\xe2\x80\x99s eyes with a stony and frightened gaze; the blood\r\nsank out of her face, drop by drop, till nothing remained but the grey\r\npallor of death; then she said, in a voice as dead as the face, \xe2\x80\x9cI know\r\nhim not!\xe2\x80\x9d and turned, with a moan and a stifled sob, and tottered out of\r\nthe room.\r\n\r\nMiles Hendon sank into a chair and covered his face with his hands.\r\nAfter a pause, his brother said to the servants--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou have observed him. \xc2\xa0Do you know him?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey shook their heads; then the master said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe servants know you not, sir. \xc2\xa0I fear there is some mistake. You have\r\nseen that my wife knew you not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThy _wife_!\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0In an instant Hugh was pinned to the wall, with an iron\r\ngrip about his throat.  \xe2\x80\x9cOh, thou fox-hearted slave, I see it all!\r\n\xc2\xa0Thou\xe2\x80\x99st writ the lying letter thyself, and my stolen bride and goods\r\nare its fruit. \xc2\xa0There--now get thee gone, lest I shame mine honourable\r\nsoldiership with the slaying of so pitiful a mannikin!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHugh, red-faced, and almost suffocated, reeled to the nearest chair, and\r\ncommanded the servants to seize and bind the murderous stranger. \xc2\xa0They\r\nhesitated, and one of them said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHe is armed, Sir Hugh, and we are weaponless.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cArmed! \xc2\xa0What of it, and ye so many? \xc2\xa0Upon him, I say!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut Miles warned them to be careful what they did, and added--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYe know me of old--I have not changed; come on, an\xe2\x80\x99 it like you.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis reminder did not hearten the servants much; they still held back.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThen go, ye paltry cowards, and arm yourselves and guard the doors,\r\nwhilst I send one to fetch the watch!\xe2\x80\x9d said Hugh. \xc2\xa0He turned at the\r\nthreshold, and said to Miles, \xe2\x80\x9cYou\xe2\x80\x99ll find it to your advantage to\r\noffend not with useless endeavours at escape.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cEscape? \xc2\xa0Spare thyself discomfort, an\xe2\x80\x99 that is all that troubles thee.\r\nFor Miles Hendon is master of Hendon Hall and all its belongings. \xc2\xa0He\r\nwill remain--doubt it not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVI. Disowned.\r\n\r\nThe King sat musing a few moments, then looked up and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9c\xe2\x80\x98Tis strange--most strange. \xc2\xa0I cannot account for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo, it is not strange, my liege. \xc2\xa0I know him, and this conduct is but\r\nnatural. \xc2\xa0He was a rascal from his birth.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, I spake not of _him_, Sir Miles.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNot of him? \xc2\xa0Then of what? \xc2\xa0What is it that is strange?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThat the King is not missed.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHow? \xc2\xa0Which? \xc2\xa0I doubt I do not understand.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIndeed? \xc2\xa0Doth it not strike you as being passing strange that the land\r\nis not filled with couriers and proclamations describing my person and\r\nmaking search for me? \xc2\xa0Is it no matter for commotion and distress that\r\nthe Head of the State is gone; that I am vanished away and lost?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMost true, my King, I had forgot.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0Then Hendon sighed, and muttered to\r\nhimself, \xe2\x80\x9cPoor ruined mind--still busy with its pathetic dream.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cBut I have a plan that shall right us both--I will write a paper, in\r\nthree tongues--Latin, Greek and English--and thou shalt haste away with\r\nit to London in the morning. \xc2\xa0Give it to none but my uncle, the Lord\r\nHertford; when he shall see it, he will know and say I wrote it. \xc2\xa0Then\r\nhe will send for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMight it not be best, my Prince, that we wait here until I prove myself\r\nand make my rights secure to my domains? \xc2\xa0I should be so much the better\r\nable then to--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King interrupted him imperiously--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cPeace! \xc2\xa0What are thy paltry domains, thy trivial interests, contrasted\r\nwith matters which concern the weal of a nation and the integrity of a\r\nthrone?\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0Then, he added, in a gentle voice, as if he were sorry for his\r\nseverity, \xe2\x80\x9cObey, and have no fear; I will right thee, I will make thee\r\nwhole--yes, more than whole. \xc2\xa0I shall remember, and requite.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo saying, he took the pen, and set himself to work. \xc2\xa0Hendon\r\ncontemplated him lovingly a while, then said to himself--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAn\xe2\x80\x99 it were dark, I should think it _was_ a king that spoke; there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nno denying it, when the humour\xe2\x80\x99s upon on him he doth thunder and lighten\r\nlike your true King; now where got he that trick? \xc2\xa0See him scribble and\r\nscratch away contentedly at his meaningless pot-hooks, fancying them to\r\nbe Latin and Greek--and except my wit shall serve me with a lucky device\r\nfor diverting him from his purpose, I shall be forced to pretend to post\r\naway to-morrow on this wild errand he hath invented for me.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe next moment Sir Miles\xe2\x80\x99s thoughts had gone back to the recent\r\nepisode. So absorbed was he in his musings, that when the King presently\r\nhanded him the paper which he had been writing, he received it and\r\npocketed it without being conscious of the act. \xe2\x80\x9cHow marvellous strange\r\nshe acted,\xe2\x80\x9d he muttered.  \xe2\x80\x9cI think she knew me--and I think she did\r\n_not_ know me. These opinions do conflict, I perceive it plainly; I\r\ncannot reconcile them, neither can I, by argument, dismiss either of the\r\ntwo, or even persuade one to outweigh the other. \xc2\xa0The matter standeth\r\nsimply thus: she _must_ have known my face, my figure, my voice, for how\r\ncould it be otherwise? \xc2\xa0Yet she __said_ _she knew me not, and that is\r\nproof perfect, for she cannot lie. \xc2\xa0But stop--I think I begin to see.\r\nPeradventure he hath influenced her, commanded her, compelled her to\r\nlie. \xc2\xa0That is the solution. \xc2\xa0The riddle is unriddled. \xc2\xa0She seemed dead\r\nwith fear--yes, she was under his compulsion. \xc2\xa0I will seek her; I will\r\nfind her; now that he is away, she will speak her true mind. \xc2\xa0She will\r\nremember the old times when we were little playfellows together, and\r\nthis will soften her heart, and she will no more betray me, but will\r\nconfess me. \xc2\xa0There is no treacherous blood in her--no, she was always\r\nhonest and true. \xc2\xa0She has loved me, in those old days--this is my\r\nsecurity; for whom one has loved, one cannot betray.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe stepped eagerly toward the door; at that moment it opened, and the\r\nLady Edith entered. \xc2\xa0She was very pale, but she walked with a firm step,\r\nand her carriage was full of grace and gentle dignity. Her face was as\r\nsad as before.\r\n\r\nMiles sprang forward, with a happy confidence, to meet her, but she\r\nchecked him with a hardly perceptible gesture, and he stopped where he\r\nwas. \xc2\xa0She seated herself, and asked him to do likewise. Thus simply did\r\nshe take the sense of old comradeship out of him, and transform him\r\ninto a stranger and a guest. \xc2\xa0The surprise of it, the bewildering\r\nunexpectedness of it, made him begin to question, for a moment, if he\r\n_was_ the person he was pretending to be, after all. \xc2\xa0The Lady Edith\r\nsaid--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSir, I have come to warn you. \xc2\xa0The mad cannot be persuaded out of\r\ntheir delusions, perchance; but doubtless they may be persuaded to avoid\r\nperils. \xc2\xa0I think this dream of yours hath the seeming of honest truth to\r\nyou, and therefore is not criminal--but do not tarry here with it; for\r\nhere it is dangerous.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0She looked steadily into Miles\xe2\x80\x99s face a moment,\r\nthen added, impressively, \xe2\x80\x9cIt is the more dangerous for that you _are_\r\nmuch like what our lost lad must have grown to be if he had lived.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHeavens, madam, but I _am_ he!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI truly think you think it, sir. \xc2\xa0I question not your honesty in that;\r\nI but warn you, that is all. \xc2\xa0My husband is master in this region; his\r\npower hath hardly any limit; the people prosper or starve, as he wills.\r\nIf you resembled not the man whom you profess to be, my husband might\r\nbid you pleasure yourself with your dream in peace; but trust me, I know\r\nhim well; I know what he will do; he will say to all that you are but a\r\nmad impostor, and straightway all will echo him.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0She bent upon Miles\r\nthat same steady look once more, and added:  \xe2\x80\x9cIf you _were_ Miles\r\nHendon, and he knew it and all the region knew it--consider what I\r\nam saying, weigh it well--you would stand in the same peril, your\r\npunishment would be no less sure; he would deny you and denounce you,\r\nand none would be bold enough to give you countenance.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMost truly I believe it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Miles, bitterly.  \xe2\x80\x9cThe power that\r\ncan command one life-long friend to betray and disown another, and be\r\nobeyed, may well look to be obeyed in quarters where bread and life are\r\non the stake and no cobweb ties of loyalty and honour are concerned.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA faint tinge appeared for a moment in the lady\xe2\x80\x99s cheek, and she dropped\r\nher eyes to the floor; but her voice betrayed no emotion when she\r\nproceeded--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI have warned you--I must still warn you--to go hence. \xc2\xa0This man will\r\ndestroy you, else. \xc2\xa0He is a tyrant who knows no pity. \xc2\xa0I, who am\r\nhis fettered slave, know this. \xc2\xa0Poor Miles, and Arthur, and my dear\r\nguardian, Sir Richard, are free of him, and at rest: \xc2\xa0better that\r\nyou were with them than that you bide here in the clutches of this\r\nmiscreant. \xc2\xa0Your pretensions are a menace to his title and possessions;\r\nyou have assaulted him in his own house: \xc2\xa0you are ruined if you stay.\r\n\xc2\xa0Go--do not hesitate. If you lack money, take this purse, I beg of you,\r\nand bribe the servants to let you pass. Oh, be warned, poor soul, and\r\nescape while you may.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nMiles declined the purse with a gesture, and rose up and stood before\r\nher.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGrant me one thing,\xe2\x80\x9d he said.  \xe2\x80\x9cLet your eyes rest upon mine, so that I\r\nmay see if they be steady. \xc2\xa0There--now answer me. \xc2\xa0Am I Miles Hendon?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo. \xc2\xa0I know you not.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSwear it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe answer was low, but distinct--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI swear.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, this passes belief!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFly! \xc2\xa0Why will you waste the precious time? \xc2\xa0Fly, and save yourself.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAt that moment the officers burst into the room, and a violent struggle\r\nbegan; but Hendon was soon overpowered and dragged away. The King was\r\ntaken also, and both were bound and led to prison.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVII. In Prison.\r\n\r\nThe cells were all crowded; so the two friends were chained in a large\r\nroom where persons charged with trifling offences were commonly kept.\r\nThey had company, for there were some twenty manacled and fettered\r\nprisoners here, of both sexes and of varying ages,--an obscene and noisy\r\ngang. \xc2\xa0The King chafed bitterly over the stupendous indignity thus put\r\nupon his royalty, but Hendon was moody and taciturn. \xc2\xa0He was pretty\r\nthoroughly bewildered; he had come home, a jubilant prodigal, expecting\r\nto find everybody wild with joy over his return; and instead had got the\r\ncold shoulder and a jail. \xc2\xa0The promise and the fulfilment differed so\r\nwidely that the effect was stunning; he could not decide whether it\r\nwas most tragic or most grotesque. \xc2\xa0He felt much as a man might who had\r\ndanced blithely out to enjoy a rainbow, and got struck by lightning.\r\n\r\nBut gradually his confused and tormenting thoughts settled down into\r\nsome sort of order, and then his mind centred itself upon Edith. \xc2\xa0He\r\nturned her conduct over, and examined it in all lights, but he could not\r\nmake anything satisfactory out of it. \xc2\xa0Did she know him--or didn\xe2\x80\x99t she\r\nknow him? \xc2\xa0It was a perplexing puzzle, and occupied him a long time; but\r\nhe ended, finally, with the conviction that she did know him, and had\r\nrepudiated him for interested reasons. \xc2\xa0He wanted to load her name with\r\ncurses now; but this name had so long been sacred to him that he found\r\nhe could not bring his tongue to profane it.\r\n\r\nWrapped in prison blankets of a soiled and tattered condition, Hendon\r\nand the King passed a troubled night. \xc2\xa0For a bribe the jailer had\r\nfurnished liquor to some of the prisoners; singing of ribald songs,\r\nfighting, shouting, and carousing was the natural consequence. \xc2\xa0At last,\r\na while after midnight, a man attacked a woman and nearly killed her by\r\nbeating her over the head with his manacles before the jailer could\r\ncome to the rescue. \xc2\xa0The jailer restored peace by giving the man a sound\r\nclubbing about the head and shoulders--then the carousing ceased;\r\nand after that, all had an opportunity to sleep who did not mind the\r\nannoyance of the moanings and groanings of the two wounded people.\r\n\r\nDuring the ensuing week, the days and nights were of a monotonous\r\nsameness as to events; men whose faces Hendon remembered more or less\r\ndistinctly, came, by day, to gaze at the \xe2\x80\x98impostor\xe2\x80\x99 and repudiate\r\nand insult him; and by night the carousing and brawling went on with\r\nsymmetrical regularity. \xc2\xa0However, there was a change of incident at\r\nlast. The jailer brought in an old man, and said to him--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe villain is in this room--cast thy old eyes about and see if thou\r\ncanst say which is he.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHendon glanced up, and experienced a pleasant sensation for the first\r\ntime since he had been in the jail. \xc2\xa0He said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cThis is Blake\r\nAndrews, a servant all his life in my father\xe2\x80\x99s family--a good honest\r\nsoul, with a right heart in his breast. That is, formerly. \xc2\xa0But none are\r\ntrue now; all are liars. \xc2\xa0This man will know me--and will deny me, too,\r\nlike the rest.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old man gazed around the room, glanced at each face in turn, and\r\nfinally said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI see none here but paltry knaves, scum o\xe2\x80\x99 the streets. \xc2\xa0Which is he?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe jailer laughed.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHere,\xe2\x80\x9d he said; \xe2\x80\x9cscan this big animal, and grant me an opinion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old man approached, and looked Hendon over, long and earnestly, then\r\nshook his head and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarry, _this_ is no Hendon--nor ever was!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cRight! \xc2\xa0Thy old eyes are sound yet. \xc2\xa0An\xe2\x80\x99 I were Sir Hugh, I would take\r\nthe shabby carle and--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe jailer finished by lifting himself a-tip-toe with an imaginary\r\nhalter, at the same time making a gurgling noise in his throat\r\nsuggestive of suffocation. \xc2\xa0The old man said, vindictively--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLet him bless God an\xe2\x80\x99 he fare no worse. \xc2\xa0An\xe2\x80\x99 _I_ had the handling o\xe2\x80\x99\r\nthe villain he should roast, or I am no true man!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe jailer laughed a pleasant hyena laugh, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive him a piece of thy mind, old man--they all do it. \xc2\xa0Thou\xe2\x80\x99lt find it\r\ngood diversion.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThen he sauntered toward his ante-room and disappeared. \xc2\xa0The old man\r\ndropped upon his knees and whispered--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGod be thanked, thou\xe2\x80\x99rt come again, my master! \xc2\xa0I believed thou wert\r\ndead these seven years, and lo, here thou art alive! \xc2\xa0I knew thee the\r\nmoment I saw thee; and main hard work it was to keep a stony countenance\r\nand seem to see none here but tuppenny knaves and rubbish o\xe2\x80\x99 the\r\nstreets. I am old and poor, Sir Miles; but say the word and I will go\r\nforth and proclaim the truth though I be strangled for it.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNo,\xe2\x80\x9d said Hendon; \xe2\x80\x9cthou shalt not. \xc2\xa0It would ruin thee, and yet help\r\nbut little in my cause. \xc2\xa0But I thank thee, for thou hast given me back\r\nsomewhat of my lost faith in my kind.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe old servant became very valuable to Hendon and the King; for\r\nhe dropped in several times a day to \xe2\x80\x98abuse\xe2\x80\x99 the former, and always\r\nsmuggled in a few delicacies to help out the prison bill of fare; he\r\nalso furnished the current news. \xc2\xa0Hendon reserved the dainties for the\r\nKing; without them his Majesty might not have survived, for he was\r\nnot able to eat the coarse and wretched food provided by the jailer.\r\n\xc2\xa0Andrews was obliged to confine himself to brief visits, in order to\r\navoid suspicion; but he managed to impart a fair degree of information\r\neach time--information delivered in a low voice, for Hendon\xe2\x80\x99s benefit,\r\nand interlarded with insulting epithets delivered in a louder voice for\r\nthe benefit of other hearers.\r\n\r\nSo, little by little, the story of the family came out. \xc2\xa0Arthur had\r\nbeen dead six years. \xc2\xa0This loss, with the absence of news from Hendon,\r\nimpaired the father\xe2\x80\x99s health; he believed he was going to die, and he\r\nwished to see Hugh and Edith settled in life before he passed away; but\r\nEdith begged hard for delay, hoping for Miles\xe2\x80\x99s return; then the letter\r\ncame which brought the news of Miles\xe2\x80\x99s death; the shock prostrated Sir\r\nRichard; he believed his end was very near, and he and Hugh insisted\r\nupon the marriage; Edith begged for and obtained a month\xe2\x80\x99s respite,\r\nthen another, and finally a third; the marriage then took place by\r\nthe death-bed of Sir Richard. \xc2\xa0It had not proved a happy one. \xc2\xa0It was\r\nwhispered about the country that shortly after the nuptials the bride\r\nfound among her husband\xe2\x80\x99s papers several rough and incomplete drafts of\r\nthe fatal letter, and had accused him of precipitating the marriage--and\r\nSir Richard\xe2\x80\x99s death, too--by a wicked forgery. Tales of cruelty to the\r\nLady Edith and the servants were to be heard on all hands; and since the\r\nfather\xe2\x80\x99s death Sir Hugh had thrown off all soft disguises and become\r\na pitiless master toward all who in any way depended upon him and his\r\ndomains for bread.\r\n\r\nThere was a bit of Andrew\xe2\x80\x99s gossip which the King listened to with a\r\nlively interest--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThere is rumour that the King is mad. \xc2\xa0But in charity forbear to say\r\n_I_ mentioned it, for \xe2\x80\x99tis death to speak of it, they say.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis Majesty glared at the old man and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe King is _not_ mad, good man--and thou\xe2\x80\x99lt find it to thy advantage\r\nto busy thyself with matters that nearer concern thee than this\r\nseditious prattle.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat doth the lad mean?\xe2\x80\x9d said Andrews, surprised at this brisk assault\r\nfrom such an unexpected quarter. \xc2\xa0Hendon gave him a sign, and he did not\r\npursue his question, but went on with his budget--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThe late King is to be buried at Windsor in a day or two--the 16th of\r\nthe month--and the new King will be crowned at Westminster the 20th.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMethinks they must needs find him first,\xe2\x80\x9d muttered his Majesty; then\r\nadded, confidently, \xe2\x80\x9cbut they will look to that--and so also shall I.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIn the name of--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nBut the old man got no further--a warning sign from Hendon checked his\r\nremark. \xc2\xa0He resumed the thread of his gossip--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSir Hugh goeth to the coronation--and with grand hopes. \xc2\xa0He confidently\r\nlooketh to come back a peer, for he is high in favour with the Lord\r\nProtector.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat Lord Protector?\xe2\x80\x9d asked his Majesty.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis Grace the Duke of Somerset.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat Duke of Somerset?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarry, there is but one--Seymour, Earl of Hertford.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe King asked sharply--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince when is _he_ a duke, and Lord Protector?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cSince the last day of January.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd prithee who made him so?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHimself and the Great Council--with help of the King.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHis Majesty started violently.  \xe2\x80\x9cThe _King_!\xe2\x80\x9d he cried.  \xe2\x80\x9c_What_ king,\r\ngood sir?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWhat king, indeed! (God-a-mercy, what aileth the boy?) \xc2\xa0Sith we have\r\nbut one, \xe2\x80\x99tis not difficult to answer--his most sacred Majesty King\r\nEdward the Sixth--whom God preserve! \xc2\xa0Yea, and a dear and gracious\r\nlittle urchin is he, too; and whether he be mad or no--and they say he\r\nmendeth daily--his praises are on all men\xe2\x80\x99s lips; and all bless him,\r\nlikewise, and offer prayers that he may be spared to reign long in\r\nEngland; for he began humanely with saving the old Duke of Norfolk\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nlife, and now is he bent on destroying the cruellest of the laws that\r\nharry and oppress the people.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThis news struck his Majesty dumb with amazement, and plunged him into\r\nso deep and dismal a reverie that he heard no more of the old man\xe2\x80\x99s\r\ngossip. He wondered if the \xe2\x80\x98little urchin\xe2\x80\x99 was the beggar-boy whom\r\nhe left dressed in his own garments in the palace. \xc2\xa0It did not seem\r\npossible that this could be, for surely his manners and speech would\r\nbetray him if he pretended to be the Prince of Wales--then he would be\r\ndriven out, and search made for the true prince. \xc2\xa0Could it be that the\r\nCourt had set up some sprig of the nobility in his place? \xc2\xa0No, for his\r\nuncle would not allow that--he was all-powerful and could and would\r\ncrush such a movement, of course. \xc2\xa0The boy\xe2\x80\x99s musings profited him\r\nnothing; the more he tried to unriddle the mystery the more perplexed he\r\nbecame, the more his head ached, and the worse he slept. \xc2\xa0His\r\nimpatience to get to London grew hourly, and his captivity became almost\r\nunendurable.\r\n\r\nHendon\xe2\x80\x99s arts all failed with the King--he could not be comforted; but a\r\ncouple of women who were chained near him succeeded better. Under their\r\ngentle ministrations he found peace and learned a degree of patience.\r\n\xc2\xa0He was very grateful, and came to love them dearly and to delight in\r\nthe sweet and soothing influence of their presence. \xc2\xa0He asked them why\r\nthey were in prison, and when they said they were Baptists, he smiled,\r\nand inquired--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIs that a crime to be shut up for in a prison? \xc2\xa0Now I grieve, for I\r\nshall lose ye--they will not keep ye long for such a little thing.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey did not answer; and something in their faces made him uneasy. He\r\nsaid, eagerly--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cYou do not speak; be good to me, and tell me--there will be no other\r\npunishment? \xc2\xa0Prithee tell me there is no fear of that.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThey tried to change the topic, but his fears were aroused, and he\r\npursued it--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cWill they scourge thee? \xc2\xa0No, no, they would not be so cruel! \xc2\xa0Say they\r\nwould not. \xc2\xa0Come, they _will_ not, will they?\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe women betrayed confusion and distress, but there was no avoiding an\r\nanswer, so one of them said, in a voice choked with emotion--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, thou\xe2\x80\x99lt break our hearts, thou gentle spirit!--God will help us to\r\nbear our--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cIt is a confession!\xe2\x80\x9d the King broke in.  \xe2\x80\x9cThen they _will_ scourge\r\nthee, the stony-hearted wretches! \xc2\xa0But oh, thou must not weep, I cannot\r\nbear it. \xc2\xa0Keep up thy courage--I shall come to my own in time to save\r\nthee from this bitter thing, and I will do it!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nWhen the King awoke in the morning, the women were gone.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThey are saved!\xe2\x80\x9d he said, joyfully; then added, despondently, \xe2\x80\x9cbut woe\r\nis me!--for they were my comforters.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nEach of them had left a shred of ribbon pinned to his clothing, in token\r\nof remembrance. \xc2\xa0He said he would keep these things always; and that\r\nsoon he would seek out these dear good friends of his and take them\r\nunder his protection.\r\n\r\nJust then the jailer came in with some subordinates, and commanded that\r\nthe prisoners be conducted to the jail-yard. \xc2\xa0The King was overjoyed--it\r\nwould be a blessed thing to see the blue sky and breathe the fresh air\r\nonce more. \xc2\xa0He fretted and chafed at the slowness of the officers, but\r\nhis turn came at last, and he was released from his staple and ordered\r\nto follow the other prisoners with Hendon.\r\n\r\nThe court or quadrangle was stone-paved, and open to the sky. \xc2\xa0The\r\nprisoners entered it through a massive archway of masonry, and were\r\nplaced in file, standing, with their backs against the wall. A rope\r\nwas stretched in front of them, and they were also guarded by their\r\nofficers. It was a chill and lowering morning, and a light snow which\r\nhad fallen during the night whitened the great empty space and added\r\nto the general dismalness of its aspect. Now and then a wintry wind\r\nshivered through the place and sent the snow eddying hither and thither.\r\n\r\nIn the centre of the court stood two women, chained to posts. \xc2\xa0A glance\r\nshowed the King that these were his good friends. \xc2\xa0He shuddered, and\r\nsaid to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cAlack, they are not gone free, as I had thought. \xc2\xa0To\r\nthink that such as these should know the lash!--in England! \xc2\xa0Ay, there\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nthe shame of it--not in Heathennesse, Christian England! \xc2\xa0They will be\r\nscourged; and I, whom they have comforted and kindly entreated, must\r\nlook on and see the great wrong done; it is strange, so strange, that\r\nI, the very source of power in this broad realm, am helpless to protect\r\nthem. But let these miscreants look well to themselves, for there is a\r\nday coming when I will require of them a heavy reckoning for this work.\r\n\xc2\xa0For every blow they strike now, they shall feel a hundred then.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nA great gate swung open, and a crowd of citizens poured in. \xc2\xa0They\r\nflocked around the two women, and hid them from the King\xe2\x80\x99s view. A\r\nclergyman entered and passed through the crowd, and he also was hidden.\r\n\xc2\xa0The King now heard talking, back and forth, as if questions were being\r\nasked and answered, but he could not make out what was said. \xc2\xa0Next there\r\nwas a deal of bustle and preparation, and much passing and repassing of\r\nofficials through that part of the crowd that stood on the further side\r\nof the women; and whilst this proceeded a deep hush gradually fell upon\r\nthe people.\r\n\r\nNow, by command, the masses parted and fell aside, and the King saw a\r\nspectacle that froze the marrow in his bones. \xc2\xa0Faggots had been piled\r\nabout the two women, and a kneeling man was lighting them!\r\n\r\nThe women bowed their heads, and covered their faces with their hands;\r\nthe yellow flames began to climb upward among the snapping and crackling\r\nfaggots, and wreaths of blue smoke to stream away on the wind; the\r\nclergyman lifted his hands and began a prayer--just then two young girls\r\ncame flying through the great gate, uttering piercing screams, and threw\r\nthemselves upon the women at the stake. \xc2\xa0Instantly they were torn away\r\nby the officers, and one of them was kept in a tight grip, but the other\r\nbroke loose, saying she would die with her mother; and before she could\r\nbe stopped she had flung her arms about her mother\xe2\x80\x99s neck again. \xc2\xa0She\r\nwas torn away once more, and with her gown on fire. \xc2\xa0Two or three men\r\nheld her, and the burning portion of her gown was snatched off and\r\nthrown flaming aside, she struggling all the while to free herself, and\r\nsaying she would be alone in the world, now; and begging to be allowed\r\nto die with her mother. \xc2\xa0Both the girls screamed continually, and fought\r\nfor freedom; but suddenly this tumult was drowned under a volley of\r\nheart-piercing shrieks of mortal agony--the King glanced from the\r\nfrantic girls to the stake, then turned away and leaned his ashen face\r\nagainst the wall, and looked no more. \xc2\xa0He said, \xe2\x80\x9cThat which I have seen,\r\nin that one little moment, will never go out from my memory, but will\r\nabide there; and I shall see it all the days, and dream of it all the\r\nnights, till I die. \xc2\xa0Would God I had been blind!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHendon was watching the King. \xc2\xa0He said to himself, with satisfaction,\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHis disorder mendeth; he hath changed, and groweth gentler. \xc2\xa0If he had\r\nfollowed his wont, he would have stormed at these varlets, and said he\r\nwas King, and commanded that the women be turned loose unscathed. \xc2\xa0Soon\r\nhis delusion will pass away and be forgotten, and his poor mind will be\r\nwhole again. \xc2\xa0God speed the day!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThat same day several prisoners were brought in to remain over night,\r\nwho were being conveyed, under guard, to various places in the kingdom,\r\nto undergo punishment for crimes committed. \xc2\xa0The King conversed with\r\nthese--he had made it a point, from the beginning, to instruct himself\r\nfor the kingly office by questioning prisoners whenever the opportunity\r\noffered--and the tale of their woes wrung his heart. \xc2\xa0One of them was\r\na poor half-witted woman who had stolen a yard or two of cloth from a\r\nweaver--she was to be hanged for it. \xc2\xa0Another was a man who had been\r\naccused of stealing a horse; he said the proof had failed, and he had\r\nimagined that he was safe from the halter; but no--he was hardly free\r\nbefore he was arraigned for killing a deer in the King\xe2\x80\x99s park; this was\r\nproved against him, and now he was on his way to the gallows. \xc2\xa0There was\r\na tradesman\xe2\x80\x99s apprentice whose case particularly distressed the King;\r\nthis youth said he found a hawk, one evening, that had escaped from its\r\nowner, and he took it home with him, imagining himself entitled to it;\r\nbut the court convicted him of stealing it, and sentenced him to death.\r\n\r\nThe King was furious over these inhumanities, and wanted Hendon to break\r\njail and fly with him to Westminster, so that he could mount his throne\r\nand hold out his sceptre in mercy over these unfortunate people and\r\nsave their lives.  \xe2\x80\x9cPoor child,\xe2\x80\x9d sighed Hendon, \xe2\x80\x9cthese woeful tales\r\nhave brought his malady upon him again; alack, but for this evil hap, he\r\nwould have been well in a little time.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nAmong these prisoners was an old lawyer--a man with a strong face and a\r\ndauntless mien. \xc2\xa0Three years past, he had written a pamphlet against the\r\nLord Chancellor, accusing him of injustice, and had been punished for\r\nit by the loss of his ears in the pillory, and degradation from the\r\nbar, and in addition had been fined 3,000 pounds and sentenced to\r\nimprisonment for life. \xc2\xa0Lately he had repeated his offence; and in\r\nconsequence was now under sentence to lose _what remained of his ears_,\r\npay a fine of 5,000 pounds, be branded on both cheeks, and remain in\r\nprison for life.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cThese be honourable scars,\xe2\x80\x9d he said, and turned back his grey hair and\r\nshowed the mutilated stubs of what had once been his ears.\r\n\r\nThe King\xe2\x80\x99s eye burned with passion. \xc2\xa0He said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cNone believe in me--neither wilt thou. \xc2\xa0But no matter--within the\r\ncompass of a month thou shalt be free; and more, the laws that have\r\ndishonoured thee, and shamed the English name, shall be swept from the\r\nstatute books. \xc2\xa0The world is made wrong; kings should go to school to\r\ntheir own laws, at times, and so learn mercy.\xe2\x80\x9d {1}\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXVIII. The sacrifice.\r\n\r\nMeantime Miles was growing sufficiently tired of confinement and\r\ninaction. \xc2\xa0But now his trial came on, to his great gratification, and\r\nhe thought he could welcome any sentence provided a further imprisonment\r\nshould not be a part of it. \xc2\xa0But he was mistaken about that. \xc2\xa0He was in\r\na fine fury when he found himself described as a \xe2\x80\x98sturdy vagabond\xe2\x80\x99 and\r\nsentenced to sit two hours in the stocks for bearing that character\r\nand for assaulting the master of Hendon Hall. \xc2\xa0His pretensions as to\r\nbrothership with his prosecutor, and rightful heirship to the Hendon\r\nhonours and estates, were left contemptuously unnoticed, as being not\r\neven worth examination.\r\n\r\nHe raged and threatened on his way to punishment, but it did no good; he\r\nwas snatched roughly along by the officers, and got an occasional cuff,\r\nbesides, for his irreverent conduct.\r\n\r\nThe King could not pierce through the rabble that swarmed behind; so\r\nhe was obliged to follow in the rear, remote from his good friend and\r\nservant. \xc2\xa0The King had been nearly condemned to the stocks himself for\r\nbeing in such bad company, but had been let off with a lecture and a\r\nwarning, in consideration of his youth. \xc2\xa0When the crowd at last halted,\r\nhe flitted feverishly from point to point around its outer rim, hunting\r\na place to get through; and at last, after a deal of difficulty and\r\ndelay, succeeded. \xc2\xa0There sat his poor henchman in the degrading stocks,\r\nthe sport and butt of a dirty mob--he, the body servant of the King\r\nof England! \xc2\xa0Edward had heard the sentence pronounced, but he had not\r\nrealised the half that it meant. \xc2\xa0His anger began to rise as the sense\r\nof this new indignity which had been put upon him sank home; it jumped\r\nto summer heat, the next moment, when he saw an egg sail through the air\r\nand crush itself against Hendon\xe2\x80\x99s cheek, and heard the crowd roar\r\nits enjoyment of the episode. \xc2\xa0He sprang across the open circle and\r\nconfronted the officer in charge, crying--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cFor shame! \xc2\xa0This is my servant--set him free! \xc2\xa0I am the--\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cOh, peace!\xe2\x80\x9d exclaimed Hendon, in a panic, \xe2\x80\x9cthou\xe2\x80\x99lt destroy thyself.\r\nMind him not, officer, he is mad.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cGive thyself no trouble as to the matter of minding him, good man, I\r\nhave small mind to mind him; but as to teaching him somewhat, to that\r\nI am well inclined.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0He turned to a subordinate and said, \xe2\x80\x9cGive the\r\nlittle fool a taste or two of the lash, to mend his manners.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cHalf a dozen will better serve his turn,\xe2\x80\x9d suggested Sir Hugh, who had\r\nridden up, a moment before, to take a passing glance at the proceedings.\r\n\r\nThe King was seized. \xc2\xa0He did not even struggle, so paralysed was he\r\nwith the mere thought of the monstrous outrage that was proposed to be\r\ninflicted upon his sacred person. \xc2\xa0History was already defiled with\r\nthe record of the scourging of an English king with whips--it was an\r\nintolerable reflection that he must furnish a duplicate of that shameful\r\npage. \xc2\xa0He was in the toils, there was no help for him; he must either\r\ntake this punishment or beg for its remission. \xc2\xa0Hard conditions; he\r\nwould take the stripes--a king might do that, but a king could not beg.\r\n\r\nBut meantime, Miles Hendon was resolving the difficulty.  \xe2\x80\x9cLet the child\r\ngo,\xe2\x80\x9d said he; \xe2\x80\x9cye heartless dogs, do ye not see how young and frail he\r\nis? \xc2\xa0Let him go--I will take his lashes.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMarry, a good thought--and thanks for it,\xe2\x80\x9d said Sir Hugh, his face\r\nlighting with a sardonic satisfaction.  \xe2\x80\x9cLet the little beggar go, and\r\ngive this fellow a dozen in his place--an honest dozen, well laid on.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n The King was in the act of entering a fierce protest, but Sir Hugh\r\nsilenced him with the potent remark, \xe2\x80\x9cYes, speak up, do, and free thy\r\nmind--only, mark ye, that for each word you utter he shall get six\r\nstrokes the more.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHendon was removed from the stocks, and his back laid bare; and whilst\r\nthe lash was applied the poor little King turned away his face and\r\nallowed unroyal tears to channel his cheeks unchecked. \xe2\x80\x9cAh, brave good\r\nheart,\xe2\x80\x9d he said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cthis loyal deed shall never perish out of\r\nmy memory. \xc2\xa0I will not forget it--and neither shall _they_!\xe2\x80\x9d he added,\r\nwith passion. \xc2\xa0Whilst he mused, his appreciation of Hendon\xe2\x80\x99s magnanimous\r\nconduct grew to greater and still greater dimensions in his mind, and\r\nso also did his gratefulness for it. \xc2\xa0Presently he said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cWho\r\nsaves his prince from wounds and possible death--and this he did for\r\nme--performs high service; but it is little--it is nothing--oh, less\r\nthan nothing!--when \xe2\x80\x99tis weighed against the act of him who saves his\r\nprince from _shame_!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHendon made no outcry under the scourge, but bore the heavy blows with\r\nsoldierly fortitude. \xc2\xa0This, together with his redeeming the boy by\r\ntaking his stripes for him, compelled the respect of even that forlorn\r\nand degraded mob that was gathered there; and its gibes and hootings\r\ndied away, and no sound remained but the sound of the falling blows.\r\n\xc2\xa0The stillness that pervaded the place, when Hendon found himself once\r\nmore in the stocks, was in strong contrast with the insulting clamour\r\nwhich had prevailed there so little a while before. \xc2\xa0The King came\r\nsoftly to Hendon\xe2\x80\x99s side, and whispered in his ear--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cKings cannot ennoble thee, thou good, great soul, for One who is higher\r\nthan kings hath done that for thee; but a king can confirm thy nobility\r\nto men.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0He picked up the scourge from the ground, touched Hendon\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nbleeding shoulders lightly with it, and whispered, \xe2\x80\x9cEdward of England\r\ndubs thee Earl!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHendon was touched. \xc2\xa0The water welled to his eyes, yet at the same time\r\nthe grisly humour of the situation and circumstances so undermined his\r\ngravity that it was all he could do to keep some sign of his inward\r\nmirth from showing outside. \xc2\xa0To be suddenly hoisted, naked and gory,\r\nfrom the common stocks to the Alpine altitude and splendour of\r\nan Earldom, seemed to him the last possibility in the line of the\r\ngrotesque. \xc2\xa0He said to himself, \xe2\x80\x9cNow am I finely tinselled, indeed!\r\n\xc2\xa0The spectre-knight of the Kingdom of Dreams and Shadows is become a\r\nspectre-earl--a dizzy flight for a callow wing! \xc2\xa0An\xe2\x80\x99 this go on, I\r\nshall presently be hung like a very maypole with fantastic gauds and\r\nmake-believe honours. \xc2\xa0But I shall value them, all valueless as\r\nthey are, for the love that doth bestow them. Better these poor mock\r\ndignities of mine, that come unasked, from a clean hand and a right\r\nspirit, than real ones bought by servility from grudging and interested\r\npower.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe dreaded Sir Hugh wheeled his horse about, and as he spurred away,\r\nthe living wall divided silently to let him pass, and as silently closed\r\ntogether again. \xc2\xa0And so remained; nobody went so far as to venture\r\na remark in favour of the prisoner, or in compliment to him; but no\r\nmatter--the absence of abuse was a sufficient homage in itself. \xc2\xa0A\r\nlate comer who was not posted as to the present circumstances, and who\r\ndelivered a sneer at the \xe2\x80\x98impostor,\xe2\x80\x99 and was in the act of following it\r\nwith a dead cat, was promptly knocked down and kicked out, without any\r\nwords, and then the deep quiet resumed sway once more.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXIX. To London.\r\n\r\nWhen Hendon\xe2\x80\x99s term of service in the stocks was finished, he was\r\nreleased and ordered to quit the region and come back no more. His sword\r\nwas restored to him, and also his mule and his donkey. He mounted\r\nand rode off, followed by the King, the crowd opening with quiet\r\nrespectfulness to let them pass, and then dispersing when they were\r\ngone.\r\n\r\nHendon was soon absorbed in thought. \xc2\xa0There were questions of high\r\nimport to be answered. \xc2\xa0What should he do? \xc2\xa0Whither should he go?\r\nPowerful help must be found somewhere, or he must relinquish his\r\ninheritance and remain under the imputation of being an impostor\r\nbesides. \xc2\xa0Where could he hope to find this powerful help? \xc2\xa0Where,\r\nindeed! \xc2\xa0It was a knotty question. By-and-by a thought occurred to him\r\nwhich pointed to a possibility--the slenderest of slender possibilities,\r\ncertainly, but still worth considering, for lack of any other that\r\npromised anything at all. \xc2\xa0He remembered what old Andrews had said about\r\nthe young King\xe2\x80\x99s goodness and his generous championship of the wronged\r\nand unfortunate. \xc2\xa0Why not go and try to get speech of him and beg for\r\njustice? \xc2\xa0Ah, yes, but could so fantastic a pauper get admission to the\r\naugust presence of a monarch? Never mind--let that matter take care of\r\nitself; it was a bridge that would not need to be crossed till he should\r\ncome to it. \xc2\xa0He was an old campaigner, and used to inventing shifts and\r\nexpedients: \xc2\xa0no doubt he would be able to find a way. \xc2\xa0Yes, he would\r\nstrike for the capital. Maybe his father\xe2\x80\x99s old friend Sir Humphrey\r\nMarlow would help him--\xe2\x80\x98good old Sir Humphrey, Head Lieutenant of the\r\nlate King\xe2\x80\x99s kitchen, or stables, or something\xe2\x80\x99--Miles could not remember\r\njust what or which. \xc2\xa0Now that he had something to turn his energies to,\r\na distinctly defined object to accomplish, the fog of humiliation and\r\ndepression which had settled down upon his spirits lifted and blew away,\r\nand he raised his head and looked about him. \xc2\xa0He was surprised to see\r\nhow far he had come; the village was away behind him. \xc2\xa0The King was\r\njogging along in his wake, with his head bowed; for he, too, was deep\r\nin plans and thinkings. \xc2\xa0A sorrowful misgiving clouded Hendon\xe2\x80\x99s new-born\r\ncheerfulness: \xc2\xa0would the boy be willing to go again to a city where,\r\nduring all his brief life, he had never known anything but ill-usage and\r\npinching want? \xc2\xa0But the question must be asked; it could not be avoided;\r\nso Hendon reined up, and called out--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cI had forgotten to inquire whither we are bound. \xc2\xa0Thy commands, my\r\nliege!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cTo London!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHendon moved on again, mightily contented with the answer--but astounded\r\nat it too.\r\n\r\nThe whole journey was made without an adventure of importance. But it\r\nended with one. \xc2\xa0About ten o\xe2\x80\x99clock on the night of the 19th of February\r\nthey stepped upon London Bridge, in the midst of a writhing, struggling\r\njam of howling and hurrahing people, whose beer-jolly faces stood out\r\nstrongly in the glare from manifold torches--and at that instant the\r\ndecaying head of some former duke or other grandee tumbled down between\r\nthem, striking Hendon on the elbow and then bounding off among the\r\nhurrying confusion of feet. So evanescent and unstable are men\xe2\x80\x99s works\r\nin this world!--the late good King is but three weeks dead and three\r\ndays in his grave, and already the adornments which he took such pains\r\nto select from prominent people for his noble bridge are falling. \xc2\xa0A\r\ncitizen stumbled over that head, and drove his own head into the back of\r\nsomebody in front of him, who turned and knocked down the first person\r\nthat came handy, and was promptly laid out himself by that person\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nfriend. \xc2\xa0It was the right ripe time for a free fight, for the\r\nfestivities of the morrow--Coronation Day--were already beginning;\r\neverybody was full of strong drink and patriotism; within five minutes\r\nthe free fight was occupying a good deal of ground; within ten or twelve\r\nit covered an acre of so, and was become a riot. \xc2\xa0By this time Hendon\r\nand the King were hopelessly separated from each other and lost in the\r\nrush and turmoil of the roaring masses of humanity. \xc2\xa0And so we leave\r\nthem.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXX. Tom\xe2\x80\x99s progress.\r\n\r\nWhilst the true King wandered about the land poorly clad, poorly\r\nfed, cuffed and derided by tramps one while, herding with thieves\r\nand murderers in a jail another, and called idiot and impostor by\r\nall impartially, the mock King Tom Canty enjoyed quite a different\r\nexperience.\r\n\r\nWhen we saw him last, royalty was just beginning to have a bright side\r\nfor him. \xc2\xa0This bright side went on brightening more and more every\r\nday: in a very little while it was become almost all sunshine and\r\ndelightfulness. \xc2\xa0He lost his fears; his misgivings faded out and died;\r\nhis embarrassments departed, and gave place to an easy and confident\r\nbearing. \xc2\xa0He worked the whipping-boy mine to ever-increasing profit.\r\n\r\nHe ordered my Lady Elizabeth and my Lady Jane Grey into his presence\r\nwhen he wanted to play or talk, and dismissed them when he was done with\r\nthem, with the air of one familiarly accustomed to such performances.\r\n\xc2\xa0It no longer confused him to have these lofty personages kiss his hand\r\nat parting.\r\n\r\nHe came to enjoy being conducted to bed in state at night, and dressed\r\nwith intricate and solemn ceremony in the morning. \xc2\xa0It came to be a\r\nproud pleasure to march to dinner attended by a glittering procession\r\nof officers of state and gentlemen-at-arms; insomuch, indeed, that he\r\ndoubled his guard of gentlemen-at-arms, and made them a hundred. \xc2\xa0He\r\nliked to hear the bugles sounding down the long corridors, and the\r\ndistant voices responding, \xe2\x80\x9cWay for the King!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe even learned to enjoy sitting in throned state in council, and\r\nseeming to be something more than the Lord Protector\xe2\x80\x99s mouthpiece. He\r\nliked to receive great ambassadors and their gorgeous trains, and listen\r\nto the affectionate messages they brought from illustrious monarchs who\r\ncalled him brother. \xc2\xa0O happy Tom Canty, late of Offal Court!\r\n\r\nHe enjoyed his splendid clothes, and ordered more: \xc2\xa0he found his four\r\nhundred servants too few for his proper grandeur, and trebled them. \xc2\xa0The\r\nadulation of salaaming courtiers came to be sweet music to his ears. \xc2\xa0He\r\nremained kind and gentle, and a sturdy and determined champion of all\r\nthat were oppressed, and he made tireless war upon unjust laws: \xc2\xa0yet\r\nupon occasion, being offended, he could turn upon an earl, or even a\r\nduke, and give him a look that would make him tremble. \xc2\xa0Once, when his\r\nroyal \xe2\x80\x98sister,\xe2\x80\x99 the grimly holy Lady Mary, set herself to reason with\r\nhim against the wisdom of his course in pardoning so many people who\r\nwould otherwise be jailed, or hanged, or burned, and reminded him that\r\ntheir august late father\xe2\x80\x99s prisons had sometimes contained as high as\r\nsixty thousand convicts at one time, and that during his admirable reign\r\nhe had delivered seventy-two thousand thieves and robbers over to death\r\nby the executioner, {9} the boy was filled with generous indignation,\r\nand commanded her to go to her closet, and beseech God to take away the\r\nstone that was in her breast, and give her a human heart.\r\n\r\nDid Tom Canty never feel troubled about the poor little rightful prince\r\nwho had treated him so kindly, and flown out with such hot zeal to\r\navenge him upon the insolent sentinel at the palace-gate? Yes; his first\r\nroyal days and nights were pretty well sprinkled with painful thoughts\r\nabout the lost prince, and with sincere longings for his return, and\r\nhappy restoration to his native rights and splendours. \xc2\xa0But as time\r\nwore on, and the prince did not come, Tom\xe2\x80\x99s mind became more and more\r\noccupied with his new and enchanting experiences, and by little and\r\nlittle the vanished monarch faded almost out of his thoughts; and\r\nfinally, when he did intrude upon them at intervals, he was become an\r\nunwelcome spectre, for he made Tom feel guilty and ashamed.\r\n\r\nTom\xe2\x80\x99s poor mother and sisters travelled the same road out of his mind.\r\nAt first he pined for them, sorrowed for them, longed to see them, but\r\nlater, the thought of their coming some day in their rags and dirt, and\r\nbetraying him with their kisses, and pulling him down from his lofty\r\nplace, and dragging him back to penury and degradation and the slums,\r\nmade him shudder. \xc2\xa0At last they ceased to trouble his thoughts almost\r\nwholly. \xc2\xa0And he was content, even glad: \xc2\xa0for, whenever their mournful\r\nand accusing faces did rise before him now, they made him feel more\r\ndespicable than the worms that crawl.\r\n\r\nAt midnight of the 19th of February, Tom Canty was sinking to sleep in\r\nhis rich bed in the palace, guarded by his loyal vassals, and surrounded\r\nby the pomps of royalty, a happy boy; for tomorrow was the day appointed\r\nfor his solemn crowning as King of England. At that same hour, Edward,\r\nthe true king, hungry and thirsty, soiled and draggled, worn with\r\ntravel, and clothed in rags and shreds--his share of the results of the\r\nriot--was wedged in among a crowd of people who were watching with deep\r\ninterest certain hurrying gangs of workmen who streamed in and out of\r\nWestminster Abbey, busy as ants: \xc2\xa0they were making the last preparation\r\nfor the royal coronation.\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXI. The Recognition procession.\r\n\r\nWhen Tom Canty awoke the next morning, the air was heavy with a\r\nthunderous murmur: \xc2\xa0all the distances were charged with it. \xc2\xa0It was\r\nmusic to him; for it meant that the English world was out in its\r\nstrength to give loyal welcome to the great day.\r\n\r\nPresently Tom found himself once more the chief figure in a wonderful\r\nfloating pageant on the Thames; for by ancient custom the \xe2\x80\x98recognition\r\nprocession\xe2\x80\x99 through London must start from the Tower, and he was bound\r\nthither.\r\n\r\nWhen he arrived there, the sides of the venerable fortress seemed\r\nsuddenly rent in a thousand places, and from every rent leaped a\r\nred tongue of flame and a white gush of smoke; a deafening explosion\r\nfollowed, which drowned the shoutings of the multitude, and made the\r\nground tremble; the flame-jets, the smoke, and the explosions, were\r\nrepeated over and over again with marvellous celerity, so that in a few\r\nmoments the old Tower disappeared in the vast fog of its own smoke, all\r\nbut the very top of the tall pile called the White Tower; this, with\r\nits banners, stood out above the dense bank of vapour as a mountain-peak\r\nprojects above a cloud-rack.\r\n\r\nTom Canty, splendidly arrayed, mounted a prancing war-steed, whose rich\r\ntrappings almost reached to the ground; his \xe2\x80\x98uncle,\xe2\x80\x99 the Lord Protector\r\nSomerset, similarly mounted, took place in his rear; the King\xe2\x80\x99s Guard\r\nformed in single ranks on either side, clad in burnished armour;\r\nafter the Protector followed a seemingly interminable procession of\r\nresplendent nobles attended by their vassals; after these came the lord\r\nmayor and the aldermanic body, in crimson velvet robes, and with their\r\ngold chains across their breasts; and after these the officers and\r\nmembers of all the guilds of London, in rich raiment, and bearing the\r\nshowy banners of the several corporations. \xc2\xa0Also in the procession, as a\r\nspecial guard of honour through the city, was the Ancient and Honourable\r\nArtillery Company--an organisation already three hundred years old\r\nat that time, and the only military body in England possessing the\r\nprivilege (which it still possesses in our day) of holding itself\r\nindependent of the commands of Parliament. \xc2\xa0It was a brilliant\r\nspectacle, and was hailed with acclamations all along the line, as it\r\ntook its stately way through the packed multitudes of citizens. The\r\nchronicler says, \xe2\x80\x98The King, as he entered the city, was received by the\r\npeople with prayers, welcomings, cries, and tender words, and all signs\r\nwhich argue an earnest love of subjects toward their sovereign; and the\r\nKing, by holding up his glad countenance to such as stood afar off, and\r\nmost tender language to those that stood nigh his Grace, showed himself\r\nno less thankful to receive the people\xe2\x80\x99s goodwill than they to offer it.\r\n\xc2\xa0To all that wished him well, he gave thanks. \xc2\xa0To such as bade \xe2\x80\x9cGod save\r\nhis Grace,\xe2\x80\x9d he said in return, \xe2\x80\x9cGod save you all!\xe2\x80\x9d and added that \xe2\x80\x9che\r\nthanked them with all his heart.\xe2\x80\x9d Wonderfully transported were the\r\npeople with the loving answers and gestures of their King.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nIn Fenchurch Street a \xe2\x80\x98fair child, in costly apparel,\xe2\x80\x99 stood on a stage\r\nto welcome his Majesty to the city. \xc2\xa0The last verse of his greeting was\r\nin these words--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x98Welcome, O King! as much as hearts can think; Welcome, again, as much\r\nas tongue can tell,--Welcome to joyous tongues, and hearts that will\r\nnot shrink: God thee preserve, we pray, and wish thee ever well.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThe people burst forth in a glad shout, repeating with one voice what\r\nthe child had said. \xc2\xa0Tom Canty gazed abroad over the surging sea of\r\neager faces, and his heart swelled with exultation; and he felt that\r\nthe one thing worth living for in this world was to be a king, and a\r\nnation\xe2\x80\x99s idol. \xc2\xa0Presently he caught sight, at a distance, of a couple\r\nof his ragged Offal Court comrades--one of them the lord high admiral in\r\nhis late mimic court, the other the first lord of the bedchamber in the\r\nsame pretentious fiction; and his pride swelled higher than ever. \xc2\xa0Oh,\r\nif they could only recognise him now! \xc2\xa0What unspeakable glory it would\r\nbe, if they could recognise him, and realise that the derided mock king\r\nof the slums and back alleys was become a real King, with illustrious\r\ndukes and princes for his humble menials, and the English world at his\r\nfeet! \xc2\xa0But he had to deny himself, and choke down his desire, for such\r\na recognition might cost more than it would come to: \xc2\xa0so he turned away\r\nhis head, and left the two soiled lads to go on with their shoutings and\r\nglad adulations, unsuspicious of whom it was they were lavishing them\r\nupon.\r\n\r\nEvery now and then rose the cry, \xe2\x80\x9cA largess! a largess!\xe2\x80\x9d and Tom\r\nresponded by scattering a handful of bright new coins abroad for the\r\nmultitude to scramble for.\r\n\r\nThe chronicler says, \xe2\x80\x98At the upper end of Gracechurch Street, before the\r\nsign of the Eagle, the city had erected a gorgeous arch, beneath which\r\nwas a stage, which stretched from one side of the street to the other.\r\nThis was an historical pageant, representing the King\xe2\x80\x99s immediate\r\nprogenitors. \xc2\xa0There sat Elizabeth of York in the midst of an immense\r\nwhite rose, whose petals formed elaborate furbelows around her; by her\r\nside was Henry VII., issuing out of a vast red rose, disposed in the\r\nsame manner: \xc2\xa0the hands of the royal pair were locked together, and the\r\nwedding-ring ostentatiously displayed. \xc2\xa0From the red and white roses\r\nproceeded a stem, which reached up to a second stage, occupied by Henry\r\nVIII., issuing from a red and white rose, with the effigy of the new\r\nKing\xe2\x80\x99s mother, Jane Seymour, represented by his side. \xc2\xa0One branch sprang\r\nfrom this pair, which mounted to a third stage, where sat the effigy of\r\nEdward VI. himself, enthroned in royal majesty; and the whole pageant\r\nwas framed with wreaths of roses, red and white.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\nThis quaint and gaudy spectacle so wrought upon the rejoicing people,\r\nthat their acclamations utterly smothered the small voice of the child\r\nwhose business it was to explain the thing in eulogistic rhymes. \xc2\xa0But\r\nTom Canty was not sorry; for this loyal uproar was sweeter music to him\r\nthan any poetry, no matter what its quality might be. \xc2\xa0Whithersoever Tom\r\nturned his happy young face, the people recognised the exactness of his\r\neffigy\xe2\x80\x99s likeness to himself, the flesh and blood counterpart; and new\r\nwhirlwinds of applause burst forth.\r\n\r\nThe great pageant moved on, and still on, under one triumphal arch after\r\nanother, and past a bewildering succession of spectacular and symbolical\r\ntableaux, each of which typified and exalted some virtue, or talent, or\r\nmerit, of the little King\xe2\x80\x99s.  \xe2\x80\x98Throughout the whole of Cheapside, from\r\nevery penthouse and window, hung banners and streamers; and the richest\r\ncarpets, stuffs, and cloth-of-gold tapestried the streets--specimens\r\nof the great wealth of the stores within; and the splendour of this\r\nthoroughfare was equalled in the other streets, and in some even\r\nsurpassed.\xe2\x80\x99\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cAnd all these wonders and these marvels are to welcome me--me!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n murmured Tom Canty.\r\n\r\nThe mock King\xe2\x80\x99s cheeks were flushed with excitement, his eyes were\r\nflashing, his senses swam in a delirium of pleasure. \xc2\xa0At this point,\r\njust as he was raising his hand to fling another rich largess, he caught\r\nsight of a pale, astounded face, which was strained forward out of\r\nthe second rank of the crowd, its intense eyes riveted upon him. \xc2\xa0A\r\nsickening consternation struck through him; he recognised his\r\nmother! and up flew his hand, palm outward, before his eyes--that old\r\ninvoluntary gesture, born of a forgotten episode, and perpetuated by\r\nhabit. \xc2\xa0In an instant more she had torn her way out of the press, and\r\npast the guards, and was at his side. \xc2\xa0She embraced his leg, she covered\r\nit with kisses, she cried, \xe2\x80\x9cO my child, my darling!\xe2\x80\x9d lifting toward him\r\na face that was transfigured with joy and love. \xc2\xa0The same instant an\r\nofficer of the King\xe2\x80\x99s Guard snatched her away with a curse, and sent\r\nher reeling back whence she came with a vigorous impulse from his\r\nstrong arm. \xc2\xa0The words \xe2\x80\x9cI do not know you, woman!\xe2\x80\x9d were falling from Tom\r\nCanty\xe2\x80\x99s lips when this piteous thing occurred; but it smote him to the\r\nheart to see her treated so; and as she turned for a last glimpse of\r\nhim, whilst the crowd was swallowing her from his sight, she seemed so\r\nwounded, so broken-hearted, that a shame fell upon him which consumed\r\nhis pride to ashes, and withered his stolen royalty. \xc2\xa0His grandeurs were\r\nstricken valueless: they seemed to fall away from him like rotten rags.\r\n\r\nThe procession moved on, and still on, through ever augmenting\r\nsplendours and ever augmenting tempests of welcome; but to Tom Canty\r\nthey were as if they had not been. \xc2\xa0He neither saw nor heard. \xc2\xa0Royalty\r\nhad lost its grace and sweetness; its pomps were become a reproach.\r\n\xc2\xa0Remorse was eating his heart out. \xc2\xa0He said, \xe2\x80\x9cWould God I were free of\r\nmy captivity!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nHe had unconsciously dropped back into the phraseology of the first days\r\nof his compulsory greatness.\r\n\r\nThe shining pageant still went winding like a radiant and interminable\r\nserpent down the crooked lanes of the quaint old city, and through the\r\nhuzzaing hosts; but still the King rode with bowed head and vacant eyes,\r\nseeing only his mother\xe2\x80\x99s face and that wounded look in it.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLargess, largess!\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0The cry fell upon an unheeding ear.\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cLong live Edward of England!\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0It seemed as if the earth shook with the\r\nexplosion; but there was no response from the King. \xc2\xa0He heard it only as\r\none hears the thunder of the surf when it is blown to the ear out of a\r\ngreat distance, for it was smothered under another sound which was still\r\nnearer, in his own breast, in his accusing conscience--a voice which\r\nkept repeating those shameful words, \xe2\x80\x9cI do not know you, woman!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe words smote upon the King\xe2\x80\x99s soul as the strokes of a funeral bell\r\nsmite upon the soul of a surviving friend when they remind him of secret\r\ntreacheries suffered at his hands by him that is gone.\r\n\r\nNew glories were unfolded at every turning; new wonders, new marvels,\r\nsprang into view; the pent clamours of waiting batteries were released;\r\nnew raptures poured from the throats of the waiting multitudes: \xc2\xa0but the\r\nKing gave no sign, and the accusing voice that went moaning through his\r\ncomfortless breast was all the sound he heard.\r\n\r\nBy-and-by the gladness in the faces of the populace changed a little,\r\nand became touched with a something like solicitude or anxiety: \xc2\xa0an\r\nabatement in the volume of the applause was observable too. \xc2\xa0The Lord\r\nProtector was quick to notice these things: \xc2\xa0he was as quick to detect\r\nthe cause. \xc2\xa0He spurred to the King\xe2\x80\x99s side, bent low in his saddle,\r\nuncovered, and said--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy liege, it is an ill time for dreaming. \xc2\xa0The people observe thy\r\ndowncast head, thy clouded mien, and they take it for an omen. \xc2\xa0Be\r\nadvised: \xc2\xa0unveil the sun of royalty, and let it shine upon these boding\r\nvapours, and disperse them. \xc2\xa0Lift up thy face, and smile upon the\r\npeople.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nSo saying, the Duke scattered a handful of coins to right and left, then\r\nretired to his place. \xc2\xa0The mock King did mechanically as he had been\r\nbidden. \xc2\xa0His smile had no heart in it, but few eyes were near enough\r\nor sharp enough to detect that. \xc2\xa0The noddings of his plumed head as he\r\nsaluted his subjects were full of grace and graciousness; the largess\r\nwhich he delivered from his hand was royally liberal: \xc2\xa0so the people\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nanxiety vanished, and the acclamations burst forth again in as mighty a\r\nvolume as before.\r\n\r\nStill once more, a little before the progress was ended, the Duke was\r\nobliged to ride forward, and make remonstrance. \xc2\xa0He whispered--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cO dread sovereign! shake off these fatal humours; the eyes of the world\r\nare upon thee.\xe2\x80\x9d \xc2\xa0Then he added with sharp annoyance, \xe2\x80\x9cPerdition catch\r\nthat crazy pauper! \xe2\x80\x99twas she that hath disturbed your Highness.\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\nThe gorgeous figure turned a lustreless eye upon the Duke, and said in a\r\ndead voice--\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cShe was my mother!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\xe2\x80\x9cMy God!\xe2\x80\x9d groaned the Protector as he reined his horse backward to his\r\npost, \xe2\x80\x9cthe omen was pregnant with prophecy. \xc2\xa0He is gone mad again!\xe2\x80\x9d\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\n\r\nCHAPTER XXXII. Coronation Day.\r\n\r\nLet us go backward a few hours, and place ourselves in Westminster\r\nAbbey, at four o\xe2\x80\x99clock in the morning of this memorable Coronation Day.\r\n\xc2\xa0We are not without company; for although it is still night, we find\r\nthe torch-lighted galleries already filling up with people who are well\r\ncontent to sit still and wait seven or eight hours till the time shall\r\ncome for them to see what they may not hope to see twice in their\r\nlives--the coronation of a King. \xc2\xa0Yes, London and Westminster have been\r\nastir ever since the warning guns boomed at three o\xe2\x80\x99clock, and already\r\ncrowds of untitled rich folk who have bought the privilege of trying\r\nto find sitting-room in the galleries are flocking in at the entrances\r\nreserved for their sort.\r\n\r\nThe hours drag along tediously enough. \xc2\xa0All stir has ceased for some\r\ntime, for every gallery has long ago been packed. \xc2\xa0We may sit, now, and\r\nlook and think at our leisure. \xc2\xa0We have glimpses, here and there\r\nand yonder, through the dim cathedral twilight, of portions of many\r\ngalleries and balconies, wedged full with other people, the other\r\nportions of these galleries and balconies being cut off from sight by\r\nintervening pillars and architectural projections. \xc2\xa0We have in view\r\nthe whole of the great north transept--empty, and waiting for England\xe2\x80\x99s\r\nprivileged ones. \xc2\xa0We see also the ample area or platform, carpeted with\r\nrich stuffs, whereon the throne stands. \xc2\xa0The throne occupies the centre\r\nof the platform, and is raised above it upon an elevation of four steps.\r\nWithin the seat of the throne is enclosed a rough flat rock--the stone\r\nof Scone--which many generations of Scottish kings sat on to be crowned,\r\nand so it in time became holy enough to answer a like purpose for\r\nEnglish monarchs. \xc2\xa0Both the throne and its footstool are covered with\r\ncloth of gold.\r\n\r\nStillness reigns, the torches blink dully, the time drags heavily.\r\nBut at last the lagging daylight asserts itself, the torches are\r\nextinguished, and a mellow radiance suffuses the great spaces. All\r\nfeatures of the noble building are distinct now, but soft and dreamy,\r\nfor the sun is lightly veiled with clouds.\r\n\r\nAt seven o\xe2\x80\x99clock the first break in the drowsy monotony occurs; for on\r\nthe stroke of this hour the first peeress enters the transept, clothed\r\nlike Solomon for splendour, and is conducted to her appointed place\r\nby an official clad in satins and velvets, whilst a duplicate of him\r\ngathers up the lady\xe2\x80\x99s long train, follows after, and, when the lady is\r\nseated, arranges the train across her lap for her. \xc2\xa0He then places her\r\nfootstool according to her desire, after which he puts her coronet where\r\nit will be convenient to her hand when the time for the simultaneous\r\ncoroneting of the nobles shall arrive.\r\n\r\nBy this time the peeresses are flowing in in a glittering stream, and\r\nthe satin-clad officials are flitting and glinting everywhere, seating\r\nthem and making them comfortable. \xc2\xa0The scene is animated enough now.\r\n\xc2\xa0There is stir and life, and shifting colour everywhere. \xc2\xa0After a time,\r\nquiet reigns again; for the peeresses are all come and are all in their\r\nplaces, a solid acre or such a matter, of human flowers, resplendent in\r\nvariegated colours, and frosted like a Milky Way with diamonds. \xc2\xa0There\r\nare all ages here: brown, wrinkled, white-haired dowagers who are able\r\nto go back, and still back, down the stream of time, and recall the\r\ncrowning of Richard III. and the troublous days of that old forgotten\r\nage; and there are handsome middle-aged dames; and lovely and gracious\r\nyoung matrons; and gentle and beautiful young girls, with beaming eyes\r\nand fresh complexions, who may possibly put on their jewelled coronets\r\nawkwardly when the great time comes; for the matter will be new to\r\nthem, and their excitement will be a sore hindrance. Still, this may\r\nnot happen, for the hair of all these ladies has been arranged with a\r\nspecial view to the swift and successful lodging of the crown in its\r\nplace when the signal comes.\r\n\r\nWe have seen that this massed array of peeresses is sown thick with\r\ndiamonds, and we also see that it is a marvellous spectacle--but now we\r\nare about to be astonished in earnest. \xc2\xa0About nine, the clouds suddenly\r\nbreak away and a shaft of sunshine cleaves the mellow atmosphere, and\r\ndrifts slowly along the ranks of ladies; and every rank it touches\r\nflames into a dazzling splendour of many-coloured fires, and we tingle\r\nto our finger-tips with the electric thrill that is shot through us by\r\nthe surprise and the beauty of the spectacle! \xc2\xa0Presently a special envoy\r\nfrom some distant corner of the Orient, marching with the general body\r\nof foreign ambassadors, crosses this bar of sunshine, and we catch our\r\nbreath, the glory that streams and flashes and palpitates about him is\r\nso overpowering; for he is crusted from head to heel with gems, and his\r\nslightest movement showers a dancing radiance all around him.\r\n\r\nLet us change the tense for convenience. \xc2\xa0The time drifted along--one\r\nhour--two hours--two hours and a half; then the deep booming of\r\nartillery told that the King and his grand procession had arrived at\r\nlast; so the waiting multitude rejoiced. \xc2\xa0All knew that a further delay\r\nmust follow, for the King must be prepared and robed for the solemn\r\nceremony; but this delay would be pleasantly occupied by the assembling\r\nof the peers of the realm in their stately robes. \xc2\xa0These were conducted\r\nceremoniously to their seats, and their coronets placed conveniently\r\nat hand; and meanwhile the multitude in the galleries were alive with\r\ninterest, for most of them were beholding for the first time, dukes,\r\nearls, and barons, whose names had been historical for five hundred\r\nyears. \xc2\xa0When all were finally seated, the spectacle from the galleries\r\nand all coigns of vantage was complete;
